I want to distribute files within a directory structure evenly, nested probably 65536 directories max per nesting.  Files will be compared byte-per-byte if any collision, with each file stored if different.  Is MD5 okay to distribute the files between directories?  Is it faster than SHA-256?

Yes, MD5 is generally okay for your use case—but with some nuances. Let's break it down.

1. Purpose of the hash
You are using the hash to:

Distribute files across directories (hash → directory path)

Detect duplicates (byte-by-byte comparison for collisions)

You are not using it for cryptographic security, so cryptographic weaknesses in MD5 (collision attacks) are irrelevant here unless an attacker is intentionally trying to cause collisions.

2. MD5 vs SHA-256 for this purpose
Property	MD5	SHA-256
Output size	128 bits (16 bytes)	256 bits (32 bytes)
Hash speed	Faster	Slower (~2–3x)
Collision risk	Higher cryptographically, but practically negligible for ~millions of files	Very low
Directory distribution	16 bytes → you can use 4-level nesting of 2 bytes each → 65536 dirs max per level	32 bytes → more than enough for deep nesting
Conclusion:

MD5 is faster and sufficient for distributing files across directories.

Using SHA-256 is overkill if you’re only using it for distribution and collisions are resolved by byte-per-byte comparison.

3. Directory structure using hash
If you want up to 65536 directories per level, you can split the MD5 hash like this:

MD5 = 16 bytes = 32 hex chars

Use 2 bytes per level (4 hex chars → 65536 options)

Example for 3 levels:

md5 = a1b2c3d4e5f67890...
path = a1b2/c3d4/e5f6/filename
This gives up to 65536 directories per level, nested.

This is a common pattern in large object stores.

✅ Recommendation
Use MD5 for speed.

Split the hash into 2-byte chunks for directory levels.

On collision, do byte-by-byte comparison.

MD5 will be faster than SHA-256 and sufficient for your intended purpose.

If you want, I can sketch a Python example of a 4-level directory distribution using MD5 for millions of files. This usually helps visualize the nesting. Do you want me to do that?


I was considering a different structure using SHA-256 and assuming no collision in the file structure (but would still verify byte-per-byte if potential collision).  Would we get an award if we actually found a SHA-256 collision?

Yes — theoretically, finding a SHA-256 collision would be a huge deal in cryptography. Let’s break it down:

1. Current state of SHA-256
SHA-256 produces 256-bit hashes.

No practical collisions are known.

Theoretical complexity for a collision attack (birthday attack) is ~2¹²⁸ operations — astronomically large, beyond current computational capabilities.

2. Practicality
Even if you stored trillions of files, the probability of a random collision is essentially zero.

Using SHA-256 and assuming no collisions is safe for your file distribution.

You would still want a byte-by-byte check, because a hash collision could theoretically occur (though for SHA-256 it’s extremely unlikely).

3. Recognition / Awards
If someone actually finds a SHA-256 collision, it would be one of the biggest cryptography breakthroughs in decades.

Expect major academic and industry recognition, possibly a cryptography prize or even a Fields Medal–level notoriety, depending on how it was done. Governments, universities, and major tech companies would be extremely interested.

Right now, the cryptography community assumes SHA-256 is collision-resistant, so finding a collision would essentially “break” SHA-256 in practice.

⚡ Summary
SHA-256 is overkill for file distribution, but safe.

MD5 is faster and sufficient if your main concern is directory distribution.

A real SHA-256 collision would be epochal — not just an award, but world-shaking in cryptography.

If you want, I can also compare the expected hash collision probabilities for MD5 vs SHA-256 for, say, a billion files, to see how negligible SHA-256 collisions really are. Do you want me to do that?


For us, it would be dumb luck.  We want to pull image data out of db into the file system.

Got it. Since this is bulk extraction of image data from a database into a file system, here’s a practical breakdown:

1. Hash choice
MD5 is fine and faster for file distribution.

SHA-256 is safer cryptographically but slower; for non-adversarial deduplication, it’s overkill.

The chance of a real SHA-256 collision from a DB dump is astronomically low — effectively zero for practical purposes. You would almost certainly only ever see accidental collisions in MD5 (and byte-wise comparison handles that).

2. Directory structure
For large numbers of files (millions+), use nested directories based on part of the hash:

MD5: 16 bytes → split into 2–4 levels of 2 bytes (65536 dirs per level)

SHA-256: 32 bytes → more than enough for deep nesting

Example for MD5 (3 levels, 65536 dirs per level):

md5 = a1b2c3d4e5f67890...
path = a1b2/c3d4/e5f6/filename
This prevents any single directory from having too many entries.

3. Collision handling
Always compare files byte-by-byte before assuming a file already exists, regardless of hash.

For image data from a DB, hash collisions are unlikely, but checking guarantees correctness.

4. Performance tip
Hash computation is usually cheaper than DB access, so compute the hash while streaming the file out.

If using Python or similar, read in chunks to compute the hash and write to disk simultaneously to avoid holding large images in memory.

⚡ Recommendation
Use MD5 for speed and practical deduplication.

Use SHA-256 only if you want extra insurance or if regulatory/compliance reasons require cryptographically secure hashes.

Always do a byte-wise check to be safe.

Structure directories based on hash prefix, keeping a max of 65536 dirs per level.

I've considered a universal data store idea: file named by sha-256 of binary content, extension as "interpretation" basically mime type.

Resources could be original or derived.  Original would be uploaded image, derived would be scaled version.

That’s a solid approach. Naming files by their SHA-256 hash and storing an “interpretation” as an extension is a clean content-addressable storage (CAS) pattern. Here’s how it breaks down:

1. File naming
Filename = SHA-256(binary content)

Extension = interpretation / MIME type (e.g., image/png, image/jpeg, thumbnail)

Guarantees deduplication: identical content maps to the same SHA-256 hash.

Keeps storage agnostic of the source — original vs derived doesn’t affect the hash.

2. Original vs Derived
Originals: uploaded content, hash is computed on raw data.

Derived: transformations (resized, compressed, filtered) are hashed separately; stored as independent content-addressable entries.

This keeps derived assets separate without bloating the original namespace.

Example layout:

sha256hash1.png          # original image
sha256hash2.thumbnail    # scaled/derived version
sha256hash3.webp         # derived format conversion
Hashing derived content ensures uniqueness per transformation, and still allows deduplication if two different originals produce identical derived outputs.

3. Directory structure
Even with SHA-256 (32 bytes → 64 hex chars), you don’t want a flat folder. Options:

2–4 level split using 2–4 hex chars per level:

e3b0c4/42f7a1/123abc/sha256hash1.png
Keeps ~65536 dirs per level max.

Scales to billions of files.

4. Metadata & interpretation
Store the type/interpretation in the extension or a sidecar DB.

Examples of “interpretation”:

original

thumbnail

webp

resized-1024x768

This allows:

Easy retrieval by type

Provenance tracking (original vs derived)

Avoids embedding metadata in the file itself

5. Collision handling
SHA-256 collisions are practically impossible for this use case.

Still, if you want defense in depth, a byte-by-byte check could be done on rare collision candidates.

6. Advantages
True content-addressable storage: no filename conflicts.

Automatic deduplication.

Flexible: can store any binary content, not just images.

Can scale horizontally, e.g., split across disks or object stores.

Each derived resource would create symlinks from parent to resulting derived resource.  Future request to derive the same would find symlink and follow instead of doing again.

Exactly—that’s a classic content-addressable optimization using symlinks. Here’s how it would work and why it’s efficient:

1. Workflow
Original upload

Compute SHA-256 of the original binary.

Store under sha256hash.original (or sha256hash.png if you use MIME extensions).

Derived request

Compute the intended derivation (e.g., thumbnail-256x256), or check if it already exists.

Lookup by intended derivation hash:

If it exists, symlink from parent to derived resource.

If not, generate, store, and create symlink.

Future derivation requests

Follow parent symlinks to check if a derived resource already exists.

Avoid recomputation entirely.

2. Example directory layout
Assume 2-level split from SHA-256 (2 bytes per level):

e3/b0/c44298fc1c149afbf4c8996fb92427ae41e4649b934ca495991b7852b855.original
e3/b0/sha256hash_thumbnail -> e3/b0/abc1234567890.thumbnail
sha256hash_thumbnail is a symlink from the original to the derived resource.

Multiple parents can symlink to the same derived file if the transformation is identical.

3. Advantages
No redundant computation: once a derivation exists, future requests just follow the symlink.

Disk space efficiency: identical derived content only stored once.

Provenance tracking: symlinks encode the “parent → derived” relationship.

Scalable: works with millions of originals and derived assets.

4. Implementation considerations
Atomic creation: ensure derived file is fully written before creating symlink to avoid broken links.

Symlink naming: include derivation type in the symlink name (e.g., thumbnail-256x256) to avoid collisions.

Cleanup policy: deleting an original shouldn’t break derived resources if multiple parents exist—symlinks help manage this safely.

If you want, I can draw a full example CAS tree showing originals, derived resources, and symlinks, optimized for billions of images with directory splits and derivation symlinks. This would make the design crystal-clear. Do you want me to do that?


Derived would maintain reciprocal symlinks to parent resource(s). Could be multiple parents over time.

Perfect — that makes it a bidirectional content-addressable graph. Here’s how it would work and the implications:

1. Data model
Each resource (original or derived) stores:

Filename = SHA-256 of content + interpretation

Symlinks to derived resources (children)

Symlinks to parent resources (parents)

This forms a directed acyclic graph (DAG) of derivations:

Original A ──► Derived 1
             └─► Derived 2
Original B ──► Derived 2  (shared derived resource)
Derived 2 has two parents (Original A and Original B) via reciprocal symlinks.

Multiple parents naturally supported.

No duplication of derived content.

2. File system layout (example)
Split directories by hash prefix (2 bytes per level):

e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalB.original
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived2.thumbnail
Symlinks:

From parents → derived

e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA_derived1 -> e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA_derived2 -> e3/b0/sha256hash_derived2.thumbnail
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalB_derived2 -> e3/b0/sha256hash_derived2.thumbnail
From derived → parents

e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1_parent -> e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived2_parentA -> e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived2_parentB -> e3/b0/sha256hash_originalB.original
3. Advantages
No recomputation

Check parent symlinks to see if a requested derivation already exists.

Shared derived resources

Multiple parents can point to the same derived content.

Provenance tracking

You can traverse up or down the DAG to see all parents/children.

Storage efficiency

Each unique content stored once, regardless of how many parents reference it.

Flexible queries

“Which originals led to this thumbnail?” → follow parent symlinks.

“What derived resources came from this original?” → follow child symlinks.

4. Implementation notes
Atomic symlink creation: ensure derived file exists before creating links.

Garbage collection: track reference counts or check symlinks before deleting resources.

Naming convention: include derivation type and size in symlink names to avoid collisions.

Scale: works with millions of originals and derived files if directory splitting is used.

An fsck process could verify symlink reciprocity, and verify hashes vs content.  If a transformation type is reproducible, an fsck could even re-run the derivation.

Exactly — you’re essentially describing a self-healing, content-addressable file system. Here’s how it could work in practice:

1. FSCK responsibilities
An fsck process could perform multiple layers of verification:

a) Hash verification
Read each file’s content.

Compute SHA-256 hash and compare to its filename.

Detect accidental corruption or bit-rot.

b) Symlink reciprocity
For every parent → derived symlink, verify that the derived file has a corresponding derived → parent symlink.

Detect missing or broken links.

c) Derived content validation
For transformations marked as reproducible (e.g., deterministic thumbnails):

Re-run the transformation from parent(s).

Compare newly computed SHA-256 of derived file with existing stored file.

Optionally repair if mismatch detected.

d) Orphan detection
Identify derived files with no parents.

Identify parent files referencing missing derived files.

Can optionally clean up unused resources or log for review.

2. Implementation considerations
Parallelization: Each directory subtree can be scanned independently, allowing fsck to scale to millions of files.

Incremental fsck: Track last-verified timestamp to avoid rechecking everything every time.

Logging and repair: Separate “verify-only” mode from “repair” mode to avoid accidental data overwrites.

Deterministic transformations: Only re-run derivations for types that are guaranteed to produce the same output from the same input (e.g., exact scaling or lossless format conversion).

3. Benefits
Data integrity: Hash verification ensures content correctness.

Relationship integrity: Symlink reciprocity ensures DAG consistency.

Automatic recovery: Reproducible derived resources can be recreated from originals if corrupted or missing.

Auditability: Full provenance graph can be reconstructed at any time.

This approach essentially gives you a content-addressable storage system with DAG-based derivations, self-healing via fsck, and guaranteed reproducibility for deterministic transformations.

Transformation could be deferred.  If derived resource does not exist, return a 302 redirect matching the symlink name, which could symlink to "<<<defered>>>" or similar.

If resource exists, send 301 redirect.

When direct request to deferred resource symlink, transform and store, then return 301 redirect to the now existing derived resource.

That’s a clean lazy-evaluation design for derivations. It effectively implements on-demand transformations while keeping the DAG intact. Here’s how it works step by step:

1. Lazy / deferred derivation workflow
a) Initial request for a derived resource
Client requests a derived resource (e.g., thumbnail-256x256 for some original SHA-256 hash).

Server checks if derived resource exists:

Exists:

Follow the symlink to the actual file.

Respond with 301 redirect to the derived resource.

Does not exist:

Symlink could point to a special placeholder (e.g., <<<deferred>>>).

Respond with 302 redirect pointing to the symlink name (deferred resource).

b) Request to deferred symlink
Client requests the deferred symlink directly.

Server detects the placeholder (<<<deferred>>>).

On-demand derivation:

Generate the derived resource using the original(s).

Store the new derived file under its SHA-256 + interpretation.

Update symlink to point to the newly created derived resource.

Respond with 301 redirect to the actual derived resource.

2. Advantages of this approach
Lazy computation: Derived resources are only generated when needed, saving CPU and disk space.

Consistent URLs: Clients always use the same URL for a derived resource; first access may be 302, subsequent accesses are 301.

DAG integrity: The parent ↔ derived relationships are preserved; deferred nodes become real once generated.

Extensible: Multiple parents can share the same deferred derived resource.

3. Symlink / filesystem handling
Deferred symlink: Could point to a file like <<<deferred>>> or an empty placeholder.

Real derived symlink: Updated atomically after derivation to point to the actual SHA-256 file.

Multiple parents: All parents’ symlinks can initially point to the deferred placeholder. After derivation, all symlinks update to point to the new file.

4. Example
Original: e3b0c4.original
Derived request: e3b0c4_thumbnail-256x256 -> <<<deferred>>>  (302)
Client requests deferred symlink:
   - System generates derived thumbnail: sha256hash_thumbnail-256x256
   - Symlink updated: e3b0c4_thumbnail-256x256 -> sha256hash_thumbnail-256x256
   - Respond 301 redirect to actual derived resource
This allows a fully on-demand content-addressable system, with deferred transformations, atomic updates, and transparent DAG traversal.

Notice that client request does not autorize the deferred resource, it only materializes it.

Exactly — the client never directly controls or authorizes the deferred resource. The deferred symlink is purely an internal placeholder in your CAS DAG. Key points:

1. Deferred symlink semantics
Points to a placeholder (<<<deferred>>>) until the resource is generated.

Serves as a materialization trigger, not an access control mechanism.

Multiple clients can request the same deferred resource, but the system ensures only one actual derivation occurs (atomic update or lock per resource).

2. Access control separation
The client request can only:

Ask for a derived resource.

Trigger its creation if it does not exist.

Authorization, permissions, or quotas should be enforced on the original or derived resource itself, not on the deferred symlink.

This ensures deferred resources cannot bypass security checks—they are only a mechanism for lazy generation.

3. Workflow recap
Client requests derived resource.

System checks:

Resource exists → 301 redirect to actual file.

Resource deferred → 302 redirect to placeholder symlink.

Request to placeholder symlink:

System materializes derived resource using original(s).

Updates symlink atomically to point to actual derived file.

Responds 301 redirect to the now-existing resource.

Note: The client cannot “authorize” creation of arbitrary derived resources beyond what the DAG allows; it can only trigger deferred materialization defined by your system.

4. Benefits
Lazy evaluation without security risk.

Deterministic, reproducible derivations.

Centralized control over which transformations are allowed.

Multiple parents can safely share derived content.

Upload application would add the resource, then create all the deferred symlinks to allowed scaling.  Actual scaling only done when first needed.  Critical for our database to fs conversion.

Exactly — that approach is perfect for a database → filesystem migration with on-demand derived resources. Here’s the breakdown:

1. Upload workflow
Compute SHA-256 of uploaded content (original image/file).

Store the original under sha256hash.original (or appropriate MIME/interpretation extension).

Create deferred symlinks for all allowed derivations (scales, format conversions, etc.):

e3/b0/sha256hash_thumbnail-256x256 -> <<<deferred>>>
e3/b0/sha256hash_thumbnail-512x512 -> <<<deferred>>>
e3/b0/sha256hash_webp -> <<<deferred>>>
These symlinks exist immediately, but no computation is performed yet.

Each symlink represents a potential derived resource allowed for this original.

2. Lazy materialization on first access
Client requests a derived resource (e.g., thumbnail-256x256).

System sees the symlink points to <<<deferred>>>:

Triggers the derivation from the original.

Stores the derived resource under SHA-256 + interpretation.

Atomically updates the symlink to point to the actual derived resource.

Returns 301 redirect to the newly created derived file.

Subsequent requests follow the updated symlink directly — no recomputation needed.

3. Advantages for DB → FS migration
No upfront CPU cost for scaling: Only materialize what is actually needed.

Controlled derivations: Only symlinks for allowed transformations are created.

Scalable DAG structure: Each derived resource can track multiple parents if reused.

Atomic updates & consistency: Guarantees symlink points to a valid file after creation.

Efficient migration: The database dump doesn’t need to precompute all transformations, just the original storage and symlink scaffolding.

4. Optional FSCK integration
FSCK can later:

Verify SHA-256 hashes of original and derived resources.

Ensure symlink reciprocity (parents ↔ children).

Optionally re-run reproducible transformations for deferred symlinks that were never materialized.

This keeps the system self-consistent over time, even if some derived resources are never accessed.

You essentially end up with a hybrid content-addressable + lazy transformation storage system, optimized for massive DB → FS migration without upfront processing.

CDN component uses csync2 to distribute in multi-master.  Any side can materialize on-demand and push to other nodes.

Exactly — that fits naturally with your lazy, content-addressable DAG design. Here’s how the multi-master + CDN scenario works:

1. Multi-master setup
All nodes are equal peers.

csync2 ensures file system consistency across nodes.

Original and derived resources (once materialized) are synchronized automatically to all other nodes.

2. On-demand materialization in a CDN node
Client requests a deferred resource at Node A:

Symlink points to <<<deferred>>>.

Node A materializes the derived resource locally.

Updates the symlink to point to the new derived file.

csync2 sync:

The new derived file and updated symlink are pushed to all other nodes.

Node B, C, etc., now have the derived resource and updated symlink without recomputation.

3. Key advantages
Lazy evaluation per node: Only the first node to see a request pays the CPU/storage cost.

Global consistency: csync2 ensures all nodes eventually have the same content and symlink structure.

No redundant work: Once a derived resource is materialized anywhere, other nodes just receive the synced file.

Supports multiple origins and transformations: Any node can materialize any allowed derivation.

4. Race condition handling
Two nodes could try to materialize the same derived resource simultaneously:

Use atomic file creation (e.g., tmpfile + mv) to ensure only one wins.

csync2 will propagate the resulting file, and the other node sees the existing file on sync.

5. Integration with DAG / symlinks
Parents ↔ derived symlinks are maintained across nodes.

Deferred placeholders (<<<deferred>>>) are replicated.

DAG relationships remain intact in all nodes, so lazy derivation works anywhere in the CDN.

This makes the system a fully distributed, on-demand, content-addressable CDN:

Upload at any node.

Deferred derivations handled lazily.

Derived resources pushed automatically via csync2.

DAG integrity preserved globally.

A clean-up process could be allowed to delete old derived resources, such as scaled images, setting back to deferred.

Exactly — implementing a deferred clean-up process fits naturally with your lazy DAG design. Here’s how it would work and the considerations:

1. Clean-up workflow
Identify old or infrequently used derived resources (e.g., scaled images, format conversions).

Could be based on last-access time, size limits, or age thresholds.

Delete the derived resource file from storage.

Reset all parent symlinks pointing to that derived resource back to a deferred placeholder (<<<deferred>>>).

Optional DAG bookkeeping:

Keep reciprocal parent ↔ derived links intact.

Only the file is removed; symlinks remain as deferred triggers.

2. Advantages
Storage efficiency: Frees space without removing the DAG structure.

Lazy re-materialization: Future requests automatically regenerate the derived resource on-demand.

DAG integrity preserved: Parents still “know” which derived resources are allowed.

Compatible with multi-master/CDN: csync2 will propagate both file deletion and symlink reset.

3. Implementation notes
Atomic updates:

Delete the file first, then update symlinks to <<<deferred>>> atomically to avoid broken links.

Race conditions with on-demand materialization:

If a materialization is in progress while clean-up occurs:

Use file locks or temporary files during creation.

Clean-up should skip files currently being materialized.

Optional metadata tracking:

Maintain a small database or sidecar file for access times to efficiently select stale derived resources for cleanup.

4. Example flow
Original: sha256hash_originalA.original
Derived exists: sha256hash_thumbnail-256x256.thumbnail
Parents’ symlinks: 
  sha256hash_originalA_thumbnail -> sha256hash_thumbnail-256x256

Cleanup triggers:
  - Delete file: sha256hash_thumbnail-256x256.thumbnail
  - Reset symlink: sha256hash_originalA_thumbnail -> <<<deferred>>>

Next client request:
  - System sees symlink points to <<<deferred>>>
  - Re-materializes thumbnail
  - Updates symlink to new file
This approach gives you full control over storage costs, while keeping the system lazy, reproducible, and consistent.

You now have a complete lifecycle: upload → deferred symlinks → on-demand derivation → CDN sync → optional cleanup → re-materialization.

Each interpretation (essentially a MIME-type or transformation type) is represented as a symlink pointing to the SHA-256 binary:

Extensions/symlink names define authorized transformations.

Only transformations represented by symlinks are allowed; requests for unlisted interpretations can be denied.

Authorization at the file system level

Prevents arbitrary derivations.

Deferred symlinks only exist for pre-approved transformations.

Deferred / lazy evaluation

Symlink can point to <<<deferred>>> until first materialization.

Multiple interpretations per binary

3. Filesystem layout example
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original

# Reciprocal parent links in derived
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1_parent -> sha256hash_originalA.original
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived2_parent -> sha256hash_originalB.original
4. Enforcement
Only create symlinks for pre-approved transformations.

Clients cannot request arbitrary interpretations because:

No corresponding symlink exists.

DAG + fsck ensures integrity.

On-demand materialization respects these authorized interpretations.

This design allows authorization, deduplication, lazy derivation, and DAG provenance to coexist cleanly in a universal data store.

Idea is that content, if dynamic transformation not needed, could be served directly by nginx for massive scalability, but will typically be served by Tomcat with interception for managing deferred and redirecting types.

Exactly — this is a hybrid serving architecture combining direct static delivery for scalable assets and application-level control for deferred/lazy derivations. Here’s how it would work:

1. Serving static content
SHA-256 binary files (originals or fully materialized derived resources) can be served directly via Nginx:

Extremely fast and scalable (millions of requests per second if needed).

Works best for fully materialized resources with no transformations required.

Example:

location /content/ {
    root /data/universal_store;
    try_files $uri =404;
}
This is ideal for popular derived resources (e.g., thumbnails, webp versions) after materialization.

2. Application interception (Tomcat)
Requests for deferred or dynamic transformations are handled by Tomcat (or another app server):

Checks if the symlink points to <<<deferred>>>.

If so, materializes the derived resource on-demand.

Updates symlink atomically to the newly created SHA-256 file.

Returns a 301 redirect to the actual derived resource.

Logic:

Request received.

If file exists → 301 redirect (or optionally serve directly if allowed).

If symlink points to <<<deferred>>> → materialize → update symlink → 301 redirect.

3. Benefits of this hybrid approach
Massive scalability for static content

Popular, fully materialized assets bypass application logic entirely.

Nginx can serve billions of requests with minimal overhead.

Lazy derivation and DAG control

Tomcat handles deferred transformations.

Ensures only authorized transformations are materialized.

Maintains parent ↔ derived DAG consistency.

Seamless CDN integration

Nginx-friendly URLs can be cached at the edge.

Lazy transformations handled at origin only when needed.

csync2 keeps nodes in sync.

Flexible delivery

Direct binary serving for fully materialized content.

Application-level interception only when required.

4. Request flow example
Client requests: /content/thumbnail-256x256/sha256hash_originalA

Nginx:
  - Checks if derived file exists
  - If exists: serves directly (static)
  - If not: proxies request to Tomcat

Tomcat:
  - Checks symlink: points to <<<deferred>>>?
  - Materializes derived resource
  - Updates symlink
  - Returns 301 redirect to SHA-256 file
This architecture combines ultra-scalable static delivery with controlled, lazy derivation in a universal data store.

You could scale billions of original + derived files, only materializing what’s needed, while still allowing on-demand transformations with full DAG provenance and authorization.

Exactly — for your current scenario, ACLs aren’t needed because all content is public. But designing for future ACL support at the original + interpretation level fits naturally:

1. ACL placement
Attach ACLs to original SHA-256 + interpretation entries.

Derived resources inherit ACLs from their parent(s):

If multiple parents, ACL can be merged or intersection of parent ACLs.

Only authorized clients can trigger materialization or access derived resources.

Deferred symlinks don’t have independent ACLs; they just reference the parent ACL via the interpretation.

2. Advantages of this design
Minimal storage impact

ACL metadata only stored at the original + interpretation level.

No need for per-derived-file ACL metadata; derived access is implied.

Lazy evaluation compatible

Unauthorized clients cannot materialize derived resources if ACLs are enforced at the parent.

Only requests passing ACL checks reach materialization logic.

Consistent multi-master / CDN behavior

ACLs can be replicated with the DAG structure, and enforcement occurs at the application layer (Tomcat).

Nginx can still serve fully public content directly without checks.

3. Implementation concept
ACL metadata could be stored as a sidecar JSON or DB entry for each original SHA-256 + interpretation:

{
  "sha256": "e3b0c44298fc1c149afbf4c8996fb924...",
  "type": "original",
  "allowed_users": ["userA", "userB"],
  "allowed_groups": ["group1"]
}
When a derived resource is requested:

Check ACL of the parent.

Only allow materialization if authorized.

Serve derived resource or redirect accordingly.

This keeps the system future-proof for ACLs while current public content remains fully accessible, without introducing overhead or complexity.

You maintain lazy, on-demand derivation, DAG consistency, and multi-master sync even if ACLs are added later.

Update symlinks atomically after derivation.

Ensure DAG consistency for parent ↔ derived symlinks.

Optional cleanup / fsck

Removing stale derived resources.

Resetting symlinks to deferred.

Verifying hashes and symlink reciprocity.

2. API design (conceptual)
// Upload original

// Request derived resource (returns redirect or path)
DerivedResource getDerived(String sha256, String interpretation, Map<String,String> options);

// Query metadata
ResourceMetadata getMetadata(String sha256, String interpretation);

// Cleanup derived resource
void cleanupDerived(String sha256, String interpretation);
Sha256Resource and DerivedResource encapsulate:

SHA-256 hash

File path / URL

Interpretation type

Parent ↔ derived links

1. Database integration
Original + type references in DB

Store something like:

CREATE TABLE image_reference (
    id BIGINT PRIMARY KEY,
    auction_consignment_id BIGINT,
    sort_order INT,

);
The DB tracks associations: which auction consignment each image belongs to, and its sort order.

The universal store is agnostic of business context; DB enforces business logic.

2. Runtime consistency checks
On every access (webapp/API):

Check if the file exists in the filesystem at expected SHA + interpretation.

If missing:

Serve a broken link placeholder.

Record the mismatch in monitoring/logs for later admin review.

Advantages

System keeps running even if files are missing.

Admins get early warning of missing or inconsistent resources.

3. Periodic FSCK + DB analysis
Run FSCK on the filesystem:

Verify SHA matches content.

Check symlink reciprocity and deferred placeholders.

Identify unreferenced derived resources.

Cross-check FSCK results with DB:

Identify DB entries pointing to missing SHA+interpretation.

Identify files on disk that have no DB references (possible orphan derived resources).

Reporting

Generate monitoring reports (email, dashboard) for:

Missing originals or derived resources.

Orphan derived resources.

Broken parent/child links in DAG.

This is purely monitoring, not blocking runtime.

4. Broken resource handling
If a DB reference points to a missing SHA+interpretation:

Serve placeholder image for user-facing apps.

Optionally mark the record as “needs attention” in DB or monitoring system.

On deferred resources:

If symlink points to <<<deferred>>> → system behaves normally, no alert needed.

5. Operational principles
Keep running as much as possible

FSCK and DB consistency checks are non-blocking.

Runtime logs all missing/mismatched resources for later inspection.

Monitoring & alerting

Track mismatches both during runtime and during periodic FSCK.

Send alerts for admin review (Slack, dashboard, email, etc.).

Optional metrics: % of images missing, orphan derived resources, deferred transformations pending.

Cleanup policies

Orphan derived resources can be pruned safely.

Missing files could be restored from backup or re-materialized if originals exist.

6. Summary
This approach gives you:

Separation of concerns: DB tracks business associations; filesystem stores content.

Resilient runtime: Serve placeholders for missing files, log issues, keep system running.

Monitoring-first integrity: FSCK + DB consistency checks produce admin reports and alerts.

Safe lazy cleanup: Orphans can be pruned, deferred resources recreated as needed.

Proactive operations: Admins can act before missing files cause user-visible errors.

In our case, could remove a resource, and all derived resources that have no other parent, when no longer referenced in the database.  Hooks in application could trigger this where at this time would be a db cascade delete.  Or could do a daily background cleanup, possibly do daily fsck+db check with cleanup, same thing done after startup.

Exactly — that gives you a safe, reference-counted cleanup strategy tied to your database. Here’s a structured approach:

1. Reference-driven deletion
Trigger point: When a database cascade deletes an original or removes the last reference to a resource.

Logic:

Identify the original SHA + interpretation being removed.

Remove the original file from the universal store.

Recursively remove derived resources that have no other parents (i.e., are no longer referenced elsewhere in the DAG).

Update all parent/child symlinks to maintain DAG integrity.

Advantage: Deletes only truly orphaned derived resources; shared derived resources with multiple parents are preserved.

2. Background cleanup / periodic FSCK
Daily or scheduled task:

FSCK on filesystem: verify hashes, symlinks, deferred placeholders.

Cross-check DB references:

Orphaned originals (no DB reference) → mark or delete.

Orphaned derived resources (no parents) → delete.

Update DAG: remove symlinks for deleted resources, reset deferred links if needed.

Generate monitoring reports for admins.

After startup: Run a similar FSCK + DB check to ensure system consistency before handling requests.

3. Runtime monitoring
Even outside cleanup tasks, log and monitor:

Requests for missing resources.

Deferred resources that fail to materialize.

DAG inconsistencies (symlink mismatches).

Ensures issues are visible without blocking runtime.

4. Implementation considerations
Atomic deletions:

Delete derived files only after removing all references (symlinks from parents).

Prevent race conditions with concurrent materialization.

Hooks in application:

When a DB cascade delete occurs, trigger resource cleanup immediately or enqueue for background processing.

Deferred resources:

If a deferred resource is no longer allowed (parent deleted or type no longer authorized), delete the symlink placeholder.

Reporting:

Keep logs of cleanup activity: deleted files, removed symlinks, orphans.

5. Summary
Cleanup is safe, deterministic, and reference-counted.

Can be triggered immediately on DB cascade or batched daily.

Works with deferred resources, multi-parent DAGs, and multi-master replication.

FSCK + DB consistency checks ensure integrity and monitoring visibility.

This design completes the lifecycle:

Upload original → create deferred symlinks.

Materialize derived resources on-demand.

Multi-master sync via csync2.

Serve fully materialized files via Nginx; deferred via Tomcat/API.

Cleanup unreferenced originals + orphaned derived resources safely, either via hooks or scheduled FSCK+DB tasks.

You now have a fully operational universal data store with lifecycle management, lazy derivation, DAG tracking, monitoring, and cleanup.

No DB knowledge.

Knows the DB.

Exactly — adding a last-accessed timestamp per derived resource in the CDN layer would let you implement intelligent, usage-based cleanup without touching the DB. Here’s how it could work:

1. Tracking last access
For each fully materialized derived resource, maintain a last-accessed timestamp.

Implementation options:

Filesystem extended attributes (xattr) storing epoch time.

Small metadata sidecar file per derived SHA+interpretation.

In-memory cache with periodic flush to disk (for very high throughput).

Update timestamp on:

Any access via Nginx (using a small module or log-based processing).

Any access via API / Tomcat for deferred materialization.

2. Usage-based cleanup policy
Periodic background task scans derived resources:

Identify stale derived files that haven’t been accessed in a defined time window (e.g., 30 days).

Only remove files that have no other parent referencing them.

Reset symlinks to <<<deferred>>> placeholders.

Advantages:

Keeps frequently accessed derived resources cached for speed.

Minimizes unnecessary storage for rarely used derivations.

Works entirely on the CDN layer — DB isn’t required for this cleanup.

3. Optional enhancements
Combine last-accessed date + size or priority to implement LRU or weighted eviction.

Log deletions and accesses for monitoring and reporting.

Could integrate with multi-master replication so all CDN nodes share last-accessed info if needed.

4. Benefits
Dynamic storage optimization: Removes derived resources intelligently based on actual usage.

No DB dependency: Works fully within CDN/storage layer.

Safe DAG handling: Only orphaned derived resources are removed; shared resources remain intact.

Synergy with deferred derivation: Removed derived files can be lazily regenerated on next request.

This gives a complementary cleanup mechanism to DB-triggered removal: one based on business references, the other based on usage patterns. Together, they can keep your store lean, fast, and consistent.

But if could be removed, only redirect to the derived sha url if is reproducible.  Otherwise serve as the parent.type/scaled-800x600 or something that captures the derivation info.

Exactly — that distinction is critical for safe lazy regeneration. You want two classes of derived resources:

1. Reproducible transformations
Definition: Given the same parent SHA + derivation parameters, the output is deterministic.

Examples: fixed-size thumbnails, lossless format conversions.

Behavior:

If a derived resource has been deleted (e.g., by last-accessed cleanup), you can safely redirect to its canonical SHA URL after regenerating it deterministically.

The SHA remains constant; symlink updates are predictable.

This supports full caching at CDN nodes.

2. Non-reproducible transformations
Definition: Transformations may produce different output each time (randomized watermarking, lossy filters, content-dependent changes).

Behavior:

If a derived resource is removed, you cannot redirect to a precomputed SHA, because it may no longer exist or may be different if regenerated.

Instead, serve it via parent.type/derivation-info URL, e.g.:

/sha256hash_originalA.original/scaled-800x600/
On access:

Lazy materialization occurs.

Symlink updated to the newly created derived SHA.

Future requests may now redirect to the new SHA URL if reproducible thereafter.

3. Filesystem / symlink layout example
# Parent original
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original

# Reproducible derived
e3/b0/thumbnail-256x256 -> sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail

# Non-reproducible derived (deleted)
e3/b0/watermarked-800x600 -> <<<deferred>>>
Access to a deleted reproducible derived resource → regenerate → redirect to sha256hash_derivedX.

Access to a deleted non-reproducible derived resource → regenerate → serve via parent.type/derivation-info.

4. Advantages
Safe caching: Only deterministic outputs are globally redirectable to SHA URLs.

Lazy regeneration: Non-reproducible outputs are still materialized on-demand without breaking the DAG.

Flexible cleanup: Last-accessed cleanup can safely remove reproducible derived resources without losing correctness; non-reproducible derived files can be removed but served dynamically until regenerated.

DAG integrity preserved: Parent ↔ derived relationships remain intact; only symlinks and files are regenerated.

This ensures storage-efficient lazy derivation while maintaining correctness and predictable caching for reproducible transformations.

You now have a complete strategy for handling both deterministic and non-deterministic derived resources in your universal data store.


Problem with deleting even when reproducible: cdn would not know how to recreate if request comes straight into sha of derived resource.  Unless we keep reciprocal symlinks and just delete the binary data for reproducible.

Exactly — that’s the subtle but crucial point: if you delete the SHA binary for a reproducible derived resource, the CDN node cannot regenerate it on its own if a request comes directly to the SHA URL. You need a way to retain the provenance information.

1. Solution: keep reciprocal symlinks, delete only binary
Symlinks from parent → derived and derived → parent remain in place.

The derived SHA file can be deleted (to reclaim storage).

On request to that SHA:

Look up the parent symlink(s) and the derivation type/parameters.

Regenerate the binary from the parent(s).

Serve or recreate the SHA file as before.

The DAG integrity is preserved; you never lose the “recipe” for regenerating a reproducible derived resource.

2. Filesystem layout example
# Original
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original

# Reproducible derived (binary deleted, symlinks remain)
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail -> <<<deferred>>>
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1_parent -> sha256hash_originalA.original
sha256hash_originalA_thumbnail-256x256 -> sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail
sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail file is deleted.

Parent → derived symlink still exists.

Derived → parent symlink still exists.

CDN receives request to /sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail → use DAG:

Find parent(s) + derivation parameters.

Re-materialize derived file.

Serve it and optionally restore SHA binary.

3. Advantages
Storage reclaim: Binary deleted when not needed.

On-demand regeneration: CDN or webapp can recreate it using the DAG.

Consistent caching: Once regenerated, SHA file can be served via Nginx/CDN as usual.

Safe for multi-master: csync2 propagates symlinks, not the regenerated content until materialized.

4. Implementation notes
Treat binary deletion as a reversible operation. DAG and symlinks are the authoritative source.

For non-reproducible derived resources, deletion should also reset symlinks to <<<deferred>>>.

Optionally, maintain a last-accessed timestamp to prioritize which reproducible derived binaries to delete.

This way, even if a CDN node receives a direct request to a SHA URL for a reproducible derived resource, it can always regenerate using the DAG and parent(s).

You effectively decouple storage from determinism: the DAG + symlinks encode the recipe; the SHA binary is just a cache.


Parents of a derived resource is sha+type+derivation.

Something is original if has no parents.  Something original could become derived if happens to match new derivation of another resource.  Consider text derivation where many things could result in same file content.

Exactly — you’re describing a content-addressable DAG with typed derivations, where identity is determined by SHA + type + derivation parameters rather than just SHA alone. Here’s a precise breakdown:

1. Resource identity
Original resource

Defined as: has no parents.

Identified by: SHA + type (e.g., sha256hash_originalA.original).

Derived resource

Defined as: has one or more parents with associated derivation parameters.

Identity: (SHA, type, derivation_parameters).

Parents stored as symlinks pointing to parent SHA + type + derivation type.

2. Multiple derivation collisions
It’s possible for different derivations to produce the same content:

Example: two text derivations produce identical output.

The resulting SHA is the same, but the parent + derivation context differs.

Implications:

File deduplication: Only one SHA file stored on disk.

Multiple DAG entries: Each parent + derivation combo gets its own symlink pointing to that single SHA.

Reciprocal links: Derived → parent symlinks track all parents with the associated derivation parameters.

3. Filesystem / symlink layout example
# Original files
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original
a7/6c/sha256hash_originalB.original

# Derived resource (same SHA from two different derivations)
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1.text
  derived1_parent1 -> sha256hash_originalA.original (derivation=text-summarize)
  derived1_parent2 -> sha256hash_originalB.original (derivation=text-extract)
  
# Parent → derived symlinks
sha256hash_originalA_text-summarize -> sha256hash_derived1.text
sha256hash_originalB_text-extract   -> sha256hash_derived1.text
SHA file stored once.

Each parent + derivation gets its own symlink.

Multiple parents can point to the same SHA file.

4. Rules
Original → derived

Original: no parents.

Derived: one or more parents + derivation parameters.

Original becomes derived

If a new derivation produces content identical to an existing original, it can become a derived resource with the existing SHA:

Original SHA file remains.

Parent symlink points to it with the derivation parameters.

Deduplication

Storage is always by SHA.

DAG tracks provenance, not just file content.

5. FSCK / DAG integrity
FSCK checks:

SHA matches file content.

Parent ↔ derived symlinks are reciprocal.

All derivations point to correct SHA file.

Orphans or missing files logged for monitoring.

This ensures multiple parents, shared SHA, and derivation collisions are handled safely.

This model fully supports:

Multiple parents per derived resource.

Deduplicated storage by SHA.

Derivation collisions (same output from different parents).

Original resources that later become derived.

If you want, I can draw a DAG diagram showing originals, derived resources with multiple parents, symlinks, and derivation parameters — that visualizes collisions and deduplication cleanly.


Sha+type maybe not a symlink, or allow a matching meta file, which could provide fast cached access to things like image resolution.

Exactly — instead of requiring a symlink for every SHA+type, you can optionally store metadata files alongside the SHA file. This gives you fast, cached access to frequently needed attributes without hitting the DAG or recomputing content.

1. Meta file per SHA+type
For each stored SHA+type, create a small JSON or binary metadata file, e.g.:

e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original.meta.json
Contents might include:

{
  "sha256": "e3b0c44298fc1c149afbf4c8996fb924...",
  "type": "original",
  "format": "image/png",
  "width": 1024,
  "height": 768,
  "color_model": "RGB",
  "derived_from": [],
  "last_accessed": "2026-04-02T12:00:00Z"
}
2. Advantages
Avoids symlink overhead

If a resource doesn’t need deferred derivation or DAG links (e.g., originals only), no symlink required.

Fast attribute access

Webapp can read metadata (dimensions, format, resolution) without opening or parsing the full binary.

Optional caching

Metadata can include precomputed values used frequently (image size, hash, MIME type, derived counts).

Supports both original and derived resources

Derived resources can include parents + derivation parameters in metadata.

Lightweight DAG integration

DAG info can be in metadata rather than filesystem symlinks if preferred.

3. Metadata + symlink hybrid
Original-only resource

SHA+type stored with metadata file.

No symlink needed unless it becomes a parent of derived resources.

Derived resource

SHA+type stored with metadata file.

Parent ↔ derived symlinks can coexist with metadata.

Metadata can cache attributes like resolution, derivation parameters, last access, reproducibility.

Example layout:

# Original
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original
e3/b0/sha256hash_originalA.original.meta.json

# Derived
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail
e3/b0/sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail.meta.json
sha256hash_originalA_thumbnail-256x256 -> sha256hash_derived1.thumbnail
sha256hash_derived1_parent -> sha256hash_originalA.original
4. Use cases for metadata
Quick image queries in webapp: resolution, format, or color space.

Determining if a resource is reproducible without recomputing.

Last-accessed tracking for smart cleanup.

DAG traversal info for FSCK without touching filesystem symlinks.

This approach gives you a hybrid storage model:

SHA+type binary → content.

Optional metadata → cached attributes, derivation info.

Optional symlinks → DAG relationships and deferred derivations.

It maximizes performance and flexibility while keeping the filesystem clean and supporting lazy derivation.

If you want, I can sketch the full hybrid layout showing binaries, metadata files, symlinks, and deferred placeholders for reproducible/non-reproducible resources.


Full fsck could possibly verify meta data.  Two pass fsck: consistency check on startup and every night, second pass content derivation and meta checks (less frequent, off hours)

Exactly — a two-pass FSCK fits perfectly for a universal data store that balances runtime availability, monitoring, and resource-heavy verification. Here’s a detailed design:

1. Pass 1: Startup / nightly consistency check
Goal: Maintain system integrity with minimal disruption.

Checks:

Filesystem consistency

Verify SHA file exists for each parent → derived symlink.

Verify symlink reciprocity: parent → derived and derived → parent.

Check deferred symlinks are present and valid.

DB cross-check

Ensure all DB references point to existing SHA+type (originals or derived).

Log any broken references for monitoring.

Quick metadata validation

Ensure metadata file exists for each SHA+type that requires it.

Characteristics:

Fast / safe

Can run at startup or during nightly maintenance without heavy CPU load.

Non-blocking

Runtime continues to serve existing requests.

Monitoring

Produce reports of missing files, broken symlinks, or DB mismatches.

2. Pass 2: Content derivation + metadata verification
Goal: Deep validation and optional regeneration of derived resources.

Checks / actions:

Verify content

Compute SHA of each stored file and compare to filename.

For derived resources, optionally regenerate from parent(s) and compare SHA.

Verify metadata

Validate stored metadata matches actual content attributes (image resolution, format, reproducibility flags, etc.).

For derived resources, validate derivation parameters recorded in metadata match parent(s).

Optional materialization

Re-materialize missing derived resources if reproducible.

Orphan cleanup

Detect unreferenced derived files (no parent symlinks, no DB reference).

Optionally delete or reset to deferred.

Characteristics:

Heavy / off-hours

CPU- and I/O-intensive; typically run during low traffic.

Optional automatic fixes

Only regenerate reproducible derived files.

Non-reproducible files may just be logged for admin review.

Monitoring

Produces detailed report for storage optimization, DAG integrity, and metadata correctness.

3. Advantages of two-pass FSCK
Continuous availability

Fast first-pass ensures system can run safely while detecting obvious problems.

Data integrity

Second pass ensures SHA correctness, metadata consistency, and DAG reliability.

Smart cleanup

Combined with last-accessed or orphan detection, keeps storage lean.

Monitoring-first approach

Admin alerts on broken links, missing files, and DB mismatches.

Flexible frequency

Pass 1: startup + nightly.

Pass 2: weekly or off-hours, depending on store size and derivation complexity.

4. Suggested flow
Startup/Nightly FSCK (pass 1)
    ├─ check symlinks & reciprocity
    ├─ check DB references
    └─ quick metadata existence check
        └─ log mismatches

Off-hours / Weekly FSCK (pass 2)
    ├─ verify file content SHA
    ├─ verify derived resource correctness
    ├─ verify metadata accuracy (resolution, format, derivation info)
    ├─ optionally regenerate reproducible derived resources
    └─ detect & report orphans / cleanup candidates
This two-pass strategy balances fast runtime checks with deep validation, preserves system availability, and gives a reliable framework for monitoring, cleanup, and storage optimization.

If you want, I can draw a full diagram showing two-pass FSCK, DAG integrity, metadata validation, and cleanup flow — it would visually show how startup, nightly, and off-hours passes interact.


Does this system exist?

There isn’t one off‑the‑shelf product that does exactly what you’ve described as a single integrated system, but the conceptual building blocks you’re assembling do exist in various mature components — and some systems come close. Your design is essentially:

Content‑addressable storage (CAS)

Lazy derivation of transformations

DAG tracking / provenance

Multi‑master replication

Filesystem or object store backing

DB‑driven metadata

On‑demand regeneration

FSCK / integrity checking

No single packaged product implements all of those things out of the box, but many systems embody subsets of the idea.

1) Content‑Addressable Storage Systems
These systems use hashes as identifiers, deduplicate, and often track provenance:

● Git
Classic CAS with DAG, but optimized for text/code, not large binary blobs.

● IPFS (InterPlanetary File System)
Global content‑addressable storage using SHA‑based CIDs.

Deduplication and content addressing are core.

Has DAG structures (Merkle DAG).

Doesn’t natively do lazy transformations, but it does preserve parent links structurally.

● Dat
A CAS/DAG protocol focused on datasets and versioning.

● CAS Layers in Storage Systems
Some object stores (Ceph/Swift, MinIO) can be backed by CAS layers.

2) Object Stores + Transformation Pipelines
These don’t use CAS naming by default, but they do manage derived resources:

● Amazon S3 + Lambda
Store original object.

On first GET or PUT, trigger Lambda to generate derived versions.

Can use S3 metadata for attributes.

No native DAG or link tracking.

● Thumbor / imgproxy / Kraken
On‑demand image transformations.

Cache results at CDN edge.

No global CAS or parent/child DAG storage by default.

● Contentful / Cloudinary / Imgix
Cloud media platforms with on‑demand transforms and caching.

They generate derivatives lazily and cache them.

Metadata and access rules exist.

But they are proprietary services.

3) Distributed Filesystems with CAS Features
● Perkeep (formerly Camlistore)
Content‑addressable blob storage.

Stores metadata separate from content.

Pluggable index and tag graph.

Not specifically optimized for lazy derivations, but data model is very close.

● Tahoe‑LAFS
Secure distributed CAS filesystem.

Integrity and replication.

No derivation layer built in.

● Nix / Guix Store
CAS store for packages.

Tracks derivations and build provenance.

Uses content hashes to determine identity; “derivations” are first‑class objects.

Strongly influenced ideas like yours.

4) Systems with Provenance & Transform Pipelines
● Reproducible build systems (e.g., Nix)
Track inputs, outputs, content hashes, and rebuild only if needed.

Supports sharing CAS objects across machines.

● Build Cache Systems
Bazel remote cache / RBE, Gradle build cache.

CAS + action cache (transformation DAG).

5) Image/Media Servers with Lazy Transform
Systems that do some parts of your design:

● Thumbor / imgproxy
Lazy image resizing with cache.

Can serve by URL parameters → cached derived assets.

Doesn’t integrate CAS naming or DB metadata.

● Cloudinary / Imgix / Fastly Image Optimizer
Lazy transformations, CDN caches results.

Not open‑source building block, but conceptually similar.

6) What Doesn’t Exist Today in One Package
Your design combines capabilities that no current system universally provides together:

Feature	Common in Existing	Complete Support
Content‑addressable naming (SHA)	Yes (CAS systems)	✗ Not with full media semantics
Lazy derivation with deferred placeholders	Partially (thumbor/Cloudinary)	✗ Not linked to CAS
Parent ↔ derived DAG with symlinks	In some CAS and build systems	✗ Not typical for media stores
Multi‑master replication + sync	Yes (filesystems, object stores)	✗ Not with DAG/provenance
FSCK / integrity + metadata checks	Yes (filesystems)	✗ Not integrated w/ lazy transforms
DB metadata + associations	Yes	Depends on integration
CDN‑agnostic direct serving of SHA URLs	Yes (static CDN)	Not combined w/ DAG regeneration
7) Closest Systems / Inspiration
You could view your target as combining:

CAS (IPFS / Perkeep)
→ lazy transform engine (Thumbor/Imgproxy)
→ DAG metadata & provenance (build systems like Nix)
→ replication (object store / sync tools)
→ web/CDN serving (Nginx/HTTP)

There are research systems that approximate parts of this — e.g., Perkeep’s blobstore with metadata, Nix’s derivation graph, and IPFS’s content addressing — but none deliver your full stack without extension.

8) Summary
Not a single existing product does all of this as you’ve architected it.

The building blocks exist in mature projects.

Your design is not academically speculative — it’s grounded in existing patterns (CAS, lazy derivation, DAG provenance, CDN caching).

You’re effectively proposing a purpose‑built universal CAS + transformation store with metadata, DAG, and cleanup, which is an advancement over most media stores today.

9) Implication
This means:

Your architecture is novel enough to warrant its own library/service.

You could potentially open‑source this as a new platform.

Engineers familiar with CAS, derivation systems, and lazy transformation pipelines would see this as a composable, scalable design.

If you want, I can map your full design into an architectural blueprint using existing components — showing how you could assemble your system from open‑source parts with minimal custom code. That would make it immediately actionable.

Idea is upload component sets defered allowed derivations, but cdn component does the work.  Less chance for cross process lock contention.  We already have a lot of work on locking within a node, but concurrent derivation on multiple nodes is possible.  If non-reproducible would generate mismatch symlink, and try to push each direction with conflict.  Automatic resolution beyond scope, we would monitor csync2 status as admin.

That division is sound — you’re effectively pushing coordination out of the critical path and accepting eventual consistency with monitoring, which is the right tradeoff at your scale.

1. Separation of responsibilities (clean model)
Upload / Webapp side
Defines:

Original content (SHA)

Allowed derivations (creates deferred symlinks)

Does no heavy work

No contention risk

CDN / Node side
Handles:

Materialization

Local locking (per-node)

Serving

Accepts that:

Multiple nodes may derive the same thing concurrently

This avoids:

Cross-node locking (hard problem)

Central coordination bottlenecks

2. Concurrent derivation across nodes
For reproducible transformations:

Multiple nodes derive → identical output → identical SHA

Result:

No real conflict

csync2 converges naturally

Extra CPU wasted, but harmless

This is exactly the kind of redundancy you want in a distributed system.

3. Non-reproducible transformations (your edge case)
You’ve correctly identified the failure mode:

Two nodes derive same “logical” resource

Output differs → different SHA

Both try to:

Update symlink

Sync via csync2

Result:

Conflict / flip-flopping symlink

Or inconsistent state across nodes

Your current stance:

Automatic resolution is out of scope; monitor via csync2

That’s reasonable — but there are a few lightweight guardrails you can add without introducing heavy coordination.

4. Low-cost mitigation strategies
A. Mark derivation type as reproducible vs non-reproducible
Store in:

symlink naming

or metadata

CDN behavior:

Reproducible → safe to race

Non-reproducible → restrict behavior

B. Single-writer hint (soft coordination)
Without global locks, you can reduce collisions:

Hash-based ownership:

e.g., node = hash(sha + derivation) % N

Only “owner” node materializes

Others:

wait / retry

or proxy request

This is:

Deterministic

No coordination required

Dramatically reduces collisions

C. Immutable derived naming for non-reproducible
Instead of:

parent -> derived
Use:

parent -> derived-request
derived-request -> sha_actual_output
So conflicts don’t overwrite each other — they just create multiple candidates.

Then:

Admin or fsck can reconcile later

No destructive overwrite

D. Conflict visibility (important)
You already rely on csync2 monitoring — good.
Enhance it slightly:

Log:

“same derivation, different SHA”

Track:

count of conflicting derivations

Alert threshold:

if > 0 for reproducible → bug

if frequent for non-reproducible → design issue

5. Why your approach is still correct
You’ve made a key architectural decision:

Prefer eventual consistency + observability over distributed locking

That aligns with systems like:

CDNs

build caches

distributed object stores

Trying to “perfectly coordinate” derivations across nodes would:

Add latency

Increase failure modes

Create central bottlenecks

6. Mental model (important)
Treat derived resources as:

Cache entries with provenance, not strictly authoritative data

Then:

Races are acceptable

Cleanup is acceptable

Regeneration is acceptable

Conflicts are observable, not fatal

7. Summary
Your design choices here are solid:

Upload defines what can exist

CDN decides when it exists

Nodes operate independently

Consistency is eventual

Conflicts are monitored, not prevented

The only real boundary to enforce:

Non-reproducible derivations must not silently overwrite each other

Everything else can remain as-is and scale cleanly.

If you want, I can help you define a small set of invariants (like 5–7 rules) that guarantee the system behaves correctly even under races and partial failures. That’s usually the fastest way to make something like this production-safe without overengineering it.


Application could add/remove allowed derivations, doing clean-up or adding as-needed on start db consistency check, such as a new scaling value allowed to match code changes.

Yes — that’s a very clean way to handle evolution of allowed transformations without tightly coupling it to deployment timing. You’re essentially making allowed derivations part of application state, not just filesystem state.

1. Model: allowed derivations as policy layer
The application (DB-aware) defines:

Which derivations are valid (e.g., thumbnail-256x256, scaled-800x600)

The filesystem/CDN layer only reflects:

What currently exists (materialized or deferred)

So at any point:

DB = source of truth for what should exist
FS = cache + DAG for what does exist

2. Startup / consistency reconciliation
On application startup (or scheduled job):

A. Add missing allowed derivations
For each original referenced in DB:

For each allowed derivation (based on current code/config):

If symlink doesn’t exist → create deferred symlink

This supports:

New scaling sizes

New formats (e.g., webp, avif)

Feature rollout without reprocessing everything

B. Remove no-longer-allowed derivations
For each existing derivation symlink:

If no longer in allowed set:

Remove symlink

Optionally:

Trigger cleanup of derived resource if orphaned

This prevents:

Stale transformations hanging around forever

Drift between code and storage

3. Interaction with cleanup
This integrates nicely with your existing model:

DB-driven cleanup

Removes entire original → cascades to derived

Allowed-derivation cleanup

Removes specific transformation types across all originals

Usage-based cleanup (optional)

Removes rarely used derived binaries but keeps deferred symlinks

Each operates independently but safely because:

DAG + symlinks preserve relationships

Deferred placeholders allow regeneration

4. Advantages
A. Schema evolution without migration jobs
Add a new size → no batch job needed

It materializes only when requested

B. Safe rollback
Remove a derivation → symlinks removed

If needed later → re-added as deferred

C. No tight coupling to deploy
Code change defines allowed derivations

System converges via startup reconciliation

D. Works with multi-node CDN
Symlink updates propagate via csync2

Nodes don’t need to know “why” — just reflect state

5. Edge considerations
A. Race with in-flight derivation
If a derivation is removed while being materialized:

Safe outcome:

File may exist temporarily

Cleanup later removes it if orphaned

B. Derived resources with multiple parents
Only delete binary if:

No remaining parent symlinks

No allowed derivation references

C. Versioning derivations (optional)
If behavior changes (e.g., new scaling algorithm):

Encode version in derivation name:

thumbnail-256x256-v2
Avoids ambiguity and cache poisoning

6. Recommended invariant
A simple rule that keeps everything consistent:

For every (original, allowed derivation), a symlink must exist (either to SHA or <<<deferred>>>)

That gives you:

Deterministic system state

Easy reconciliation logic

Predictable lazy materialization

7. Summary
You now have three layers working together:

DB / application

Defines what should exist (allowed derivations, associations)

Filesystem / DAG

Tracks what exists and how it was derived

CDN / runtime

Decides when things are materialized

This is a strong design because it allows:

Feature evolution without migrations

Lazy computation

Safe cleanup

Eventual consistency across nodes

csync2 is slow in many files, but current system does sync specific directories as they are changed which is fast enough.

That makes sense — and it highlights an important constraint: csync2 works well for localized change propagation, but not for large-scale churn.

Your current usage pattern is actually aligned with its strengths:

Small, targeted updates → fast sync
Large directory-wide changes → slow

1. Why your current approach works
Uploads and derivations:

Touch very small subsets of the tree

Sync only a few files/symlinks at a time

csync2:

Efficient when change sets are small and incremental

Avoids full directory scans

So your system behaves like:

Write amplification low

Sync granularity fine

→ Good performance

2. Where problems will show up
You’ll hit slowdowns when you do:

A. Bulk operations
Adding a new allowed derivation across millions of originals

Removing a derivation globally

Large cleanup passes

These cause:

Many symlink creations/deletions

Large directory churn

csync2 scanning overhead

3. Your design already avoids the worst case
Because you:

Use deferred symlinks

Do lazy materialization

Avoid precomputing everything

You’ve already eliminated the biggest scaling risk:

“create millions of derived files at once”

4. Recommended operational strategy
A. Prefer incremental reconciliation
Instead of:

“add derivation to all files now”

Do:

Add derivation only when touched

Or batch slowly in background

B. Throttle bulk changes
If you must do global changes:

Process in small batches

e.g., 1k–10k files per run

Sleep between batches

Let csync2 keep up

C. Directory locality matters
Your hash-based directory layout helps:

Changes are spread across many directories

Reduces hotspot contention

Keeps per-directory sync small

This is a major advantage of your design.

D. Avoid unnecessary symlink churn
When reconciling allowed derivations:

Check before creating/removing

Only modify when state actually differs

This avoids:

needless inode changes

unnecessary csync2 propagation

5. Future optimization options (if needed)
If csync2 becomes a bottleneck later:

Option 1: Event-driven sync
Instead of scanning, push changed paths directly

(csync2 partially supports this via hints)

Option 2: Split sync domains
Separate:

originals

derived

metadata

Sync independently

Option 3: Eventually replace csync2
If scale grows large enough, systems like:

rsync with change queues

or object storage replication

But for now, your usage pattern doesn’t justify that complexity.

6. Key insight
Your architecture works with csync2 because:

You’ve turned large, synchronous work into small, asynchronous events

Upload → small change

First access → small change

Cleanup → controlled change

That’s why it scales despite csync2’s limitations.

7. Practical rule
To keep it working well:

Never introduce a feature that causes “touch everything now” behavior

As long as you maintain:

Lazy derivation

Incremental reconciliation

Controlled cleanup

csync2 will remain “fast enough”.
