We already have a lot of the code, but was not using sha-256 and had to account for collisions.  With uniqueness assumption on SHA-256, simplifies the data model.

Yes — switching to SHA-256 with a practical uniqueness assumption meaningfully simplifies your system in several important ways. You’re essentially removing an entire class of complexity that was previously leaking into your design.

1. What you can eliminate
With collision-handling removed (except for rare verification fallback), you no longer need:

A. Secondary identity layer
Previously:

SHA → possible multiple contents

Required:

byte-by-byte verification

alternate storage paths

collision resolution logic

Now:

SHA = identity

File path is authoritative

B. Ambiguous DAG edges
Previously:

Parent → derived might not uniquely identify content

Needed:

extra checks

defensive linking

Now:

Parent + derivation → deterministic SHA (for reproducible)

DAG becomes clean and deterministic

C. Collision-aware storage layout
No need for:

collision buckets

suffixes or disambiguators

Directory structure becomes purely:

/xx/yy/<sha>.<type>
2. What simplifies directly in your design
A. Derived resource handling
Before:

“Is this actually the same content?”

Now:

If SHA matches → it is the same content

No verification path needed (except optional FSCK)

B. Concurrent derivation
Multiple nodes derive same reproducible resource:

Same SHA → no conflict

No need to:

compare binaries

reconcile duplicates

C. Cleanup logic
Orphan detection becomes:

purely graph-based (symlinks + DB refs)

No need to consider:

“same SHA but different content” edge case

D. Metadata trust
Metadata keyed by SHA becomes stable:

resolution, format, etc.

No risk that:

same SHA maps to different content later

3. Where you still keep safety (cheaply)
Even with the assumption, you can keep low-cost verification:

FSCK pass 2:
Recompute SHA of file

Compare to filename

If mismatch:

flag as corruption (not collision in practice)

This gives you:

integrity guarantee

without runtime overhead

4. Subtle but important improvement
Your earlier concern:

“original could become derived if content matches”

With SHA-256:

This becomes natural and safe

Same SHA:

one file

multiple parents

No ambiguity

This is actually a feature, not a complication.

5. Net effect on your system
You’ve effectively:

Removed identity ambiguity

Simplified DAG semantics

Eliminated collision handling paths

Reduced branching logic everywhere

Which means:

Your system shifts from “defensive” to “deterministic”

6. Practical outcome
This change alone likely cuts:

Significant code complexity

Many edge-case branches

Testing surface area

And more importantly:

Makes the system easier to reason about

Makes FSCK and cleanup reliable

Makes distributed behavior predictable

7. One remaining rule (important)
Even with SHA-256:

Always treat content mismatch as corruption, not collision

So:

If hash != content → error condition

Don’t try to “resolve” it

That keeps the model clean.

Summary
Switching to SHA-256 with uniqueness assumption:

Collapses identity to a single dimension (SHA)

Makes DAG relationships deterministic

Removes collision-handling complexity

Strengthens correctness guarantees

This is one of the highest-leverage simplifications you could make — it aligns your system with how most modern CAS systems are actually implemented in practice.


We could always verify sha when the byte are available, such as an image reupload.  If there is a mismatch, verify content in cdn, it is most likely corrupted: replace corrupted content.  If true collision detected, throw exception telling that we won a prize.

That’s a sound and pragmatic policy. You’re treating SHA-256 as authoritative identity, while still having a sanity check path when bytes are already in hand, which gives you integrity without runtime overhead.

1. When to verify
Your trigger points are correct:

On upload / reupload

You already have the bytes → compute SHA-256

Compare against expected or existing

On FSCK (pass 2)

Periodic deep verification

Optional: on suspicious cases

e.g., failed decode, unexpected metadata mismatch

Outside of that, no need to verify on normal reads.

2. Decision flow
When bytes are available:

computed_sha = sha256(content)

if computed_sha == expected_sha:
    OK
else:
    investigate
Investigation path:
Fetch existing CDN copy (if applicable)

Compare:

uploaded content

stored content

3. Outcome handling
A. Most likely: corruption
Cases:

Disk corruption

Partial write

Sync issue (csync2)

Bit rot

Action:

Treat CDN copy as bad

Replace with known-good content

Log + alert

This is the normal failure mode.

B. Very unlikely: true SHA-256 collision
Conditions:

Two different byte streams

Same SHA-256

Action:

Raise hard exception

Preserve both payloads

Disable automatic overwrite

Log aggressively

Your “we won a prize” path is effectively correct — but operationally:

Treat it as a security-level event, not just novelty

4. Important invariant
You want to enforce:

A given SHA must always map to exactly one byte sequence

So:

Never overwrite a SHA with different content silently

Never allow “last write wins” on mismatch

Always stop and investigate

5. Why this works well with your system
This policy fits your architecture:

CDN nodes are not authoritative

They can be repaired

SHA is authoritative

Defines truth

DB is metadata only

Not involved in integrity

So recovery is simple:

Re-upload or re-derive

Propagate via csync2

System converges

6. Optional hardening (low cost)
You could add:

A. Quarantine path
On mismatch:

move bad file aside instead of deleting immediately

Useful for debugging rare issues

B. Hash recheck before overwrite
Double-check before replacing content

Avoid cascading corruption

C. Metrics
Count:

mismatches

repairs

Alert if > 0 (should be extremely rare)

7. Summary
Your approach:

Uses SHA-256 as primary identity

Verifies only when cheap and natural

Treats mismatch as:

corruption (almost always)

collision (theoretical edge)

This gives you:

Strong integrity guarantees

Minimal runtime overhead

Simple operational model

And yes — if you ever hit a real SHA-256 collision in this system, debugging your storage layer will become the least interesting thing happening.


During full fsck, if sha/content mismatch found, could somehow invalide such that csync2 would reupload on next sync.

Exactly — you can turn a mismatch detected during FSCK into a self-healing event in a distributed sync context. The idea is to make the node act as if the file needs propagation without touching unrelated files.

1. Invalidate the file for csync2
csync2 decides what to sync based on modification times, checksums, or flags. You can leverage this:

Mark file as changed

Either touch the file (touch <file>)

Or update a checksum/metadata indicator

Optional quarantine

Move mismatched file aside (e.g., <sha>.corrupt)

Replace with placeholder (optional, e.g., deferred symlink)

This ensures that clients requesting the resource see either valid content or deferred state

Next csync2 pass

Node sees “file changed”

Pushes valid copy from authoritative source (upload node or another CDN node)

2. Flow example
FSCK detects: sha mismatch for /xx/yy/<sha>.type

→ move /xx/yy/<sha>.type → /xx/yy/<sha>.type.corrupt
→ touch placeholder or deferred symlink
→ log event

Next incremental csync2:
→ other nodes see new file state
→ propagate valid copy to replace invalid
→ FSCK logs repaired
Advantages:

Avoids manual intervention for every mismatch

Keeps the system eventually consistent

Works even with multi-master CDN nodes

3. Considerations
Re-derivable files: For reproducible derived content, you could optionally trigger local re-derivation instead of waiting for sync.

Non-reproducible derived content: Must rely on other authoritative nodes or original upload; you can’t safely regenerate locally.

Logging: Always log which files were invalidated — helps detect storage issues or disk corruption patterns.

Atomic replacement: Ensure that replacement or symlink swap is atomic to avoid transient broken states.

4. Optional optimization
Instead of touching every corrupted file, you could maintain a “needs-sync” index.

FSCK adds mismatched SHAs to the index

csync2 or your own sync wrapper only touches those files

Reduces churn in very large file sets

5. Summary
This approach turns FSCK into self-healing:

Detect corruption → mark for propagation → next sync repairs

Works with multi-node CDN

Maintains SHA-identity invariant

Minimal operational overhead

This ensures that mismatched or corrupted content never silently persists across your distributed system.


If is a derived reproducible, could just delete the corrupted binary data.

Exactly — for reproducible derived resources, you can treat the binary as ephemeral because it can always be regenerated from its parent(s) and the derivation parameters.

1. FSCK handling for reproducible derived resources
When a SHA/content mismatch is detected:

Delete corrupted binary

rm /path/to/derived/<sha>.type
Leave symlinks intact

Parent → derived symlink still exists

Can point to <<<deferred>>> placeholder if desired

Next request triggers regeneration

CDN node sees deferred symlink

Materializes derived resource from parent

Updates SHA/filename

Propagation via csync2

Once regenerated, csync2 syncs the correct content to other nodes

2. Advantages
No manual repair needed

Corrupted content automatically disappears and is regenerated on-demand

Minimal disruption to serving

Symlink still exists; system can return deferred placeholder or 302 redirect

No conflict risk

Reproducible derivations always generate the same SHA → deterministic convergence

Reduced storage churn

Only the corrupted binary is removed; no need to touch parents or unrelated derived resources

3. Optional enhancement
Track invalidated derived binaries

For monitoring or alerting

Gives visibility into corruption patterns without blocking requests

Atomic deletion and placeholder

Avoid transient broken symlinks during regeneration

4. Summary
For reproducible derived resources:

Corrupted content = safe to delete

Parent + derivation → guaranteed to reproduce same SHA

FSCK or runtime can safely remove corrupted binary

System automatically regenerates on next access

Distributed sync propagates correct content without extra intervention

This makes FSCK-driven repair very simple and efficient for derived reproducible content.


Current implementation has no communication between cdn nodes other that csync2 itself.

That fits perfectly with your overall design and keeps things simple. In other words: the CDN nodes are effectively stateless caches for your content-addressable storage, and csync2 is the only propagation mechanism.

1. Implications of no inter-node communication
A. Materialization
Each node can independently materialize a deferred derived resource.

Reproducible derivations are safe because:

Same parent + derivation → same SHA

Multiple nodes generating the same resource concurrently produces identical content

Non-reproducible derivations may conflict, but your design accepts that as rare and logs it.

B. Consistency
Nodes eventually converge via csync2.

There is no central coordination, which avoids:

Cross-node locks

Race conditions

Latency penalties

C. Failure handling
Node crash during materialization → partial file may exist

Next FSCK or request regenerates or replaces corrupted binary

Corrupted binaries are detected locally or via FSCK and resynced

2. Operational constraints
Because there’s no direct communication:

All “truth” comes from:

Original SHA files

DAG via symlinks

Allowed derivation set in the DB/application

Sync timing matters:

csync2 incremental updates propagate changes between nodes

Full CSYNC2 checks on startup or daily ensure convergence

Monitoring is critical:

Admin must watch:

csync2 conflict logs

FSCK mismatch reports

Since nodes don’t negotiate, human oversight prevents silent divergence

3. Advantages
Simpler architecture

No distributed locking or messaging required

Scalable

Any number of CDN nodes can operate independently

Safe for high concurrency

Materialization, deletion, and cleanup are local operations

Predictable behavior

Deterministic SHA + DAG → eventual consistency is guaranteed

4. Limitations
Non-reproducible derived resources may temporarily diverge

Only resolved when manual intervention or monitoring detects conflicts

Sync lag

Newly materialized resources may not be immediately available on other nodes until csync2 propagates

No real-time awareness

Nodes can’t coordinate to avoid duplicated work, though reproducible derivations make this low impact

5. Summary
Your design philosophy is:

CDN nodes are dumb workers + caches.
All “truth” flows from SHA-identified files and DAG symlinks, with csync2 propagating changes.

This keeps the system simple, scalable, and resilient, with minimal coordination overhead.

Optional future improvements (if needed) could add lightweight ownership hints or a work-queue for non-reproducible derivations, but they’re not required for correctness.


Is posible to flag local file as corrupted? Monitoring would alert on unrecoverable corrupted file.  Sysadmin are already fluent with csync2. Would investigate and push from a verified intact node.

Yes — you can flag a local file as corrupted without breaking the DAG or symlink structure, and let your operational workflow handle recovery. The key is to mark it locally so csync2 treats it as needing refresh, without deleting symlinks or causing errors for clients.

1. Ways to flag a file as corrupted
A. Move to a quarantine location
Rename the file:

mv /xx/yy/<sha>.type /xx/yy/<sha>.type.corrupt
Leave symlinks pointing to the SHA intact (optional deferred placeholder)

Pros:

Prevents accidental serving of corrupted data

Preserves the file for offline inspection

Cons:

Takes extra disk space temporarily

B. Replace with placeholder
Overwrite the file with a zero-byte or “<<<deferred>>>” marker

Pros:

Keeps filesystem structure consistent

Next request triggers regeneration

Cons:

Original corrupted content lost unless copied elsewhere

C. Touch the file to force csync2 update
If csync2 is using timestamps to detect changes:

touch /xx/yy/<sha>.type
Then the verified node’s copy will overwrite it on next sync

Pros:

Minimal disruption

Simple workflow

Cons:

Only works if csync2 uses timestamps or checksums to propagate updates

2. Monitoring integration
FSCK or runtime verification can log:

SHA mismatch

Binary corruption detected

Push alerts to sysadmins

Sysadmins investigate:

Check /corrupt files

Replace from verified node if needed

This is exactly compatible with your existing workflow.

3. Recommended workflow for reproducible vs non-reproducible
Type	Action on corruption
Reproducible derived	Delete binary (or move to .corrupt), keep symlink. Next request regenerates.
Non-reproducible derived	Flag file (move .corrupt), alert. Admin pushes verified copy from another node.
Original content	Flag file, alert. Admin replaces from canonical upload or backup.
4. Why this works with csync2
csync2 sees the file as changed (via timestamp, checksum, or rename)

Propagates updated copy from verified nodes

No need for extra inter-node communication

Admin intervention is only needed for unrecoverable cases

5. Summary
Yes — local corruption can be safely flagged:

Use rename, placeholder, or touch depending on recovery strategy

Keep DAG/symlinks intact

Monitoring alerts sysadmins

csync2 automatically propagates verified content on next sync

Workflow fits your existing operational model perfectly

This preserves correctness while keeping your distributed system simple and reliable.


But that mv would be detected by csync2 and delete the intact copy on other nodes.

Exactly — a simple mv of the local file would make csync2 think the file was deleted locally, and propagate that deletion to other nodes, which is dangerous. You need a way to flag corruption without breaking the DAG or triggering destructive syncs.

1. Safe ways to mark corruption for csync2
A. Leave the filename intact and mark via metadata
Add a small sidecar file or extended attribute:

/xx/yy/<sha>.type.corrupt = 1
Or set an xattr on the file:

setfattr -n user.corrupt -v 1 /xx/yy/<sha>.type
Benefits:

File still exists → csync2 sees no deletion

FSCK or runtime can detect the flag

Admin workflow can react

B. Rename within a hidden subdirectory (not in synced path)
Move corrupted file to a directory outside csync2’s watched tree:

/corrupt/<sha>.type
Leave the original path empty (or put a placeholder deferred file)

Benefits:

csync2 sees the placeholder → triggers propagation of verified copy

Corrupted binary is preserved for investigation

Requirements:

The placeholder must exist at the original path to prevent csync2 deletion

C. Overwrite with a placeholder or zero-length file
Replace the corrupted content with a marker:

echo "<<<deferred>>>" > /xx/yy/<sha>.type
csync2 sees this as “modified” and propagates from the verified node

Benefits:

No accidental deletion elsewhere

Symlinks still valid

Optional: keep the original corrupted binary in a .corrupt directory for inspection

2. Recommended workflow
For your system:

FSCK detects a corruption.

If reproducible derived:

Delete binary; leave symlink → will regenerate on demand.

If non-reproducible derived or original:

Overwrite with placeholder or deferred marker

Store corrupted copy in /corrupt/ (outside csync2 path)

Alert monitoring / sysadmin

csync2 next sync propagates the correct copy from another node

This way:

No deletion propagates accidentally

DAG and symlinks remain intact

Integrity is restored automatically or via admin intervention

3. Key principle
Never remove or rename a file within the csync2 watched path when it is still considered authoritative elsewhere.
Always signal corruption without breaking the filename or symlink.

This preserves both distributed consistency and recoverability.

If you want, I can sketch a minimal safe FSCK repair algorithm that flags corrupted files without risking destructive csync2 propagation.


Hmmm.  Could have a per-node corrupt status.  Csync2 would sync the corrupt flags.  Then during their daily fsck could push to other corrupted nodes automatically.

Node with corrupted flag could redirect to other node automatically, such as cdn2.example.com.  Cdn2 would both serve the request and notice the corruption flag and resync.

Direct node requests would be rare, but possible.

Corruption redirects could pass list of nodes where corrupted.  Possible multiple corruptions.  If all nodes in list, fail nowhere else to try.

Yes — that model is both safe and fully compatible with your multi-master CDN setup. It decouples corruption tracking from file presence while allowing automatic recovery via other nodes.

1. Per-node corruption flags
Each node maintains a corruption status map, e.g.:

/xx/yy/<sha>.type.corrupt = true
csync2 propagates these flags like any other metadata file.

Key properties:

Original file remains in place → csync2 never deletes the authoritative copy elsewhere.

Nodes can independently detect corruption.

Flags can include timestamp, source node, etc.

2. Automatic redirection
When a client requests a corrupted resource:

Node sees local .corrupt flag.

Respond with HTTP 302/307 to another node that has an uncorrupted copy:

e.g., cdn2.example.com/xx/yy/<sha>.type

Optionally, include list of nodes with corruption:

Helps the client or monitoring system avoid nodes with bad data.

Example response header
X-Corrupt-Nodes: cdn1.example.com, cdn3.example.com
Location: https://cdn2.example.com/xx/yy/<sha>.type
Direct node requests are rare but possible.

If all known nodes are flagged corrupted → return HTTP 503 / unavailable.

3. Recovery and resync
Node receiving redirected request notices corruption on the original node via flags.

Node can automatically pull verified content from another node (or regenerate if derived and reproducible).

After successful recovery:

Clear local .corrupt flag

csync2 propagates cleared flag → all nodes converge

4. Advantages
Safe: no destructive deletion of authoritative content.

Self-healing: corrupted nodes can repair themselves on-demand or during daily FSCK.

Transparent to clients: requests are redirected automatically.

Monitoring-friendly: corruption flags are explicit and can be aggregated.

Handles multi-node corruption: list of corrupt nodes allows intelligent fallback.

5. Operational notes
Keep corruption flags lightweight; could be empty files or small JSON metadata.

CSYNC2 will propagate changes efficiently if flags are in the watched tree.

FSCK job can:

Detect local corruption

Flag the node

Optionally trigger regeneration or fetch from a healthy node

Admins are alerted only for unrecoverable corruption (all nodes corrupted).

6. Edge cases
Multiple corrupted nodes: client may iterate through fallback nodes, or system can pick a random healthy node.

Reproducible derived resources: corrupted nodes can regenerate on-demand instead of fetching.

Non-reproducible originals: rely on other healthy nodes for restoration.

This design essentially creates a distributed corruption-awareness layer on top of your existing SHA+symlink DAG, keeping your CDN nodes stateless with respect to original content but self-healing under rare corruption events.

If you want, I can outline a flow diagram for corruption handling showing request path, flag propagation, and recovery.

You’re essentially building the foundation now for a future automated self-healing CDN, while keeping production safe with manual admin intervention. This is a standard staged approach for distributed content systems.


Concurrent derivation is likely since all clients switch to new auction lot at the same time.

Yes — with multiple clients hitting the same auction lot simultaneously, you’re guaranteed to see high concurrency on derivation, especially for reproducible resources like scaled images.

1. Why this is safe in your current design
SHA-identity ensures deterministic output

Multiple nodes deriving the same resource simultaneously produce identical SHA

No race conditions on final content, as long as writes are atomic

Symlinks remain valid

Parent → derived symlink can point to <<<deferred>>> until materialization completes

CSYNC2 syncs after the fact

Only propagates the completed, identical file

No coordination between nodes is required

2. Potential issues
Partial writes / temporary files

Node may crash mid-derivation → corrupted file

Solution: write to temporary path, then rename atomically to final SHA

This ensures other nodes never see incomplete files

Concurrent fsync/load

Multiple threads may try to materialize the same derived resource

Solution: per-node locking (mutex or file lock) prevents redundant work locally

Cross-node duplication is harmless because SHA is identical

Non-reproducible derivations

Rare, but concurrent derivation may generate conflicting SHA

Solution: log conflict; manual or automated resolution depending on your policy

3. Optimizations for high-concurrency events
Defer materialization initially

Keep symlinks pointing to <<<deferred>>> until first access

Avoid pre-computing all derived versions at auction start

Local per-SHA locking

Prevent redundant derivation work per node

Reduces CPU and I/O spikes

Batch fsck / cleanup runs

Avoid full cleanup or regeneration during peak traffic

Cache hot derived resources

If a resource is likely requested by all clients at once, pre-generate on one node and allow csync2 to propagate

4. Behavior under extreme concurrency
All nodes might attempt the same derivation simultaneously

Outcome:

Correct final SHA always results

Temporary extra CPU usage per node

No data corruption or DAG inconsistencies

FSCK / monitoring can detect and handle rare partial files

5. Summary
Concurrent derivation at lot change is expected and safe in your architecture because:

Deterministic SHA → identical content

Atomic writes → prevent partial corruption

Local locking → prevents wasted work

csync2 → propagates final content to all nodes

The main operational concern is CPU/memory load during peak derivation, not correctness.


Do we support multiple derivations when not reproducible?  If so, each derivation could create a unique symlink, possibly based of the materialized sha

Yes — your system can support multiple non-reproducible derivations, but it requires a slightly different approach than reproducible ones because the output SHA is not deterministic.

1. Key principle
Non-reproducible derivations → same parent + derivation may produce different SHA on each materialization.

Implication: You cannot reuse a previously materialized SHA unless you explicitly match content after the fact.

Therefore:

Each materialized output must have a unique identity in the DAG.

Symlinks must point to the specific SHA of that instance.

2. Symlink strategy
Option A: SHA-based unique symlink
After derivation, SHA is computed: <derived-sha>.type

Create symlink from parent + derivation path to this SHA:

parent-sha.type / derivation-name-<instance-id> → <derived-sha>.type
<instance-id> can be:

timestamp

incremental counter

truncated SHA of derived content

This ensures:

Each non-reproducible instance is uniquely addressable

Original parent remains unchanged

Multiple derivations coexist without collision

Option B: Materialization-time UUID
Generate a UUID for each materialization

Symlink:

parent-sha.type / derivation-uuid → <derived-sha>.type
SHA still identifies content for storage, but symlink namespace handles multiple instances

Allows arbitrary repeated derivations without overwriting anything

3. Implications for CDN and fsck
FSCK / monitoring:

Non-reproducible derived content may have multiple “children” per derivation type

Symlink traversal must check each child separately

No automatic regeneration — each is unique

CDN nodes:

Multiple nodes may materialize the same derivation simultaneously → new SHA each time

Safe as long as atomic write + unique symlink is used

Conflicts logged for monitoring

4. Cleanup considerations
Non-reproducible derived resources can be deleted when:

No parent references them (orphans)

Optional TTL or admin policy

Deletion removes only that instance

Symlinks from parent must be cleared or updated to indicate missing content

5. Summary
For non-reproducible derivations:

Each derivation instance gets a unique SHA

Each instance gets a unique symlink (parent → instance)

No reuse assumption — content is distinct

FSCK/cleanup must handle multiple children per parent

Optional metadata can store derivation parameters for tracking

This approach safely allows multiple derivations from the same parent while keeping DAG semantics clear and avoiding accidental overwrites.

If you want, I can sketch a naming scheme for symlinks and derived SHA files that works for both reproducible and non-reproducible derivations.


What if we never assume reproducibility?  Some things considered reproducible could change with JDK version, Unicode standards, over long time periods.

If you never assume reproducibility, your system needs to treat all derived resources as potentially unique, even if the transformation appears deterministic today. This fundamentally changes the DAG, symlink, and cleanup semantics, but it is safer for long-term correctness.

1. Core principle
Every derivation, no matter how “deterministic,” produces a new SHA.

You cannot reuse previously materialized SHA based solely on parent + derivation type.

The DAG must allow multiple derived nodes per parent for the same derivation type.

2. Symlink strategy
Use a unique symlink per materialization:

parent-sha.type / derivation-name-<instance-id> → <derived-sha>.type
Where <instance-id> could be:

Timestamp

Incremental counter

Short hash of derived content

Metadata can store:

Parent SHA

Derivation type

Derivation parameters

Materialization timestamp

This ensures:

Multiple derivations coexist

DAG is explicit

No accidental overwrites

3. Implications for CDN and FSCK
Materialization

Every request for a derivation may produce a new SHA.

Symlinks point to that specific SHA.

FSCK / Monitoring

Cannot assume a missing derived resource can be regenerated identically.

Orphan detection: only remove derived resources if no symlinks and no DB references.

Metadata is critical to track derivation lineage.

Cache / CDN

Nodes may generate different SHA from the same derivation independently.

Safe because:

Each derived SHA is unique

No conflicts occur

Optional: sync metadata only to track which derivations exist.

4. Cleanup and pruning
Orphan detection must be based on symlink + DB references, not reproducibility.

Derived resources can be removed when:

No parents reference them

Optional TTL policy

There is no regeneration assumption, so deleting a derived resource means that future requests may produce another, potentially different SHA.

5. Operational consequences
Pros

Correctness over long time scales (JDK updates, Unicode changes, library updates)

Fully deterministic SHA-content mapping

Avoids accidental corruption due to subtle differences in supposedly deterministic derivations

Cons

Higher storage usage (multiple “copies” of the same derivation over time)

Cannot rely on reuse of previously generated derived files

FSCK and cleanup logic is more complex (must track all instances, not just one per derivation type)

6. Summary
If you never assume reproducibility:

Treat every derivation as producing new unique content (new SHA).

Always create a unique symlink per materialization.

DAG becomes one-to-many: parent → multiple derived SHAs.

Cleanup must rely on explicit references, not ability to regenerate.

FSCK verifies SHA matches content and DAG integrity, but cannot regenerate missing derived files.

This is the most robust long-term model for a universal content-addressable store where transformation behavior may evolve over time.

1. Principle
Do not rely on a separate meta file for creation time for derived resources.

Metadata can still store derivation type, parent SHA, and last-accessed, but creation time is read-only from filesystem.

mv on the same filesystem preserves atomicity

The creation timestamp of /xx/yy/<sha>.type is now reliable

B. Access / cleanup
Last-accessed can be tracked via:

Filesystem atime (if reliable)

Or separate last-accessed metadata file (not used for creation)

Cleanup logic compares atime or explicit last-accessed with policy

Creation time is always filesystem’s ctime or birthtime → prevents concurrent metadata mismatch

No risk of meta file race corrupting creation time

Works across multiple nodes materializing the same SHA independently

Compatible with your multi-node csync2 system

Derivation parameters

Last-accessed timestamps

Pinning flags

Never store creation time in metadata; always read from filesystem

Summary:

This avoids race conditions and keeps your DAG and cleanup logic reliable.


To reduce chances of concurrent cleanuo issues, cdn nodes could stagger their fsck: if two nodes, 12 hours apart, manual scheduling via per-node cron-like scheduler.

Yes — staggering FSCK and cleanup across CDN nodes is a simple and effective way to avoid concurrent deletion conflicts.

1. Why staggering helps
Each node runs its own FSCK + cleanup process independently.

If FSCK runs at the exact same time on multiple nodes:

Both may detect “orphaned” derived resources

Both attempt deletion or cleanup simultaneously → minor race or redundant work

Staggering ensures:

One node cleans first

Other node sees the result (via csync2) or skips the already-cleaned resource

Reduces I/O spikes and contention

2. Implementation strategy
Per-node cron or scheduler

Node A: FSCK at 02:00

Node B: FSCK at 14:00

Longer intervals possible if many nodes

Randomized jitter

Add ±5–10 min to avoid exact alignment after restarts

Optional lockfile per node

Ensure FSCK does not overlap with itself if restarted

Example: /var/run/fsck.lock

3. Cleanup considerations
Derived resources flagged for deletion should:

Be safe to delete independently (no other parent references OR properly tracked)

Only delete if unpinned and last-accessed threshold exceeded

Staggering reduces the chance that two nodes try to delete the same derived SHA simultaneously, even if it’s unreferenced

4. Additional benefits
I/O load smoothing: nodes don’t all scan millions of files at once

Predictable operation: easier to monitor and correlate FSCK/cleanup logs

Safe for multi-master CDN with csync2: csync2 sees deletions propagated naturally after the first node cleans

Summary:

Staggered FSCK schedules per node (e.g., 12 hours apart for 2 nodes) reduce concurrent cleanup conflicts, smooth I/O load, and work well with your distributed SHA+symlink DAG and csync2 propagation.

This is a low-complexity, robust operational design for multi-node environments.

Concurrency cinsiderations in locking, each resource accessed highly concurrently.

When each resource (SHA file or derived resource) is highly concurrently accessed, you need a locking strategy that ensures correctness without introducing excessive contention or blocking. Here’s a robust approach for your system:

1. Lock granularity
Per-resource (per-SHA) locks are ideal:

Each SHA gets its own lock file:

/locks/<sha>.lock
Only threads/nodes materializing or modifying that specific SHA block each other.

Avoid coarse locks (e.g., per-directory or global), which create bottlenecks under high concurrency.

2. Lock types and usage
Exclusive lock (write lock)

Needed when:

Materializing a new derived resource (writing temp → final)

Cleaning up orphaned temp files

Updating metadata that affects the SHA directly (e.g., pin/unpin)

Implementation: flock or fcntl advisory lock on /locks/<sha>.lock

Shared lock (optional read lock)

Needed when:

Reading metadata or last-accessed without modifying file

Optional if FSCK or cleanup only reads DAG

Reduces contention for read-heavy access

3. Atomic operations under lock
Materialization workflow (highly concurrent):

Acquire exclusive lock for SHA

Delete any leftover temp files (*.tmp)

Materialize to temp file

mv temp → final SHA

Release lock

Observation: multiple threads/nodes can attempt materialization concurrently, but only one succeeds at a time, preventing partial writes or duplicate updates.

4. Metadata handling
Metadata (pinned, last-accessed) should also be updated under the same per-SHA lock to prevent races.

If last-accessed is frequently updated, consider:

Atomic append/log file per SHA for high-frequency access

Or in-memory cache with periodic flush to FS

Avoid separate global metadata locks, which become a hotspot.

5. FSCK and cleanup coordination
FSCK acquires same per-SHA locks before touching a SHA:

Safely deletes temp files

Verifies SHA content

Updates metadata

With staggered FSCK (per-node cron), contention is minimal.

6. High concurrency considerations
Lock contention is proportional to:

Number of simultaneous requests for the same SHA

Duration of the critical section (materialization + rename)

Optimizations:

Minimize work inside the lock (e.g., materialize outside lock → rename under lock)

Use per-node caching for frequent reads to avoid repeated lock acquisition

Batch cleanup operations to reduce lock churn

7. Summary guidelines
Per-SHA lock only; no global or per-directory locks.

Exclusive lock for materialization, cleanup, metadata updates.

Lock only long enough for atomic operations (rename, metadata update).

FSCK uses same locks for safety, staggered across nodes.

Optional read-shared locks if metadata access is very frequent.

Minimize lock contention by caching frequent reads and deferring expensive work outside the critical section.

This design allows hundreds or thousands of clients to access resources concurrently while ensuring:

No partial or corrupted files

No symlink/DAG inconsistencies

Safe multi-node FSCK and cleanup

It fits your SHA-based universal store with deferred derivation and multi-master webapp/CDN architecture.

1. Handling offline nodes
csync2 guarantees eventual consistency:

Files marked dirty are synchronized once the node comes online.

No need for immediate blocking; background batching handles retries automatically.

Materialization workflow is unchanged:

Atomic rename + dirty mark happens on local node.

Response to client is returned immediately.

Offline nodes will pull the update once they reconnect.

Derived resource is materialized locally and symlink updated immediately.

Other nodes may not see it yet:

If a request arrives on a node that is still offline, you may see a deferred or missing derived SHA.

Only operate on files locally present.

Do not assume that missing derived SHAs on one node are truly orphaned.

4. Concurrency + offline nodes
Multiple nodes can independently materialize the same derived resource while another node is offline:

SHA ensures content-addressed storage avoids duplication conflicts.

Each node marks its local copy dirty; csync2 merges changes when offline node returns.

Atomic rename guarantees full content visibility locally.

SHA identity ensures identical content is deduplicated automatically.

Always mark file dirty locally immediately after materialization.

Background batching for csync2 handles upload asynchronously.

FSCK must be aware of multi-node replication:

Only clean up unreferenced, fully synchronized files

Optionally skip cleanup for nodes recently offline

Summary:

FSCK and cleanup should consider partial replication and avoid deleting files that may still need to sync. This approach keeps your high-concurrency, multi-node, deferred derivation system consistent and safe even with nodes intermittently offline.


How can replace symlink atomically?

On a POSIX filesystem, symlink replacement is inherently atomic if you use the rename() system call (or equivalent). You do not modify the symlink in place; instead, you create a new symlink and atomically swap it over the old one. Here’s the pattern:

1. Atomic symlink replacement workflow
Create a temporary symlink pointing to the new target:

ln -s <new-target-sha>.type /xx/yy/<symlink>.tmp
Rename it over the existing symlink:

mv -T /xx/yy/<symlink>.tmp /xx/yy/<symlink>
mv -T ensures the rename replaces the existing symlink atomically.

POSIX guarantees rename() is atomic on the same filesystem.

Optional lock (per-SHA or per-parent):

Acquire lock before rename if multiple threads/nodes could update the same symlink concurrently.

2. Why this is safe
Readers of the symlink either see:

The old target, or

The new target

There is no intermediate state where the symlink is partially written.

Even concurrent readers during rename are safe: OS guarantees atomic pointer swap.

3. Integration with your DAG
Per-parent symlink pointing to derived SHA:

parent-sha.type / derivation-name → <derived-sha>.type
To update to a newer derived SHA:

Create derivation-name.tmp → new SHA

mv -T derivation-name.tmp derivation-name atomically

Ensures latest-first serving without race conditions.

4. Additional notes
Temporary symlink (*.tmp) should be excluded from csync2 and FSCK, like temp files for materialization.

Always rename on the same filesystem; cross-filesystem rename is not atomic.

Locks are only needed if multiple threads/nodes might try to update the same symlink at the same time, otherwise atomic rename is sufficient.

Summary:

To replace a symlink atomically: create a temporary symlink pointing to the new target, then use rename() (or mv -T) to overwrite the existing symlink. Readers see either the old or new target, never a partial state. This integrates safely with your DAG and high-concurrency materialization model.

If you want, I can sketch the full atomic update flow for derived SHA + symlink replacement, including locks, background csync2, and FSCK integration.


Can read locks be upgraded to write locks?  Start read, with concurrency, thej upgrade to exclusive when a write will need to be performed.  Once obtain write, verify conditions have not changed?  Deadlock possible?

In the fast path, everything already materialized, the lock can be held short-term, just long enough to get open file handle.

Yes — what you describe is called lock upgrade (shared → exclusive). On POSIX systems with advisory locks (like flock or fcntl), it’s possible, but you have to be very careful because deadlocks can occur. Let’s go through the details.

1. Read → Write Lock Upgrade
Start with shared/read lock to allow many readers concurrently.

When a write becomes necessary:

Attempt to upgrade to exclusive/write lock.

Once write lock is acquired:

Verify conditions haven’t changed (e.g., SHA file still missing or temp file still valid)

Perform write operation (materialization, rename, metadata update)

Release lock afterward.

2. Deadlock possibility
If multiple threads or nodes try to upgrade simultaneously, deadlock can occur:

Thread A holds read lock, tries to upgrade.

Thread B holds read lock on the same resource, also tries to upgrade.

Both wait for the other to release → deadlock.

Mitigation:

Prefer release read lock before acquiring write lock, then re-check state.

Avoid holding read locks while waiting for upgrade.

3. Recommended upgrade pattern
Acquire shared/read lock

Check conditions:

If no write needed → continue read path, release lock normally

If write needed → release read lock

Acquire exclusive/write lock

Re-check conditions

Example: SHA file may have been materialized while upgrading lock

Perform write if still necessary

Release exclusive lock

This is safe and avoids deadlock while maintaining concurrency on the fast path.

4. Fast-path optimization
If everything is already materialized:

Hold the lock very briefly just to:

Open the file handle

Possibly update last-accessed metadata

No need to upgrade at all in this case

Lock contention is minimal because critical section is tiny

5. Additional considerations
Advisory locks are per-process, so multiple threads in the same process may require separate coordination.

Multi-node locks (across NFS/shared FS) may be more complex; advisory locks rely on the OS, and not all NFS implementations are reliable. Your POSIX assumption is good for local FS.

Always re-check conditions after acquiring write lock — necessary for correctness under concurrency.

Summary:

Lock upgrade from shared to exclusive is possible but dangerous if done directly due to deadlock risk. Safe pattern: release read lock, acquire write lock, then re-check conditions. For fast-path access, hold locks only briefly to open the file or update metadata. This maximizes concurrency while ensuring correctness in highly concurrent materialization scenarios.

If you want, I can draw a concurrency diagram showing read-lock fast path vs. write-upgrade path for materialization + FSCK + symlink updates.


Last-accessed meta data allowed to be updated with read lock?

Yes — last-accessed metadata can be updated under a read/shared lock, provided that the update itself is atomic and does not change critical state.

1. Why it’s safe
Last-accessed is non-critical, mutable metadata.

You’re not changing the content of the file or the DAG structure, only recording a timestamp.

Multiple readers can concurrently update it without interfering with each other.

2. Atomic update methods
File-based sidecar metadata:

Update last-accessed timestamp in a single write (O_WRONLY | O_TRUNC), then fsync() if needed.

Use append or atomic write-rename if multiple threads may race.

File xattr (extended attribute):

setxattr() is atomic at OS level

Multiple readers updating last-accessed concurrently is safe

In-memory cache with periodic flush:

Each reader updates its own local value

Flush to FS asynchronously or in batch

Reduces contention on high-frequency reads

3. Why exclusive lock is unnecessary
Last-accessed does not affect correctness of DAG, SHA, symlink, or derivation logic.

Exclusive locks should be reserved for:

Materialization (write to SHA/temp file)

Symlink replacement

Metadata that affects content addressability (pinned flag, derivation type)

4. Concurrency tips
If very high concurrency, consider atomic append or incrementing counters to avoid lost updates.

For last-accessed timestamps, minor race (one update overwriting another) is usually acceptable, since the value is only for cleanup heuristics.

Summary:

Updating last-accessed metadata under a read/shared lock is safe. Exclusive locks are unnecessary because last-accessed does not affect file content or DAG consistency. Use atomic writes, xattrs, or in-memory batching to handle high concurrency efficiently.

This allows fast-path reads without blocking while keeping cleanup policies accurate.


If multiple may write due to non-exclusive lock, need randomized tmp filenames, too.  Cleanup under exclusive lock would still delete the tmp files.

Exactly — if multiple threads or nodes can write while only holding a shared/read lock, randomized temp filenames are required to avoid collisions. Here’s the proper pattern:

1. Randomized temp filenames
Instead of <sha>.tmp, generate a unique temporary file per attempt:

<sha>.tmp.<random-id>
Random ID options:

Process ID + thread ID + timestamp

Secure random string (e.g., 6–8 chars)

Guarantees no two writers overwrite each other’s temp files.

2. Materialization workflow under shared lock
Acquire shared/read lock (optional if reading metadata)

Determine that a write is necessary (e.g., derived resource missing)

Generate randomized temp file:

/xx/yy/<sha>.tmp.<random-id>
Materialize content into temp file

Acquire exclusive lock to:

Verify conditions have not changed

Atomically rename temp → final SHA

Release lock

Background csync2 batch marks file dirty

3. Cleanup workflow
FSCK or cleanup process acquires exclusive lock per SHA or derived resource:

Deletes any orphaned temp files:

/xx/yy/<sha>.tmp.*
Safe even if multiple temp files exist from concurrent writers

Ensures no leftover temp files cause storage bloat or conflicts

4. Advantages
Supports highly concurrent materialization without collisions

Randomized temp files allow multiple threads/nodes to write simultaneously under non-exclusive locks

Exclusive lock during rename ensures atomic final placement

Cleanup safely removes all leftover temp files without affecting the final SHA

5. Summary
When multiple writers may operate under a non-exclusive lock, generate randomized temp filenames for each attempt. Materialize content to the unique temp, then acquire exclusive lock to verify conditions and atomically rename to final SHA. FSCK or cleanup under exclusive lock can safely delete any leftover temp files matching the randomized pattern.

This guarantees atomicity, concurrency safety, and cleanup correctness in your SHA-based, multi-node derived resource system.


Synchronous mark dirty under exclusive lock.  Background and batched sync/upload.  Complicated solution could coalesce concurrent synchronous calls to csync2 for setting dirty.

Yes — that’s a very solid pattern. Here’s a breakdown of how to implement it efficiently and safely:

1. Synchronous dirty marking
Mark file dirty while holding the exclusive lock, immediately after atomic rename of the temp file.

This ensures:

The filesystem sees the file as updated before release.

Any concurrent processes reading the file will see a consistent state.

The call is synchronous but very fast, just a metadata update for csync2.

2. Background batched sync/upload
After releasing the lock, enqueue the SHA in a background batching thread:

Queue multiple newly materialized files

Process in batches (e.g., 50–500 files)

Invoke csync2 once per batch

Optional low initial delay (a few ms) allows coalescing multiple rapid materializations.

3. Coalescing concurrent dirty marks
If multiple threads/materializations happen near-simultaneously:

They all attempt to mark dirty synchronously under their respective locks.

Since dirty marking is idempotent, coalescing is safe:

Only one dirty mark is needed for csync2 to detect the file as changed.

Background batching ensures csync2 only uploads once per SHA even if multiple threads mark dirty.

4. Workflow diagram (logical)
Thread 1/2/…/N:
   [Acquire lock] → check SHA → materialize temp → mv temp → mark dirty (synchronous)
   [Release lock]
   → Enqueue SHA to background batch queue

Background thread:
   → Wait for batch interval / batch size
   → Call csync2 for all queued SHAs
   → Clear queue
5. Advantages
Low-latency client response: atomic rename + synchronous dirty mark is fast.

Safe for multi-node multi-master: csync2 sees all changed SHAs.

Efficient: batching reduces csync2 invocation overhead.

Idempotent: repeated synchronous dirty calls do not break correctness.

Supports high concurrency: multiple writers can safely mark dirty without conflicts.

6. Optional optimizations
Deduplicate SHA entries in queue before batch upload → avoids unnecessary csync2 calls.

Prioritize hot SHAs: immediate sync for critical derived resources, batch the rest.

Monitor queue length and batch duration → detect bottlenecks under high load.

Summary:

Hold exclusive lock to perform synchronous csync2 “mark dirty” immediately after atomic rename. Release lock quickly. Background thread batches all dirty SHAs and performs csync2 uploads asynchronously. Concurrent synchronous marks are safe and can be coalesced for efficiency.

This approach balances correctness, concurrency, and performance in your SHA-based multi-node derived resource system.


At risk of losing dirty state until next full csync verification, could offload setting dirty to a separate thread, and uoloading to yet a different thread.

Yes — you can decouple dirty marking from actual csync2 upload by using separate threads. This reduces client latency and avoids blocking on filesystem or network operations, while slightly increasing the window where a node could temporarily “forget” a newly materialized file is dirty. Here’s a safe approach:

1. Separate threads
Dirty-marking thread

Runs immediately after atomic rename under exclusive lock

Marks SHA as dirty locally (csync2 metadata)

Fast, does not block materialization

Can enqueue SHA for background upload

Background upload thread

Collects SHAs from dirty-marking thread in a queue

Performs csync2 batch upload asynchronously

Can coalesce multiple entries per SHA to reduce redundant uploads

2. Risk window
Between atomic rename and the next dirty mark, a crash could cause csync2 to miss the file temporarily.

Mitigations:

Ensure dirty-marking thread runs immediately after rename

Periodic full csync2 verification will catch any missed SHAs

Optional: persist pending dirty SHAs in a small local journal file so a crash does not lose them

3. Benefits
Materialization critical path is very fast

Upload latency is decoupled from client request

High concurrency is safe: multiple threads enqueue to the background upload queue

System remains eventually consistent; full csync2 verification closes any gaps

4. Implementation notes
Use a thread-safe queue for communication between dirty-marking and upload threads

Background upload thread can batch SHAs, e.g., every few milliseconds or after N entries

Optional: track last successful upload per SHA to detect repeated retries or failures

Summary:

Offloading dirty marking and actual csync2 uploads to separate threads is safe and efficient. Dirty marking happens immediately after atomic rename, while uploads occur asynchronously in batches. Eventual consistency is ensured by periodic full csync2 verification, minimizing the risk of missed dirty SHAs.

This approach keeps fast-path client responses and high concurrency while preserving multi-node consistency.


We already have batching code for paths.  When multiple paths in one directory were affected, we pass entire directory for csync to process uploads.

Perfect — that fits very well with your multi-node, high-concurrency design. Using directory-level batching simplifies csync2 usage and reduces overhead. Here’s how to integrate it cleanly:

1. Directory-level batching workflow
During materialization:

Track all affected paths within the directory in a thread-safe set

Can be multiple SHAs or derived resources in the same directory

Dirty marking:

Synchronous under exclusive lock:

Mark each file dirty for csync2, or mark the directory itself as needing sync

Still fast because it’s only metadata updates

Background upload thread:

Every batch interval (or when set reaches N entries), call csync2 on the directory instead of individual files

Ensures all recently materialized or modified SHAs are uploaded together

2. Advantages of directory-level batching
Fewer csync2 calls → less process overhead

Coalesces multiple files in high-concurrency scenarios

Simpler queue management:

Only need one entry per directory, regardless of how many files inside changed

Safe for offline nodes:

csync2 handles eventual propagation of all files in the directory

3. Concurrency considerations
Multiple threads materializing different SHAs in the same directory:

Each marks its file dirty synchronously under its lock

Add the directory to the thread-safe background batch set

Background thread performs one csync2 per directory, covering all changed files

Cleanup or FSCK:

Still acquire exclusive lock per SHA for temp cleanup or verification

Directory-level batching doesn’t interfere with atomic file operations

4. Integration with your existing batching
If you already have batching code for paths, modify it to accept a directory-level coalescing layer:

Collect changed directories in a set

Background uploader dequeues one directory at a time

Calls csync2 on that directory

Removes directory from the queue after upload completes

5. Summary
Using directory-level batching for csync2 uploads allows multiple materialized SHAs in the same directory to be synchronized in one call. Each file is still marked dirty synchronously under its exclusive lock, but the upload can be batched in the background. This reduces csync2 overhead, coalesces high-concurrency updates, and works safely with FSCK, temp file cleanup, and offline nodes.

This complements your atomic materialization + randomized temp files + per-SHA locking workflow perfectly.


Would be smart to have a sync call for the image upload component.  It would at least wait for dirty to be set, and possibly wait for an attempt to sync all nodes, reporting warning of any nodes timed out or errored.  Maybe not throw exception, but return a per-node status and possible related throwable

Exactly — providing a synchronous “upload sync” API from the image upload component gives clients confidence that a newly materialized or uploaded SHA is fully propagated (or at least attempted) across the CDN nodes, without blocking the fast-path materialization. Here’s a structured approach:

1. Purpose of a sync call
Wait for dirty marking to complete locally

Trigger or wait for background sync attempt for affected directories/nodes

Return per-node propagation status:

SUCCESS → node confirmed synced

TIMEOUT → node didn’t respond in time

ERROR → node reported failure

Include optional exception or cause for errors

2. Workflow
Client uploads or materializes image (SHA file) → atomic rename + mark dirty

API call syncSha(sha) does:

syncSha(sha):
    wait for local dirty mark to complete
    enqueue SHA/directory for background csync2 if not already enqueued
    optionally trigger immediate csync2 attempt (blocking)
    for each node in cluster:
        wait for csync2 to report success, timeout, or error
    return per-node status map (node -> result + optional throwable)
Optional timeout for waiting per node

API does not throw exception on failed nodes, but reports them to client

3. Implementation notes
Use thread-safe tracking structures:

Map SHA → dirty-marked flag

Map SHA → per-node last sync attempt

Background uploader continues batching as usual; syncSha can either:

Block until background thread finishes batch, or

Trigger a one-off immediate csync2 for the directory containing the SHA

Allows clients to:

Verify propagation before proceeding

Log or warn about nodes offline or slow

4. Error handling philosophy
Do not throw exceptions for transient sync failures:

Nodes may be temporarily offline

Eventual consistency will eventually propagate files

Return structured status to caller for monitoring/alerting

Persistent failures can be surfaced via admin monitoring or logs

5. Advantages
Provides stronger operational visibility for critical uploads

Integrates with high-concurrency batching:

Fast-path materialization is still non-blocking

Optional blocking only when the client explicitly wants sync confirmation

Compatible with offline nodes, background batching, and multi-master design

Summary:

Implement a syncSha API that waits for local dirty mark, optionally triggers immediate batch sync, and returns per-node status with errors or timeouts. Do not throw exceptions; report failures to client for monitoring. This gives controlled consistency guarantees without blocking the fast path or breaking concurrency design.

This fits seamlessly with your atomic rename + temp files + per-SHA locking + csync2 batching + high-concurrency materialization workflow.


If sync called after upload, is possible paths already through dirty and upload threads.  If not found, just status "Nothing To Do".  Or, have a synchronous flag on the upload itself, which would directly mark dirty and upload, or wait until other threads complete the affects paths?

Yes — both approaches are feasible, and the choice depends on client expectations vs. throughput optimization. Here’s a breakdown:

1. Separate sync call after upload
Workflow:

Client uploads or materializes SHA.

Fast-path completes: atomic rename + async dirty mark + background batched upload.

Later, client calls syncSha(sha):

If SHA already queued and dirty/upload thread is running → wait or check status.

If SHA already fully uploaded → return SUCCESS.

If SHA not found (e.g., no dirty mark needed, already exists) → return "Nothing To Do".

Pros:

Fast initial upload, minimal blocking.

Simple client logic: optional “confirm propagation”.

Cons:

Client may need two calls: upload + sync.

2. Synchronous flag on upload
Workflow:

Client calls uploadSha(sha, sync=true).

During upload:

Atomic rename happens.

Acquire exclusive lock.

Synchronously mark dirty.

Optionally wait for background uploader to finish affected paths.

Return per-node status after actual upload attempt.

Pros:

Single API call for client.

Guarantees that when API returns, SHA is at least attempted to be uploaded to nodes.

Cons:

Blocking call; may increase latency.

Must coordinate with background threads to avoid race conditions.

3. Implementation options for synchronous flag
Option A: Immediate synchronous upload

Upload thread temporarily bypasses batching and performs csync2 immediately for affected paths.

Simple, but may impact throughput if many concurrent uploads.

Option B: Wait for background thread

Mark SHA dirty synchronously.

Wait until the background uploader finishes processing the relevant directory.

Requires thread-safe coordination:

Track which SHA/directory is being processed.

Background uploader signals completion to waiting threads.

Maintains batching efficiency but adds complexity.

4. Safe concurrency considerations
Multiple threads might be materializing files in the same directory:

Only one thread needs to trigger upload; others can wait for result or skip.

Use per-directory or per-SHA tracking for:

Dirty-marked flag

Upload-in-progress flag

Completion notification for synchronous callers

5. Suggested design
Fast-path always asynchronous (atomic rename + dirty mark + background batch upload)

Optional sync=true flag on upload:

Immediately mark dirty synchronously

Wait for batch completion or immediate one-off upload

Return per-node status

syncSha API can be used independently later, returning status or "Nothing To Do".

This allows both lightweight fast-path uploads and strong synchronous consistency when required.

Fast-path async upload

Optional synchronous upload

Background batch thread

Handling multiple concurrent threads on the same directory

This makes the logic very clear for your high-concurrency CDN + derived resource system.

Http caching aggressive once resource materialized.  Likely ine year immutable, this timespan would match last-accessed auto cleanup, or possibly double the cache timespan before cleanup.

Yes — that fits perfectly with your content-addressed, immutable SHA-based design. Once a resource (original or derived) is materialized, you can treat it as effectively immutable for long-term HTTP caching. Here’s how to think about it:

1. HTTP caching strategy
Cache-Control headers for materialized SHAs:

Cache-Control: public, max-age=31536000, immutable
max-age=1 year or whatever matches your cleanup policy.

immutable signals that clients and CDNs don’t need to revalidate.

ETag / Last-Modified:

Optional but usually redundant because SHA-based URL is inherently unique per content.

Can help clients validate if ever needed, but usually unnecessary.

2. Interaction with last-accessed cleanup
Last-accessed metadata is used for automated cleanup of derived resources.

Since HTTP caching can be longer than cleanup, you could:

Allow last-accessed auto-cleanup to use a slightly longer window than the cache duration (e.g., 2× max-age)

Ensures that if a cached client request arrives, the resource still exists.

3. Benefits
Clients and CDN nodes don’t repeatedly request immutable content.

Reduced bandwidth and CPU usage for repeated GETs.

Cleanup process can remove older derived resources without worrying about invalidating client caches, since the SHA URL is permanent and unique.

Fits multi-node CSYNC2 batching model perfectly: once materialized, content can be aggressively cached anywhere.

4. Practical considerations
Derived resources:

Same caching rules as originals if SHA-based.

If non-reproducible derivations may change, do not set immutable — rely on versioned SHA URLs.

Deferred resources:

Before materialization, HTTP GET should return 302/301 redirect to deferred handler.

Cache for short duration (or no-cache) until materialized.

Summary:

Once a SHA-based resource is materialized, it can be aggressively cached by HTTP clients and CDNs for ~1 year using immutable headers. The last-accessed cleanup window can be slightly longer than cache duration to ensure that materialized resources remain available for any long-lived caches. Deferred or non-reproducible derivations require shorter caching.

This aligns HTTP caching, cleanup, and SHA-based immutability seamlessly for your high-concurrency CDN + derived resource system.

If we use Amazon S3, we'd still need to code integration, derivation, cleanup, ...

Exactly — moving storage to Amazon S3 wouldn’t eliminate most of the complexity:

Integration:

Your webapp still needs to store/retrieve by SHA, handle original vs. derived resources, and maintain the DAG/symlink logic (or equivalent in metadata).

Deferred derivation:

Materialization-on-demand still requires code to generate the derived resource and push it to S3.

Cleanup / lifecycle:

You’d need code to track last-accessed or reference counts to remove old derived resources from S3, possibly using S3 object lifecycle policies but still needing coordination with your DAG.

Concurrency / multi-node considerations:

Even with S3, multiple application nodes might try to materialize the same derived resource simultaneously — you’d still need locking or atomic creation patterns.

Monitoring / consistency:

Detecting missing or corrupted objects, re-uploading, and reporting errors would still require logic in your application.

Net effect: S3 mostly offloads storage and durability, but all orchestration, derivation logic, and consistency handling remains your responsibility. You just trade local disk complexity for cloud storage costs and S3 API usage.

So your current self-hosted plan avoids monthly storage costs while keeping full control, at the expense of maintaining the OSS components yourself.
