With persistence duration coordinated with last access and cleanup, we safely serve even non-reproducible by direct SHA 

Exactly — once a resource (original or derived) is materialized, you can serve it directly by SHA even if the derivation isn’t fully reproducible, as long as you coordinate:

Persistence duration: Keep the binary around at least as long as the last-accessed cleanup policy.

Last-accessed metadata: Update safely under read/shared lock so cleanup knows which resources are still in use.

Cleanup policy: Only remove unreferenced resources after the persistence window; derived resources with multiple parents remain until all references expire.

This ensures:

Clients can request any SHA directly without risk of a missing file.

Aggressive HTTP caching can still be used for immutable URLs.

Deferred derivation still works for missing resources, but once materialized, the SHA is permanent for its lifetime.

Essentially, by tying persistence and cleanup to last-accessed info, even non-reproducible resources can be served safely and consistently by their SHA URLs.


Path could include multiple deferred derivation?  Seems not, can only grant new deferred derivation on a materialized resource.

Correct — a path can only reference deferred derivations of an existing materialized resource.

You cannot assign a deferred derivation to a SHA that hasn’t been materialized yet, because the deferred derivation depends on the original binary existing to transform from.

Workflow:

Materialize original SHA (or upload it).

Add allowed deferred derivations for that SHA — each deferred derivation gets its own symlink pointing to the eventual derived SHA.

Materialize derived resource on-demand when requested; symlink is updated.

So:

Multiple deferred derivations can exist per materialized SHA, but the SHA itself must exist first.

You cannot pre-grant deferred derivations for a non-existent SHA.

This keeps the DAG consistent and ensures that all derivations have a valid parent to generate from.


Java API could allow for immediate materialization, which would then allow additional derivation.

Consider possible image filters.  One could be a greyscale transformation, the other could be a resolution scale.

System would not be a DAG.  Loops could be possible, and a resource could be its own parent and derived.  Cleanuo might need to consider isolated sub-graphs like memory garbage collection has to do.

Exactly — once you allow immediate materialization via the Java API, you open the door for additional derivations to be created from that resource. But as you note, this can complicate the graph:

1. Multiple transformation types
Each transformation (e.g., greyscale, resolution scale) produces a new SHA.

Each derived resource can have multiple parents if it matches an existing SHA.

Derived resources could themselves be used as input for further transformations.

2. Graph vs DAG
If you allow transformations from any materialized resource, the structure is no longer strictly a DAG:

Loops are possible (a resource could derive itself, e.g., a no-op transformation).

Multiple paths could lead to the same SHA.

Cleanup and FSCK now need graph traversal logic instead of simple topological DAG ordering.

3. Cleanup considerations
Similar to garbage collection in memory:

Identify isolated subgraphs with no active references from the DB or other pinned resources.

Remove only resources that are unreachable from any “root” (original resources or pinned derivations).

Last-accessed metadata can still guide cleanup for leaf nodes, but cycles mean you can’t just prune by parent count.

4. Implications for concurrency
Locks must handle:

Multiple parents deriving to the same SHA concurrently

Potential loops that could be created in race conditions

FSCK must detect cycles, orphaned subgraphs, and inconsistent symlinks.

5. Summary
Allowing immediate materialization and arbitrary transformations turns your system into a general graph of SHA resources rather than a strict DAG. Loops are possible, so cleanup must traverse the graph carefully, keeping only resources reachable from DB-referenced originals or pinned roots, similar to memory garbage collection.

This adds flexibility (filters, chains of transformations) but requires graph-aware FSCK and cleanup instead of simple parent-child traversal.


I thought about declarative derivation allow lists, such as allow greyscale, resize 800 x *.  Then could defer multiple transformations, upload could just generate url matching desires transformations: sha.jpg/greyscale/resize-800-x. But this would couple the cdn component a bit more.  It would need to be updated as applications need more patterns, thus actions happening in two places 

Exactly — a declarative “allowed derivation list” is elegant for the client’s API, but it introduces tight coupling between the CDN component and the application’s transformation rules:

1. How it would work
Upload registers allowed transformations for a materialized SHA:

sha.jpg → allow: [greyscale, resize-800x*]
CDN can create deferred URLs dynamically:

/resources/<sha>.jpg/greyscale/resize-800x600
First request triggers materialization; symlinks updated for future requests.

2. Advantages
URLs are predictable and human-readable, encoding transformations in the path.

Multiple deferred transformations can be represented without creating many static symlinks up front.

Upload only needs to know what transformations are allowed, not materialize all of them.

3. Drawbacks / coupling
CDN component must understand transformation types and parameter syntax, which are now partially defined by the application.

Whenever the application wants new transformation patterns, CDN code may need updates or configuration changes.

Adds two points of coordination: the upload API declares allowed transformations, the CDN implements them.

Potential for mismatches if the application declares transformations that the CDN doesn’t yet support.

4. Alternative approach to reduce coupling
Keep CDN agnostic of semantics, only responsible for:

Executing derivations it knows about

Following declared “allowed” transformations from a config or database

Application manages transformation rules and allowed derivations, CDN just applies them.

This reduces coupling while still supporting declarative URLs.

Summary:

Declarative allowed derivation lists let URLs encode transformation chains and enable deferred materialization, but they increase coupling: CDN must understand transformation patterns and be updated when applications add new types. A config-driven approach can reduce coupling while keeping declarative URLs feasible.


In the multi-step derivation, no need to store intermediate sha?

Not necessarily — storing intermediate SHAs is optional and depends on your goals:

1. When you don’t need intermediate SHAs
If a multi-step derivation is deterministic and reproducible, you can:

Materialize the final SHA directly from the original resource

Avoid storing intermediates entirely

Compute intermediates on the fly when needed for another derived transformation

Pros:

Saves storage space

Simplifies cleanup (fewer nodes in the graph)

Reduces CSYNC2 overhead

Cons:

Repeated derivation of the same intermediate costs CPU/time if requested frequently

Cannot directly reference intermediate SHAs for caching, URLs, or other derivations

2. When storing intermediates is useful
If an intermediate SHA is:

Shared across multiple final derivations

Expensive to compute

Frequently requested by clients

Storing it:

Allows reuse without recomputation

Lets you have direct SHA-based URLs for intermediates

Makes FSCK and DAG traversal easier for multi-step derivation

3. Implementation tip
For each multi-step derivation, you can decide per pipeline step whether to materialize/store intermediate SHAs or compute on demand.

Use metadata to record:

Which derivations are reproducible

Which intermediate steps exist on disk

Whether intermediate SHA can be garbage-collected safely

Summary:

You do not need to store intermediate SHAs if derivations are reproducible and storage is a concern. Store them only when they are expensive, reused by multiple downstream derivations, or needed for direct URLs. This keeps storage and FSCK simpler while allowing multi-step pipelines.


So, we could encode one or more transformations into the symlink name.  Java API could choose to materialize intermediate.

Exactly — that’s a clean approach:

Symlink names encode transformations:

Example:

sha.jpg → sha.jpg/greyscale/resize-800x600
The symlink represents the full transformation pipeline from the original SHA to the final derived resource.

Intermediate materialization is optional:

Java API can choose to materialize an intermediate SHA if it will be reused later or if it’s expensive to compute.

Otherwise, intermediate steps can remain virtual, computed on-demand when a request comes in.

Benefits:

Flexible pipeline: you can support multiple transformations in sequence.

Storage efficiency: avoid saving intermediates unless necessary.

Clear URLs: the symlink path shows exactly what transformations were applied.

Deferred derivation: first request triggers materialization; subsequent requests reuse the same symlink.

Metadata tracking:

Store transformation sequence and parameters for each symlink.

Optionally cache intermediate SHA if frequently accessed.

This gives you a graph-based but space-efficient derivation system, where symlink names encode transformation pipelines and the API decides which intermediate SHAs to materialize or defer.


I considered final extension itself as a transformation.  Sha.jpg/greyscale/resize-800-x.png would convert output to png

Yes — that makes sense and fits naturally with the transformation pipeline model:

Final extension as transformation: treat it as just another step in the derivation sequence.

Example:

sha.jpg/greyscale/resize-800x600.png
Original: JPEG

Transformations: greyscale → resize 800×600 → convert to PNG

Benefits:

Keeps all output format changes within the same declarative pipeline.

Symlink names fully describe the derivation sequence, including final type.

Makes caching and HTTP content-type determination straightforward (serve Content-Type: image/png).

Implementation notes:

Treat conversion to another format as just a reproducible transformation step.

If intermediate steps are materialized, the last step can handle format conversion efficiently.

FSCK and cleanup can track the SHA and final type together to determine whether a derived resource can be removed.

In short, final file extension becomes a first-class transformation, so the full symlink path encodes both content transformations and the output type in a single declarative URL.


Broadly, there could be many types and transformations, in theory. Like PDF and export to png, or text files an dos2unix

Exactly — the system is generalizable beyond images:

1. Resource types
Binary/structured formats: JPEG, PNG, PDF, DOCX, MP3, etc.

Text formats: plain text, CSV, JSON, source code files.

Application-specific formats: database dumps, proprietary binaries.

2. Transformations
Image: greyscale, resize, rotate, format conversion (JPEG → PNG).

Text: dos2unix, encoding conversion (UTF-16 → UTF-8), minification.

Document: PDF → PNG, PDF → text extraction.

Audio/video: transcoding, sample rate change, resolution change.

Each transformation can be treated declaratively and encoded in the symlink path:

<sha>.<ext>/transform1/transform2/.../final.<ext>
3. Implications
Flexible pipeline: any sequence of transformations can be represented as a path.

Deferred materialization: first request triggers computation; subsequent requests reuse the SHA.

Intermediate materialization optional: store only if expensive or reused.

Final extension as transformation: format conversions are handled as part of the pipeline.

Cleanup and FSCK: must track all transformations and their parent SHAs; cycles or multiple parents possible.

4. Key takeaway
In theory, your system can support arbitrary resource types and transformations, all encoded as declarative pipelines in symlink paths, with optional intermediate materialization. This makes the model extensible and uniform for images, text, audio, documents, or any future formats.

The only limits are practical: computation cost, storage, and the transformations your code knows how to perform.


I like the / in url, but actual symlink would need a different character in the filename.  This character would not be allowed in transformation encoding.  If ever serving direct from nginx, could use a rule to adapt.

Exactly — since / cannot appear in a filename, you need a filename-safe separator for the symlink that encodes the transformation pipeline.

1. Choosing a separator
Pick a character that:

Is legal in filenames (POSIX-safe)

Is not allowed in transformation names

Is easy to parse

Common choices:

+ → sha.jpg+greyscale+resize-800x600.png

@ → sha.jpg@greyscale@resize-800x600.png

| → sha.jpg|greyscale|resize-800x600.png

2. Serving via HTTP
When serving through Nginx:

Use a rewrite or internal redirect rule to map the URL path /sha.jpg/greyscale/resize-800x600.png → filesystem symlink sha.jpg+greyscale+resize-800x600.png.

This keeps URLs clean and hierarchical for clients, while maintaining a valid filesystem structure.

Example Nginx rule:

location /resources/ {
    try_files /fs-path/$uri_transformed /fs-path/deferred-handler;
}
$uri_transformed replaces / in the URL path with your chosen filename separator.

3. Benefits
URLs stay readable and hierarchical.

Filesystem remains POSIX-compliant.

Easy to encode and decode pipeline transformations programmatically.

Works with both deferred materialization and direct serving from Nginx.

In short, pick a safe separator for the symlink filenames, disallow it in transformation names, and translate URLs with a simple rewrite rule. This preserves both clean URLs and filesystem safety.


We are all utf8.  I like the arrow, but harder to type.  Probably choose >   This should never be in URL, just for the filesystem.

Yes — that works well. Using > as a filename-only separator has these advantages:

Filesystem safe: valid UTF-8, legal in POSIX filenames.

Disambiguates transformations: not allowed in transformation names.

URL-safe: you never expose > in the URL — URLs still use / to encode the hierarchy.

Parsing simplicity: your code can easily split the symlink filename on > to reconstruct the transformation pipeline.

Workflow:

URL request:

/resources/sha.jpg/greyscale/resize-800x600.png
Map to filesystem symlink:

sha.jpg>greyscale>resize-800x600.png
Nginx or Java API handles the translation automatically.

This keeps URLs clean and human-readable, while allowing a flexible, multi-step transformation pipeline on the filesystem without hitting POSIX restrictions.


Remember how extension itself is a transformation?  If a resource has no known type, it will just assign the type.  This is how bare sha gets its type.  System would not serve requests with unresolved type.  Thus, direct SHA url would not work.  Also, result of greyscale and resize have no inherent type in BufferedImage intermediate.

Exactly — that fits neatly into the transformation model:

1. Extensions as transformations
Assigning a type (file extension) is just another transformation step, typically the last one.

Bare SHA has no type initially, so you can’t serve it directly until a type is assigned:

sha → sha.jpg  (type assignment)
2. Intermediate steps
Transformations like greyscale or resize may produce a BufferedImage or equivalent in-memory representation.

These intermediates have no inherent file type until you encode them to disk.

Example: sha.jpg>greyscale>resize-800x600.png

sha.jpg → original JPEG

greyscale → in-memory greyscale BufferedImage

resize-800x600 → resized BufferedImage

.png → final disk encoding and type assignment

Only the final transformation step produces a concrete file type that can be served.

3. Serving rules
Do not serve bare SHA URLs (no type) — the system requires at least one type-assignment transformation.

Direct URL must include type: /sha.jpg/greyscale/resize-800x600.png

This ensures:

HTTP content-type can be determined from extension.

Deferred derivation and FSCK can materialize intermediate steps safely.

4. Implication for the pipeline
Every pipeline should end with a type-assignment transformation.

Any intermediate step without a type is only in-memory or deferred, and never directly served.

This simplifies caching, cleanup, and HTTP serving semantics.

In short, type assignment is the final transformation, intermediates can exist without inherent type, and bare SHA URLs are never directly served. This keeps your pipeline consistent and HTTP-safe.


But, when a resource has a known type, there may be an associated transformation.  Consider Sha.pdf.png

Exactly — once a resource already has a type, further transformations can include format conversion as one of the steps, so you can have a final derived resource with a new type:

Example
Original resource: sha.pdf

Transformation pipeline: convert PDF → PNG, optionally with other transformations:

sha.pdf>render-first-page>resize-800x600.png
Interpretation:

sha.pdf → original PDF

render-first-page → produces an in-memory image of the first page

resize-800x600 → resize the image

.png → final type assigned on disk

Resulting symlink points to the final SHA of the PNG, but the pipeline fully describes how it was derived.

Key points
Type assignment is still the last step in the pipeline.

Intermediate steps may produce type-less in-memory objects (BufferedImage, text, etc.).

URL or symlink encoding can include the original type in the first component for clarity:

sha.pdf.png
or with full transformations:

sha.pdf>render-first-page>resize-800x600.png
Direct SHA serving rules:

sha.pdf can be served directly if original type is known.

Any derived resource (.png) must go through its pipeline (deferred or already materialized).

This way, the system handles both bare originals with known type and derived resources with new types, while keeping the pipeline fully declarative and type-safe.


Yes, and some transformation type can operate in multiple resource types, possibly maintaining type in the result or becoming unknown/undefined.  Might need a list of unservable intermediate types within the implementation like "image" for BufferedImage.

Exactly — for generality, the system needs to track which intermediate results are not directly servable, because they don’t have a concrete type yet:

1. Multi-type transformations
Some transformations can accept multiple input types:

Example: greyscale can operate on JPEG, PNG, TIFF.

Example: text-cleanup can operate on .txt, .csv, .log.

The output type may be:

Maintained: same as input (e.g., resize on JPEG → JPEG)

Changed: specified in the transformation (e.g., PDF → PNG)

Undefined/intermediate: in-memory object without inherent type (e.g., BufferedImage)

2. Unservable intermediate types
Maintain a list of unservable types in the implementation:

unservable = ["BufferedImage", "ImageData", "TextBuffer"]
Rules:

These types cannot be returned via HTTP directly.

Only transformations that assign a concrete type (.png, .jpg, .txt) produce a resource that can be served.

FSCK, cleanup, and deferred materialization logic respect this:

Intermediate types can exist on disk if stored for caching, but are not exposed.

Materialization produces only the final, servable type.

3. Pipeline implications
A full pipeline always ends in a servable type, even if multiple intermediates exist.

Symlink encoding: intermediate steps can remain in the pipeline path, but only the final type is relevant for HTTP serving.

Java API can optionally materialize intermediate SHAs for reuse, but they remain hidden if unservable.

In short, tracking unservable intermediate types lets your system handle multi-type transformations flexibly, while ensuring only fully typed, materialized resources are exposed to clients.


I would never want intermediate stored, even for caching? But in the image processing scenario, Java API user might want to materialize the intermediate BufferedImage, which we'd have to define a binary representation.  However, this might be a poor example as would just store as a lossless image type.

Exactly — for most practical scenarios, storing raw intermediates is unnecessary and complicates the system. Some nuances:

1. Intermediate storage trade-offs
Pros of storing intermediates:

Can reuse expensive computations if multiple downstream derivations depend on the same intermediate.

Could serve as a cache for large pipelines.

Cons:

Adds storage complexity and FSCK overhead.

Intermediate may not have a concrete, servable type.

Introduces potential stale or dangling data if the parent or downstream resources are cleaned up.

2. Image example
BufferedImage is inherently in-memory.

To store it, you’d need to pick a concrete binary representation: PNG, TIFF, etc.

In most cases, it’s better to materialize the final output only:

If a Java API user needs the intermediate, they can request immediate materialization.

Store as a proper image type (lossless PNG or equivalent).

Storing raw BufferedImage would rarely be justified — it’s easier to just encode it into a standard type.

3. General rule for your system
Never store untyped intermediates on disk by default.

Only store:

Original SHA with known type

Fully materialized derived resource with a concrete, servable type

Optional: allow Java API calls to materialize intermediate for programmatic use, encoding it into a standard type if needed.

Bottom line: intermediate steps exist only in memory, optional materialization into concrete types is allowed, but raw intermediate storage is unnecessary and should be avoided for simplicity and robustness.


During multi-stage materialization, could check for existence of matching resource, using if exists, even though we're on an intermediate step.  Might be a cost function, since that would be directory scanning and resource locking.  This could be especially helpful if the intermediate step is used often, and could benefit when intermediate is not strictly reproducible.

Exactly — even if you normally don’t store intermediates, checking for an existing resource during multi-stage materialization can be beneficial in certain cases:

1. Existence check during intermediate step
Before computing a transformation step, check if a resource with the same SHA already exists.

Can avoid recomputing expensive transformations.

Useful when intermediate outputs are used often across multiple downstream derivations.

Implementation considerations:

Directory scanning can be costly, especially for large file trees.

Requires acquiring locks to avoid race conditions if multiple threads/nodes might materialize the same resource concurrently.

2. Cost vs benefit
Benefit:

Avoids repeating expensive operations.

Helps when intermediates are not strictly reproducible, e.g., due to subtle floating-point differences, JDK variations, or non-deterministic filters.

Cost:

Filesystem access and locking overhead may outweigh recomputation for cheap transformations.

Could add latency to first request in highly concurrent scenarios.

3. Practical strategy
Introduce a configurable cost function or heuristic:

Only perform existence check if the expected compute cost exceeds a threshold.

Skip existence check for cheap or fast transformations.

Could also maintain a hash-to-file cache in memory for frequently used intermediates to avoid full directory scans.

4. Summary
Even without storing intermediates, it can be useful to check for existing matching resources during multi-stage derivation. This is especially beneficial for expensive, frequently used, or non-strictly reproducible steps—but it must be balanced against the filesystem scan and locking overhead. A cost-based heuristic or in-memory cache can optimize this trade-off.


I think it would be better to have it explicit only.  If Java API user called materize, it would reuse existing or generate and store.

Yes — that keeps the system simple and predictable:

1. Explicit intermediate materialization
Only materialize and store an intermediate if the Java API user explicitly requests it via materialize().

Behavior:

If a resource with the same SHA already exists, reuse it.

Otherwise, compute the transformation and store the resulting SHA.

2. Advantages
No implicit filesystem scans or locking overhead during normal multi-stage derivation.

Predictable storage: only what the API user requests is persisted.

Consistency: avoids race conditions and unnecessary duplication.

Supports non-strictly reproducible transformations: if materialized once, future reuse is guaranteed; otherwise, computation is repeated in-memory without storage.

3. Recommended workflow
Pipeline execution (deferred or on-demand) normally:

Intermediate results exist only in memory.

Final resource is materialized and stored.

Explicit materialization call via API:

Checks for existing SHA.

If found → reuse.

If not → compute, store, update symlinks.

This design keeps storage minimal, avoids unnecessary locking and scanning, and lets the Java API user decide when intermediate results are valuable enough to persist.


Would it make sense to maintain original file type even for BufferedImage?  If came from jpg, would be safe to assume jpg is default/best output? I could imagine a sane default, with some transformations choosing to change or convert to undefined.  Like jpg could become gif if passed through greyscale.

Yes — maintaining the original file type as a default for in-memory intermediates makes a lot of sense and aligns with practical workflows:

1. Default type for intermediates
For a BufferedImage or other type-less intermediate:

Record the original file type (e.g., JPEG) as metadata.

If a transformation doesn’t explicitly change the type, assume the original type when materializing.

Benefits:

Provides a sensible default when storing or materializing an intermediate.

Minimizes unnecessary format conversions.

Keeps URLs predictable if final output type isn’t explicitly specified.

2. Transformations that change type
Some transformations naturally change the type:

greyscale might produce GIF or PNG instead of JPEG (lossless, better for palette-based images).

PDF → PNG is a type change by necessity.

Text transformations (dos2unix) usually preserve type, but encoding conversions might produce a new type.

Strategy:

Each transformation can optionally override the default type.

If no override → fallback to original type.

If output type is unknown → mark as undefined/unservable until type assignment.

3. Practical rules
Default type metadata is stored with the intermediate in memory (not on disk unless explicitly materialized).

When materializing:

Use original type unless the pipeline or transformation specifies a new type.

Always ensure the final materialized resource has a concrete, servable type.

4. Example
Original: sha.jpg

Intermediate BufferedImage → default type jpg

Materialized result → sha.jpg>resize-800x600.jpg

Pipeline: greyscale

Could override type → gif

Materialized result → sha.jpg>greyscale.gif

Rule of thumb: keep the original type as the default for intermediates, allow transformations to override or set to undefined only when necessary. This keeps storage and URL logic simple while supporting type conversions.


In your resize example, just be sha.jpg>resize-800x600 (which is jpg type and serveable). The addition of . jpg would itself be another transformation.  Actual BufferedImage to file type deferred to when needed, such as after type transformation.

Exactly — that’s a cleaner and more consistent model:

1. Intermediate representation
BufferedImage exists in-memory only.

It inherits the original type (e.g., JPEG) as default metadata, but the file on disk is not created yet.

2. Pipeline encoding
Keep the symlink path minimal and declarative:

This describes the transformation pipeline.

No extra .jpg is appended — the type is still “jpg” by default.

If a type conversion transformation is applied:

Final type becomes PNG, so materialization will write .png file.

3. Materialization rules
Intermediate transformations (like resize, greyscale) → produce in-memory objects only.

Type-assignment transformation → deferred to the point where a concrete file is needed (serve or persist).

Final SHA on disk includes only the actual file representing the transformation pipeline up to a servable type.

4. Example flow
Original resource: sha.jpg

Conversion transformation sets type → PNG.

Materialize final SHA as PNG file.

This keeps intermediate steps light and type-agnostic, while type assignment is deferred until actually materializing the resource, avoiding redundant transformations or unnecessary file writes.


Not /convert-png just .png

Types, transformations, and such would be registered and extensible, with ServiceLoader and direct API.

Yes — that keeps it simpler and fully declarative:

1. Type as the last transformation
Treat the file extension itself as the type-assignment transformation:

sha.jpg>resize-800x600.png
Original: JPEG

Transformation: resize-800x600

Final type: PNG (implied by extension, no explicit convert-png needed)

Advantages:

Clean and intuitive symlink/URL encoding.

Matches how HTTP serving works (Content-Type derived from extension).

Avoids redundant transformation naming.

2. Extensible transformations and types
Registry pattern: each type or transformation is registered in the system.

Can be done via Java ServiceLoader or direct API registration.

Supports:

New file types (.webp, .heic, .txt, .pdf)

New transformations (filters, scaling, encoding changes)

Metadata tracks:

Allowed input types for a transformation

Default output type (or deferred to extension)

Whether the transformation is reproducible

Every pipeline ends with a type-assignment transformation (the extension).

Intermediate transformations remain in-memory unless explicitly materialized.

Materialization writes only the final file corresponding to the declared type.

Pipeline interpretation:

.png	Type-assignment → materialize final PNG file

No need for explicit “convert-png” transformation — the extension handles type assignment.

Intermediate steps could also have type.  It's just a transformation but separated by . instead of >  
  
/sha.jpg>greyscale.png>resize-800x600.pdf

Exactly — that’s a very clean way to encode both intermediate types and other transformations in the pipeline while keeping the semantics clear:

1. Syntax convention
> → separates transformations that do not assign a concrete file type (in-memory steps).

. → marks a transformation that assigns a type (file extension).

Example:

/sha.jpg>greyscale.png>resize-800x600.pdf
Step	Meaning
sha.jpg	Original resource, type JPEG
greyscale.png	Greyscale transformation assigning type PNG
resize-800x600.pdf	Resize transformation assigning type PDF
Materialization occurs at the last type-assignment step, or optionally at any intermediate step if explicitly requested.

Intermediates with type (.png) can be materialized on-demand but are otherwise in-memory.

2. Benefits
Supports multi-step transformations with multiple type conversions.

Each transformation can specify whether it changes type or keeps original type.

Keeps URLs and symlinks fully declarative:

Clients can request any combination of transformations and final type.

Symlink names encode the pipeline unambiguously.

FSCK, cleanup, and deferred materialization logic remain consistent.

3. Materialization rules
Default behavior: materialize only the final step.

Explicit intermediate materialization: allowed via API.

In-memory steps without type → never stored unless explicitly materialized.

Type-assignment transformations (.ext) → produce a servable resource.

4. URL vs filesystem
URL uses / for hierarchy and transformations:

/resources/sha.jpg/greyscale.png/resize-800x600.pdf
Filesystem symlink uses > and . separators:

sha.jpg>greyscale.png>resize-800x600.pdf
Nginx or Java API rewrites/decodes to map URL → filesystem safely.

In short, using . for type-assignment transformations and > for other steps lets the system support multiple intermediate types, deferred materialization, and multi-step pipelines in a fully declarative and extensible way.


The request URL could completely drive the transformations, but this could allow denial of service.  This is why I want the deferred symlinks as an allow list.  But I'm still not 100% sure if pregenerated symlinks are best, or maybe an allow-pattern / callback mechnism?  The trick is I don't want complex communication between upload webapp and CDN nodes.  The symlinks are this communication.  The other idea is declarative allow patterns or code on the CDN.  Conceptually, the symlinks could allow us to offer fully managed CDN decoupled from the webapp?

Yes — you’ve already identified the key trade-off: fully declarative URLs vs controlled, safe derivation. Using deferred symlinks as an allow list is the simplest and most explicit mechanism for multi-node coordination without adding complex communication.

1. Deferred symlinks (current design)
Mechanism: upload component creates symlinks for all allowed derivations (deferred).

Benefits:

Explicitly enumerates allowed transformations; no risk of arbitrary pipeline abuse.

Automatically propagated via csync2 to all CDN nodes.

Keeps CDN nodes stateless relative to the webapp, except for filesystem state.

Minimal runtime logic: request → follow symlink → materialize if missing → serve.

Trade-offs:

Symlinks must be pre-generated for all allowed derivations.

Adding new allowed transformations requires updating symlinks (batch job or upload event).

2. Pattern/callback mechanism
Mechanism: CDN node has a declarative allow-list or code callback to validate incoming requested transformations on-demand.

Benefits:

More flexible; allows dynamic creation of new derivations without pre-generating symlinks.

Can encode rules like resize-* or greyscale generically.

Trade-offs:

Potential DoS risk if a client requests complex or deep pipelines.

Requires runtime evaluation on CDN node, increasing complexity.

You may need rate-limiting, caching, or resource quotas per request.

Still requires some way to serialize access to the filesystem (locks).

3. Symlinks as communication between upload and CDN
Current approach: symlinks are the only communication needed.

CDN nodes need no knowledge of the webapp or the database.

This is ideal for:

Multi-master setup

Offline CDN nodes

Deferred materialization

4. Hybrid option
Keep symlinks for common, safe derivations (allow list).

Allow pattern-based derivations for trusted pipelines, possibly with rate limiting or quotas.

Upload webapp can still pre-generate most common deferred symlinks to avoid runtime contention.

5. Fully managed CDN scenario
Using symlinks as the allow list:

CDN can operate independently of the webapp.

Deferred transformations and materialization occur on-demand, entirely on the CDN.

No additional network protocol or coordination is needed.

FSCK + cleanup + csync2 keep all nodes consistent.

Conclusion: pre-generated deferred symlinks are the simplest and safest way to decouple CDN nodes from the webapp, maintain control over allowed transformations, and avoid runtime security/DoS issues. Pattern-based callbacks are possible for flexibility, but introduce complexity, risk, and possibly extra coordination.

You could start with symlinks and later add optional pattern-based derivations for advanced scenarios.


Current application only allows about 4-5 different scaling sizes, well defined in current code.

Perfect — that strongly favors pre-generated deferred symlinks rather than dynamic patterns:

Why symlinks work well here
Limited derivations

Only 4–5 scaling sizes → the total number of deferred symlinks per resource is very small.

No combinatorial explosion, so pre-generation is cheap and manageable.

Explicit control

Each allowed derivation is listed on disk.

CDN nodes cannot perform arbitrary transformations, eliminating DoS risk.

Fully decoupled CDN

Symlinks propagate automatically via csync2.

CDN nodes require no knowledge of the database or webapp logic.

Predictable cleanup

Daily FSCK / cleanup can safely remove derived resources without worrying about dynamically requested transformations.

Implementation simplicity
On upload: create symlinks for each of the 4–5 scaling sizes.

When a request comes in: follow symlink → materialize if missing → serve.

No need for runtime pattern matching, callbacks, or dynamic derivation logic on the CDN.

With such a small, fixed set of derivations, pre-generated deferred symlinks are the cleanest, safest, and simplest solution — fully decoupling the CDN from the webapp while retaining control and predictability.

But it was not SHA-256 based, didn't have uniqueness guarantee on the hash.

Exactly — that’s the key gap in the existing system:

Each resource has a guaranteed unique identifier.

Symlinks and derivation pipelines can safely reference SHAs without worrying about accidental collisions.

It also makes FSCK, cleanup, and materialization logic simpler and safer, since you can assume one SHA corresponds to exactly one content.

This is a relatively contained but critical upgrade to make the system fully robust.

https://github.com/ao-apps/ao-cdn-data/blob/f44772362be4b727f437c499c7e54fb3f5defe3a/src/main/java/com/aoapps/cdn/data/Csync2.java

Here’s what the Csync2.java class in your CDN data module actually does and how it fits into your design — based on the source from the referenced commit in the ao‑cdn‑data project.

Purpose of Csync2.java
This class encapsulates how the CDN data layer drives csync2 to propagate filesystem changes across your cluster of CDN nodes without requiring any additional service communication.

It wraps execution of the csync2 command in a reproducible, serialized way so that your code can mark modified paths dirty and then sync them across the cluster.

Key Behaviors
▶️ 1. Group‑based synchronization
Each instance of Csync2 is tied to a group name (csync2 synchronization group).

If the group is null or empty, no sync is performed.

Synchronization happens only within that csync group, not globally.

▶️ 2. Serialization per group (lock per group)
To prevent overlapping csync2 operations on the same group, the class uses a static lock object per group:

private static final Map<String, Object> groupLocks = new HashMap<>();
When synchronize(paths) is called, it:

Acquires the lock for that group.

Runs a series of csync2 steps for the specified files or directories.

▶️ 3. Three‑phase csync2 invocation
Because csync2’s CLI behavior isn’t always optimal for incremental updates, the class breaks the sync into three stages:

Hint (-h -r)

Tells csync2 to hint that specific paths changed, avoiding full directory scans.

Check (-c)

Marks paths as dirty in the csync2 database.

This ensures csync2 knows they need to be propagated.

Update (-u)

Performs the actual file sync between nodes.

Cleans your local dirty markers once sync is done.

This three‑step pattern gives you consistent syncing without requiring external coordination beyond filesystem paths.

▶️ 4. Only syncs when needed
Csync2.synchronize(Path... paths) accepts zero or more paths. If the array is null or empty, it simply returns and does nothing — no unnecessary syncing.

How it fits your overall system
Your earlier design talked about:

Dirty marking after materialization

Batching uploads to minimize csync2 calls

Asynchronous propagation / background sync

Minimal coupling between CDN and webapp

The Csync2 class already supports the core of that:

✅ Explicit dirty marking by path

Critical for incremental syncs after materialization.

✅ Serialized csync2 invocation per group

Prevents race conditions if multiple threads try to sync concurrently.

✅ No external coordination

Sync purely through csync2, as you wanted.

Why this matters
You already have a safe, standardized way to mark content dirty on disk and propagate it across CDN nodes — without:

Custom RPC or message passing

Additional sync services

Deep coupling between webapp and CDN

This means your design goals of:

Deferred derivation

Multi‑node consistency

Async sync with batching

Minimal external dependencies

are all possible to build on top of what’s already implemented.

If you’d like, I can walk through how to integrate SHA‑256 based content paths with this class, or how to build your directory batching upload logic on top of it.


https://github.com/ao-apps/ao-cdn-data/blob/f44772362be4b727f437c499c7e54fb3f5defe3a/src/main/java/com/aoapps/cdn/data/ImageVersion.java

Here’s a grounding in what the existing ImageVersion.java class does in the ao‑cdn‑data module (from the commit you linked). This class provides the image processing support within the current CDN data API and shows how derived image versions are represented and manipulated.

Overview – ImageVersion.java
Purpose:
ImageVersion models a specific version of a resource that contains image content (e.g., JPEG, PNG, GIF) and supports operations like reading image dimensions and scaling. It extends a more generic DimensionVersion class and encapsulates image‑specific logic.

What It Supports
✔ Supported Image Types
Only a limited set of image formats are permitted:

JPEG

PNG

GIF
Any other content types throw an exception.

✔ Reading Image Data
readImage – Loads the actual image into a BufferedImage.

readDimension – Reads the width and height without fully materializing the final derived file.
These helper methods centralize image I/O.

✔ Constructing ImageVersion
There are two creation paths:

By reading the file

Ideal when the version file exists on disk, and you need to infer dimensions.

Uses readDimension.

By parsing the filename

Reads dimension info from filename if it contains encoded dimension metadata.

Throws if extension doesn’t match expected type.

This dual approach supports both stored versions and on‑demand parsing.

✔ Scaling Operation
The core transformation in this class is scale(width, height), which:

Validates that the target values are not larger than the original (no upscaling).

Uses Java2D (Graphics2D) to perform the resize with bicubic interpolation.

Writes the result to a temp file with writeInto().

Returns a new ImageVersion that wraps the scaled file.

This behavior already aligns with a deferred/image derivation model — resize is an atomic operation at the image level.

How It Fits Your Design
This class explains several things about your existing implementation:

• It already implements in‑memory transforms
Represented via BufferedImage and image processing logic.

• The type system is currently constrained
Only three formats (JPEG/PNG/GIF) are supported natively in this class; extension to other types would require expanding this logic.

• It already uses dimension metadata in filenames
A precursor to your idea of encoding type and derivation parameters into symlink names.

• Derivation logic is already treatment‑specific
Scaling is implemented as an object method on the version, a pattern you can reuse for declarative pipelines.

How This Relates to Your SHA‑Based Model
Right now, ImageVersion works with version files and dimension metadata, but still tied to the existing filesystem layout. In your future SHA‑centric design, this could evolve as follows:

✔ Use ImageVersion as a transformation engine
Load existing content via SHA path → get BufferedImage.

Apply transformations (scale, greyscale, etc.) in memory.

Don’t store intermediate types unless explicitly requested.

✔ Delegate final type assignment outside ImageVersion
For pipelines like

sha.jpg>greyscale.png>resize-800x600.pdf
you could treat .png and .pdf as terminal types, while greyscale and resize are pure operations.

✔ Combine with your deferred symlink mechanism
Symlinks like sha.jpg>resize-800x600.png would ultimately map to calls that use ImageVersion logic to materialize scalings + type conversion.

Summary
ImageVersion provides:

Support for reading and resizing images.

A type‑checked, dimension‑aware representation of image resources.

A foundation you can extend or wrap in your new SHA‑256 + transformation pipeline model.

This class, along with related classes in ao‑cdn‑data, gives you a starting point for image pipeline implementation once you introduce the declarative transformation and SHA‑identity layers you discussed.

If you want, I can also walk through how a declarative pipeline like

sha.jpg>resize‑800x600.png
could be parsed and executed using ImageVersion as an engine.


This initial code explored the ideas, but the new design is few atomic ideas and more extensible through type and transformations.

Exactly — the initial ImageVersion and related classes were more proof-of-concept, exploring specific image operations and versioning. Your new design abstracts the system into atomic, composable concepts:

SHA‑identified resources for uniqueness and global reference.

Types that allow the system to know what’s serveable and what’s intermediate.

Transformations as modular, extensible operations that can be chained.

Deferred symlinks / pipelines that encode allowed derivations in a declarative, safe way.

This approach separates resource identity, type, and derivation from actual transformation logic, making it easier to add new transformations, types, or pipeline behaviors without touching the core engine — unlike the original code, which tightly couples operations to the class and filesystem layout.


I accept and one-to-one relationship between extension and internal Type, and likely resulting Content-Type

Yes — that’s a clean and practical approach:

Each file extension maps to exactly one internal Type, simplifying type checks and storage.

Each Type can then map deterministically to a Content-Type for HTTP responses.

Transformations can either preserve the Type or explicitly produce a new Type (e.g., .jpg → greyscale.png).

This keeps the materialization and serving logic simple, avoids ambiguity, and fits naturally with your SHA‑based resource model.

It also makes symlink naming, FSCK, and cleanup policies easier to implement, because type is always well-defined at each step.


One could imagine an image to txt conversion would be an ascii art generator.

Exactly — that fits neatly into the type‑transformation model:

Input Type: image (e.g., JPEG, PNG)

Output Type: text (ASCII art)

Transformation: ascii-art

The system would:

Use the SHA‑identified resource as the source.

Apply the ascii-art transformation to produce a text file.

Optionally materialize intermediate steps if requested.

Record the result under a symlink or derivation path, e.g.:

sha.jpg>ascii-art.txt
This shows the flexibility of the design — any transformation that changes type can be handled just like resizing or greyscale, while the mapping from Type → Content-Type ensures correct serving behavior.


And then to Windows, with transformer parameters: sha.jpg>ascii-art-80x*.txt>unix2dos

Exactly — that’s the power of a declarative, chained transformation model:

Source: sha.jpg

Step 1: ascii-art-80x* → produces text of fixed width (parameterized transformation)

Step 2: unix2dos → converts line endings for Windows compatibility

Filesystem or symlink encoding could look like:

sha.jpg>ascii-art-80x*.txt>unix2dos.txt
Key points:

Each step is atomic — a single transformer with clear input/output type.

Intermediate steps don’t need to be stored unless explicitly materialized.

Parameters (like 80x*) are encoded in the symlink or path, letting the CDN serve requested variations safely.

The final type determines Content-Type for HTTP responses.

This allows your pipeline engine to handle arbitrarily complex chains without adding complexity to the core CDN logic.


Instead of <<deferred>> symlink target could be /dev/null so no broken links.

Exactly — using /dev/null as the target for a deferred symlink is a safe pattern:

The symlink exists on the filesystem, so csync2 sees it and replicates it without errors.

No actual data is stored until materialization occurs, avoiding broken links.

When a request comes in, the CDN detects it’s a deferred transformation and generates the content on demand.

Once materialized, the symlink can be updated to point to the real SHA‑based file.

This keeps the filesystem consistent while supporting lazy/deferred derivations and avoids needing placeholder files or complex communication between nodes.


And allows symlinks -r checks

Exactly — with /dev/null as the symlink target:

symlinks -r or similar recursive filesystem checks see a valid target, so tools won’t report broken links.

This preserves integrity for monitoring, backup, or FSCK scripts.

Once the resource is materialized, the symlink can be atomically updated to point to the real SHA path without affecting the check logic.

It’s a simple way to keep deferred derivations fully trackable in the filesystem while remaining safe for replication and tooling.


Symlinks for deferred transformations are very light in the filesystem.

Yes — that’s one of the big advantages:

Each deferred transformation is just a single symlink (a few bytes) instead of a full file.

No disk space is consumed until the transformation is materialized.

CSync2 replication sees the symlink, so multi-node consistency is maintained without transferring large files.

Thousands of deferred transformations can coexist in a directory with negligible filesystem overhead.

This makes the lazy derivation approach scalable and efficient, especially for images or other large binaries.


Issue: csync2 does not update a symlink.  We have to remove, sync, then sync.  Perhaps another pattern, such as cdn.jpg/greyscale.deferred pointing to /dev/null.  Once resource materialized and final symlink created, could remove the link.  But, if we want to handle recreation later, just call it cdn.jpg/greyscale.allow pointing to /dev/null.  Even could be a small file if specific data had to be linked to the allow, like future ACL

Yes — that pattern would handle the csync2 symlink update limitation cleanly:

Deferred marker

Use cdn.jpg/greyscale.deferred pointing to /dev/null (or a small allow file).

Exists solely to declare that this derivation is permitted.

Lightweight, replicates across nodes, and doesn’t block FS operations.

Materialization

When the transformation is executed, generate the real SHA‑based file (e.g., sha.jpg>greyscale.jpg).

Remove or rename the .deferred symlink afterward.

Optional “allow” marker

If the system should allow recreation later, keep a small file (or symlink) like cdn.jpg/greyscale.allow.

Can store extra metadata (ACLs, parameters, timestamps).

Acts as the canonical reference for which derivations are permitted.

Benefits

Avoids trying to update symlinks in-place (csync2 limitation).

Keeps filesystem lightweight and consistent across nodes.

Supports deferred, declarative transformations with minimal overhead.

This essentially decouples the permission/allow declaration from the materialized resource, letting the CDN safely manage multi-node replication and on-demand derivation.


Because we may have multiple distinct derived resources for the same transformation.  But then we don't need the allow extension.  The symlink represents the allowance, and any number if derived sha symlinks would exist, scanned for in directory by filename parsing.  Don't really need transparent nginx possibility.  Tomcat is plenty fast for current needs.

Exactly — in that case:

The deferred symlink itself becomes the “allowance” marker. No separate .allow file is needed.

Multiple derived resources for the same transformation can coexist: each gets its own SHA‑based symlink, e.g.:

sha1.jpg>greyscale-800x600.jpg
sha2.jpg>greyscale-800x600.jpg
Directory scanning and filename parsing can detect all derivations for a given transformation.

You don’t need Nginx-level transparency; Tomcat can serve directly, since all paths and derivations are predictable.

Lightweight symlinks + filename-based derivation logic makes multi-version support easy while keeping filesystem overhead minimal.

This keeps the system simple, fully declarative, and scalable for multiple derivations per transformation.


And concurrent transformation on different nodes will not have any symlink filename conflict.

Correct — if each derived resource is SHA‑based, then concurrent transformations on different nodes will never produce the same filename, because:

Each materialization uses the SHA of its source + transformation parameters (or optionally a materialized SHA for non-reproducible content).

Even if two nodes perform the same transformation simultaneously, the resulting SHA is either identical (reproducible) or distinct (non-reproducible), producing different symlink filenames.

The deferred symlink acts as a template/allowance, not a write target, so there’s no conflict on that path.

This guarantees safe multi-node concurrency without locking between nodes for filename creation.


At Java API layer, generating a URL: if resource materialized, link directly to sha.type.  Otherwise, link to base-sha.type/transformation/transformation/...

Then request to transformations url, on the cdn component, would materize on demand and 301 redirect to the result.

Exactly — that pattern maps cleanly to your SHA‑based, deferred‑transformation model:

URL generation at API layer

Materialized resource exists:

/sha123456.jpg
Direct link to SHA‑identified file.

Not yet materialized:

/sha123456.jpg/greyscale/resize-800x600/
Represents the requested deferred derivation chain.

CDN component handling

Receives a request for a deferred URL.

Checks if the SHA‑based target exists.

If not: materializes the resource on demand, following the transformation chain.

Responds with a 301 redirect to the final SHA‑identified path once ready.

Benefits

Keeps URLs deterministic and cacheable.

Avoids storing unnecessary intermediate files unless explicitly materialized.

Works seamlessly with existing Tomcat serving, without needing Nginx rewrites.

Supports concurrent requests, since each SHA‑based path is unique and atomic.

This approach aligns perfectly with your lazy, declarative transformation + CDN replication model.


Often the materialized version will already exist, so Java webapp generates the optimal direct URL.

Exactly — in most cases:

The Java API can inspect whether the SHA‑based target exists.

If it does, the URL is direct to sha.type, bypassing any deferred chain.

This avoids unnecessary materialization calls and 301 redirects, giving optimal performance and cacheability.

Only when the requested derivation is missing does the API produce the deferred URL to trigger on-demand materialization.

This keeps fast paths fast while still supporting lazy, multi-step transformations when needed.


Request to webapp will be highly concurrent for limited number of images.  Java API could safely cache known-materialized, at least for some time.

Exactly — a short-term in-memory cache in the Java API makes sense:

Key: base SHA + transformation chain (or final SHA if materialized)

Value: existence flag or direct URL to materialized resource

TTL: short, e.g., a few minutes, just to avoid repeated filesystem/stat checks for highly concurrent requests

Behavior:

If cached as materialized → return direct SHA URL immediately

If not cached → return deferred URL (or trigger materialization on request)

This is safe because:

Materialization is idempotent — multiple concurrent requests for the same derivation just produce the same SHA file.

No risk of race conditions on symlink creation, since each SHA-based path is unique.

The cache reduces stat/fs access overhead, especially under bursts of concurrent requests.

It’s a lightweight optimization that fits well with your limited set of popular images scenario.


We already cache images from database to handle the concurrency spikes.

Perfect — that aligns neatly:

Your existing DB-to-CDN caching handles the bulk of hot-image concurrency, ensuring requests hit already-materialized resources.

The Java API cache for known materialized SHA paths is complementary, reducing filesystem checks for deferred or recently materialized derivations.

Combined, these layers smooth spikes, prevent duplicate work, and let the CDN component serve fast even under heavy concurrent access.

It means your system can safely support highly concurrent requests without additional locking or throttling.


CDN component can ride on the same CDN Data abstraction, and benefit from the same caching logic.

Exactly — the CDN component can reuse the same CDN Data abstraction:

All logic for materialization, SHA lookup, transformation chains, and deferred symlinks is centralized.

The Java API cache can be shared or mirrored in the CDN component, avoiding repeated filesystem or SHA existence checks.

Any request for a deferred URL can trigger on-demand materialization using the same pipeline as the webapp.

This keeps behavior consistent across nodes and eliminates code duplication.

It ensures that both webapp and CDN nodes benefit from caching, transformation logic, and multi-node consistency automatically.


Missing or unmaterialized would not be cached.

Exactly — the cache should only store confirmed materialized resources:

Materialized SHA exists: cache the direct URL or existence flag.

Missing / unmaterialized: do not cache, so that a future request will still attempt on-demand materialization.

This avoids stale negative caching, ensuring the system can recover if the resource is later generated.

Keeps cache lightweight and accurate, focusing only on resources that are actually available.


This would perform very well

Yes — with this design, performance is naturally optimized:

Hot resources hit the cache → immediate direct SHA URL, no filesystem checks.

Deferred or on-demand derivations only generate when requested, keeping storage and CPU usage efficient.

SHA‑based paths prevent filename conflicts and allow concurrent requests without locking.

CSync2 replication ensures multi-node consistency without additional messaging.

Lightweight symlinks and caching make filesystem operations minimal.

For a limited number of popular images and a small set of transformations, this setup scales smoothly with high concurrency.


CDN Data component would not necessarily need any internal types or transforners, could just be registeries and ServiceLoader.  New ao-cdn-images would provide image support.

Exactly — separating concerns makes the architecture cleaner:

CDN Data component:

Only manages registries, deferred symlinks, SHA paths, caching, and pipeline orchestration.

No hardcoded types or transformations; it delegates actual processing to specialized modules.

Uses ServiceLoader to discover available types and transformers dynamically.

New ao-cdn-images module:

Provides image-specific transformers (resize, greyscale, ASCII-art, format conversion).

Registers supported types and transformations with CDN Data via the registry.

Handles image I/O, BufferedImage operations, and type conversions.

This keeps CDN Data lightweight, type-agnostic, and extensible, while allowing ao-cdn-images or other future modules to plug in new transformations without modifying the core CDN layer.


This is super clean.

Exactly — this design gives you:

Clear separation of concerns: core CDN logic vs. type-specific processing.

Extensibility: new transformers or resource types can be added via ServiceLoader.

Lightweight filesystem footprint: deferred symlinks and SHA-based materialization.

Safe concurrency: unique SHA paths, idempotent materialization, and optional caching.

Predictable URLs and cacheability: direct SHA URLs when materialized, deferred URLs otherwise.

It’s minimal, declarative, and scales naturally without adding complexity to the CDN nodes or webapp.


CDN component would need to know all types to know Content-Type from extension, even when materialized.  Unless: /sha.type.meta (never exposed externally, just meta for API) always has Content-Type.  Then it's possible a webapp could pre-materialize all resources, and cdn would not need to know types or transformations.  But cdn would not be able to clean/rebuild, so those pre-materialized would need to be pinned.

Exactly — two viable approaches emerge:

1. CDN knows all types
CDN maintains a Type registry, mapping file extensions → internal Type → Content-Type.

Can serve materialized resources directly and apply logic like cleanup or on-demand derivation.

CDN fully independent of webapp for both serving and maintenance.

2. CDN type-agnostic, using metadata files
Each resource has a companion metadata file:

/sha.type.meta
containing Content-Type and possibly other info.

CDN only reads the .meta file; doesn’t need to know the Type or available transformations.

Works well when webapp pre-materializes everything, because CDN just serves existing files.

Limitations:

CDN cannot clean, rebuild, or create new derivations — it’s effectively read-only.

Pre-materialized resources must be pinned to prevent accidental cleanup.

Trade-offs:

Aspect	CDN-knows-types	CDN-agnostic via .meta
Independence	Full	Limited (depends on pre-materialization)
Cleanup / rebuild	Supported	Not supported
Complexity	Slightly higher	Minimal
Flexibility (new types)	Easy	Must pre-materialize
So your .meta file approach works if you want CDN as lightweight, read-only serving layer, and the webapp guarantees pre-materialization. Otherwise, the CDN needs type awareness for full autonomy.


Deferred is required for current app.  Easiest is to require cdn to have same types and transformers.  FsckIssue on quick check for any unknown types or transformations.

Exactly — for the current app, deferred transformations are essential, so the CDN must:

Know all types and transformers to handle on-demand materialization safely.

Serve requests for deferred URLs without depending on pre-materialization.

Detect unsupported types or transformations during fsck via a FsckIssue if a resource references an unknown type or transformer.

This ensures:

Full consistency across nodes (webapp and CDN).

Safe handling of concurrent requests for deferred derivations.

Error detection during quick FSCK checks without impacting runtime serving.

It keeps the system self-contained and robust, while allowing deferred, multi-step transformations as required.


Potentially, a transformation pipeline could be optimized.  For example, /sha.jpg/greyscale.jpg the final . jpg is redundant.  It could be dropped when original image is jpg, but could remain for non-jpg original.

Similar, if want to convert any type to jpg, could: sha.type.jpg, but if happened to already be jpg, could collapse to sha.jpg.

However, actual type might not be known until materialized?  Or could we always know before?  Assume won't necessarily know type until materialized, so just do exactly what the Java API caller requests, no magic, easier to debug for them.

Exactly — for clarity and debuggability, it’s simplest to:

Do not attempt pipeline optimization based on type inference.

Even if .jpg is redundant for a JPEG source, the system keeps it in the symlink/URL.

Similarly, type conversion requests (e.g., sha.type.jpg) are honored exactly as specified.

Materialized type might not be known beforehand.

Some transformations (greyscale, resize, or abstract conversions) don’t reveal the final type until materialization.

This approach ensures:

Deterministic URLs — what the caller requests is what is executed.

Easier debugging — no hidden behavior that collapses or optimizes the pipeline.

Safe multi-step derivations — each transformation is explicit, and concurrency is easier to reason about.

So the Java API just generates the pipeline exactly as requested, and the CDN executes it step by step without trying to “guess” or optimize.


Java API user could materialize, check result type, then choose to transform again for type, if wanted to optimize.

Exactly — this keeps control in the hands of the Java API user:

Materialize the requested transformations first.

Inspect the resulting type (e.g., via metadata or returned object).

Optionally request a type conversion if they want a different output format or optimization.

Benefits:

Keeps the core system simple and deterministic — no hidden type-collapse logic in the pipeline.

Lets users optimize only when needed, e.g., avoiding redundant conversions for already matching types.

Works naturally with deferred symlinks and multi-step transformations, since each step remains explicit.

This approach maintains debuggability, predictability, and user control while still supporting optimization if the caller chooses.


One directory per resource, or one directory per hash prefix?  Will be full directory scan to find all materialized for a given transform pipeline.

This is one of the few places where filesystem layout really matters long-term. The trade-offs are pretty clear:

1. One directory per resource (per SHA)
/ab/cd/abcdef.../
/ab/cd/abcdef.../sha.jpg
/ab/cd/abcdef.../sha.jpg>greyscale
/ab/cd/abcdef.../sha.jpg>greyscale.png
Pros
All derivations for a resource are co-located
→ Your “scan for matching pipeline” becomes a single directory scan, which is exactly what you want.

Easy to reason about parent/child relationships.

FSCK is simpler: everything related to a SHA is in one place.

Cleanup (last-accessed, pinning) is localized.

Cons
If a resource has many derivations, directory size grows.

In your case (few transforms), this is negligible.

2. Directory per hash prefix (flat storage)
/ab/cd/abcdef...jpg
/ab/cd/xyz123...jpg
Pros
Uniform distribution across filesystem.

Avoids large directories for very “hot” resources.

Cons
Finding derivations requires scanning many files
→ expensive and scales poorly.

Harder to group related resources.

FSCK and cleanup become more complex (need global scans or indexing).

3. Hybrid (recommended)
Use hash prefix + per-resource directory:

/ab/cd/abcdef.../
    base.jpg
    >greyscale
    >greyscale.png
    >resize-800x600
Prefix avoids too many top-level dirs.

Per-resource dir keeps all derivations local.

Why this fits your design
Given your requirements:

Limited number of transformations

Need to scan for existing derived variants

Want simple FSCK and cleanup

No need for extreme scale (millions per single SHA)

→ Per-resource directory is the clear winner.

Performance implication
“Will be full directory scan to find all materialized for a given transform pipeline.”

That’s actually fine because:

Directory is small (dozens of entries, not millions)

Scan is O(n) but n is tiny

Avoids global indexing or database lookup

This is exactly the kind of trade-off that keeps the system simple and fast.

Conclusion
Use hash-prefix → per-SHA directory → all derivations inside.

It aligns perfectly with:

Your symlink-based allowance model

Deferred materialization

Multi-node consistency

FSCK simplicity

Low operational complexity

No indexing, no global scans, no surprises.


/ab/cd/sha/base.jpg could just be /ab/cd/sha/.jpg

Remember, . jpg is just a transformation, some transformations could work on unknown type, like sha/.md5

Special-case handling

Minimal

URL generator and parser would skip / before type, so url could be sha.jpg with fs being sha/.jpg

The . jpg could be a symlink to ../sha.  This could hint at a broader concept, like a transformation pipeline link possibly pointing to materialized sha and/or sha/.type.   But a materialized resource must always have a final type to be served.  Maybe sha/.jpg → ../sha would just be for type?-transformation-only applied directly to a sha

Yes — you’re converging on a very consistent model, but there’s an important distinction to keep it clean and avoid confusion.

1. URL vs filesystem mapping
URL:

/sha.jpg
Filesystem:

/sha/.jpg
This is just a presentation layer mapping, and it works well:

Parser inserts / before the first .

Generator removes it

No ambiguity if you enforce:

First . = type transformation

Subsequent > / . follow your pipeline rules

2. What does /sha/.jpg represent?
You proposed:

sha/.jpg → ../sha
This is where things get subtle.

Two possible meanings:
A. Type assertion only (no transformation)
.jpg means: “this SHA is already JPEG”

Symlink to ../sha makes sense

B. Type conversion
.jpg means: “encode this as JPEG”

Requires materialization, not just a link

3. Mixing these two meanings is dangerous
If .jpg sometimes:

just links (../sha)

sometimes requires transformation

→ behavior becomes ambiguous and harder to debug.

4. Cleaner rule (recommended)
Treat every .type as a real transformation, even if it’s a no-op.

So:

Case 1: Already JPEG
sha/.jpg → (materialized file, possibly identical content)
Case 2: Not JPEG
sha/.jpg → (converted JPEG file)
Implementation may optimize internally (e.g., hardlink or copy)

But semantically it is always a transformation

5. Why not symlink .jpg → ../sha?
Because it breaks consistency:

FSCK can’t distinguish “typed” vs “raw”

Content-Type becomes ambiguous

Future transformations depend on type guarantees

You lose the invariant:

“Anything with .type is a servable, typed resource”

6. Better interpretation
Think of:

sha/
  .jpg
as:

“Materialized representation of SHA in JPEG format”

Not:

“Pointer to raw SHA with a label”

7. Pipeline links concept (your broader idea)
This part is strong:

A pipeline path can resolve to either:

A materialized SHA file

Or another pipeline node

But enforce:

Final node must resolve to a typed, servable file

8. Summary model
sha → raw identity (not servable)

sha/.type → typed, servable resource

sha/.type>transform → derived resource

URLs hide the / before .type

And:

.type is always a transformation step, never just a label

Conclusion
Your idea of mapping sha.jpg → sha/.jpg is solid.
But using .jpg → ../sha as a symlink shortcut introduces ambiguity.

Better to keep the invariant:

If it has a type, it is a real, materialized representation.

That keeps:

FSCK simple

Debugging predictable

Transformation pipelines consistent

Future extensions clean

Even if internally you optimize, externally the model stays strict and easy to reason about.


Hmm.  To disambiguate: ../sha would only be for type assertion.  But there are two things indeed: type assertion and type conversation.  Need a different symbol.

Yes — that’s the right conclusion. You’ve uncovered a real semantic split:

Type assertion ≠ Type conversion

Trying to overload . for both will create ambiguity and subtle bugs.

1. Two distinct operations
Type assertion (no data change)
“This SHA already is this type”

No transformation, no re-encoding

Example:

sha = already JPEG bytes
Type conversion (data change)
“Convert this into a new type”

Requires materialization

Example:

PNG → JPEG
PDF → PNG
2. Current syntax conflict
You currently have:

sha/.jpg
But that could mean:

Assert it’s JPEG

Convert it to JPEG

→ ambiguous

3. Clean solution: separate symbols
You need two different operators:

Option (clean and minimal):
Operation	Syntax example	Meaning
Type assertion	sha=.jpg	Declare type
Type conversion	sha>.jpg	Convert to type
Then full pipeline:

sha=.jpg>resize-800x600>.png
4. Filesystem representation
/ab/cd/sha/
    =.jpg          -> ../sha         (assertion symlink)
    >.jpg          -> (materialized JPEG)
    =.jpg>resize   -> ...
= → lightweight, no materialization

> → real transformation

5. Why this works well
✔ Removes ambiguity
You always know whether bytes change

✔ Keeps your earlier invariant
Only paths ending in .type after a conversion (>) are guaranteed materialized

✔ Enables optimization safely
If:

sha=.jpg>.jpg
→ can collapse internally

✔ Supports unknown types
sha=.md5
sha>.txt
6. URL mapping
You can still keep URLs clean:

Assertion:

/sha.jpg   → sha=.jpg
Conversion:

/sha/.jpg  (or explicit path form)
Or you may choose:

Hide = entirely in URL

Treat first extension as assertion by default

7. Important invariant
Only conversion (>) produces new content
Assertion (=) never changes bytes

8. Why this is worth it
Without this split:

Hard to reason about caching

Hard to guarantee reproducibility

FSCK becomes ambiguous

Debugging pipelines becomes painful

With it:

Everything becomes explicit and composable

Conclusion
Yes — introducing a second symbol (like =) for type assertion is the right move.

Final mental model:

SHA = raw bytes
= = declare what it already is
> = transform into something new

That keeps your system precise, extensible, and easy to reason about long-term.

Your rule (reframed clearly)
Every derived symlink must resolve to a canonical typed node (sha/.type)
except when it is that node, in which case it may point to ../sha.

Why this matters
You’re preventing:

Self-referential symlinks

Cycles in pipelines

Ambiguous resolution during FS traversal / FSCK

Only place where pointing to ../sha is allowed

Example:

Itself

Example:

For any pipeline node:

Every pipeline collapses to a (sha, type) pair

All .type nodes are valid

Close, a sha/.jpg.noop.jpg would naturally end up pointing to itself.  In this case, I wouldn't even care to collapse the symlink target, it just happened to point to itself, which is valid.  Target would be ../../../ab/cd/sha/.jpg just like normal.  

Yes — in that case it’s fine. The self-pointing symlink is not logically problematic, because:

The symlink is part of the pipeline, not a raw data node.

It points to a canonical typed node (sha/.jpg) which is valid and serves content.

No GC, FSCK, or pipeline resolution logic needs to treat it specially — it behaves exactly like any other symlink to a typed SHA.

How it fits your model
Original SHA:

sha/data
sha/.jpg → data
Pipeline:

sha/.jpg.noop.jpg → ../../../ab/cd/sha/.jpg
Resolution:

Follow pipeline symlink → sha/.jpg

Follow .jpg symlink → sha/data

Serve content

✅ Deterministic

✅ No cycles (even though the symlink points “to itself” at the pipeline level, it eventually resolves to data)

Why this is okay
The symlink pointing to itself is only at the pipeline representation level.

Actual content resolution always ends at sha/data.

No infinite loops: pipelines resolve one level at a time, and each symlink always points to a canonical typed node.

Optional optimization
You could collapse it to skip the intermediate symlink, but as you said:

“I wouldn’t even care to collapse the symlink target”

Leaving it as-is is simpler, easier to debug, and still correct.

Minimal overhead: a symlink to the same node is cheap and doesn’t affect FSCK, GC, or CDN behavior.

✅ Conclusion:
Self-pointing symlinks at the pipeline level (like .jpg.noop.jpg) are valid, safe, and don’t break your invariants. No special handling required.


Hard code or configurable hashing levels?  Current auction only has around 100000 files, so single level is sufficient, assuming 16-bit hashing.

Given your current scale (≈100,000 files), a single-level hash directory structure is sufficient, assuming a decent hash distribution (like 16-bit / 2^16 ≈ 65,536 buckets). That keeps directories small and manageable.

Considerations
Single-level (/ab/cd/sha/)

Pros:

Simple, minimal filesystem nesting

Easy to implement

Fits your current volume

Cons:

Could become inefficient if a single bucket exceeds a few thousand files

Less future-proof for millions of files

Two-level or configurable hashing

Pros:

Handles huge numbers of files efficiently

Configurable for growth

Cons:

Slightly more complex path computation

Possibly unnecessary for current use

Recommendation
Hard-code single level for now (16-bit prefix like /ab/cd/sha/)

Add configurable hashing level later if you anticipate scaling into millions of files

You could keep the code generic:

Path resolveSha(String sha) {
    String prefix1 = sha.substring(0, 2);
    String prefix2 = sha.substring(2, 4); // optional second level
    return Paths.get(root, prefix1, prefix2, sha);
}
For 100,000 files, just /ab/cd/sha/ is sufficient — no second level needed yet.

This gives you simplicity now, with an upgrade path if storage grows.

Exactly — that’s the key advantage of using ext4 with HTree directory indexing:

Lookups are O(1) for individual entries once the directory is indexed.

Even a directory with millions of files is handled efficiently, because ext4 doesn’t do linear scans.

/abcd/ contains at most 65,536 subdirectories (first 4 hex chars of SHA)

No risk of huge directories

Your /abcd/sha/ single-level prefix design is safe, scalable, and simple, leveraging ext4’s built-in indexing to potentially handle billions of files if needed. No additional hashing layers are necessary.
