Reciprocal symlinks: each materialized pipeline has two links.  Need some more symbols.    
    
"shaA/.jpg>greyscale→shaB" points to symlink target "../../shaB/.jpg"
shaB here is important, can be multiple versions for a single pipeline   
    
Backlink:    
"shaB/.jpg←sgaA.jpg>greyscale" points to symlink target "../../shaA/.jpg"

Yes — that creates a two-way mapping between pipeline definitions and materialized results, which is very useful for GC, FSCK, and bookkeeping. Using distinct symbols for forward/backward links makes it explicit and unambiguous.

Proposed notation
Forward pipeline symlink

shaA/.jpg>greyscale → ../../shaB/.jpg
Meaning: “Materialize this pipeline starting from shaA/.jpg via greyscale; result is shaB/.jpg”

Allows multiple shaB versions for the same pipeline.

Backlink (reciprocal)

shaB/.jpg←shaA.jpg>greyscale → ../../shaA/.jpg
Meaning: “This materialized result came from shaA/.jpg via greyscale”

Provides reverse mapping for cleanup and GC: if shaB/.jpg is removed, you can check which pipeline(s) depended on it.

Implementation notes
Use distinct arrows (→ for forward, ← for backlink) in filenames to avoid collisions.

Symlinks always point to typed nodes (.type) rather than directories.

Multiple materializations: each shaB gets its own backlink pointing to its pipeline origin.

Example structure:

shaA/
  .jpg
  .jpg>greyscale → ../../shaB/.jpg

shaB/
  .jpg
  .jpg←shaA.jpg>greyscale → ../../shaA/.jpg
Forward symlink lets CDN resolve pipeline → materialization

Backlink lets GC/FSCK find origins → prune safely

Benefits
Multiple materializations supported

Each result has a backlink to its source SHA/pipeline.

Safe cleanup

Check backlinks before removing source or materialized data.

Deterministic navigation

Forward: pipeline → result

Backward: result → originating pipeline

No ambiguity

Arrow direction explicitly encodes relationship

This approach maintains your acyclic structure at the data level while allowing multiple derivations per pipeline.

If you want, I can sketch the full naming convention for all symlink types, including type assertions, pipeline forwards, and backlinks, in a single unified schema. This would make implementation straightforward.


There's a catch.  Some transformations can use the original data directly, untyped, like md5 checksum.  So our invariants were incorrect somewhat.

Ah — yes, that changes things slightly. The earlier invariants assumed all transformations work on typed nodes (sha/.type), but if some transformations can operate directly on the raw data (sha/data) without a type, you need to adjust the model.

Adjusted invariants
Typed nodes (sha/.type)

Most transformations still operate on these.

Pipeline symlinks continue to point to .type or to materialized typed nodes.

Raw data node (sha/data)

Some transformations (like md5, sha1, or other content hashes) can operate directly on data, bypassing type.

Pipelines that start from raw data must point to sha/data instead of sha/.type.

Forward pipeline symlinks

shaA/.jpg>greyscale → ../../shaB/.jpg         # typed input
shaA/md5 → ../../shaB/.md5                     # raw input
The arrow still points to the resulting materialized node.

Backlinks

shaB/.jpg←shaA.jpg>greyscale → ../../shaA/.jpg
shaB/.md5←shaA.md5 → ../../shaA/data
Must indicate whether the source was typed or raw, so GC and FSCK know what was used.

Materialization rules

If transformation requires a type: must use sha/.type

If transformation can work untyped: may use sha/data

Implications
GC and reciprocal links:

Need to handle both .type → .type and data → result.

Reciprocal symlinks still work; just distinguish whether the source was typed or raw.

Pipeline resolution:

When resolving a pipeline, check whether the first node is typed or raw.

Follow symlink as usual.

URL layer:

External URLs can still reference pipelines normally.

Users do not need to know if the source was raw or typed.

Summary
The main change: allow raw-data pipelines for transformations that do not require type.

Everything else — forward symlink, backlink, multiple materializations, GC — still works.

Your invariant “all pipelines point to .type” needs to be relaxed to:

“Pipeline symlinks point to either typed node (.type) or raw data (data), depending on transformation requirements.”

This keeps the system fully consistent while supporting untyped transformations.

If you want, I can redraw the full forward/backlink symlink schema including raw-data transformations so it’s crystal clear.


Would the type of md5 be txt?

shaA/md5 points to shaB/.txt, where shaB is the SGA of the MD5 of the data associated with shaA

Exactly — in this case, the MD5 transformation outputs a typed result, even though the transformation itself can start from raw data:

Example
Original SHA: shaA

Raw data: shaA/data

(Optional typed node: shaA/.jpg)

MD5 pipeline:

shaA/md5 → ../../shaB/.txt
shaB is the SHA of the MD5 hash string itself (content-addressed).

Typed node: shaB/.txt

Data: shaB/data contains the MD5 string (e.g., "e2c569be17396eca2a2e3c11578123ed")

Backlink:

shaB/.txt←shaA/md5 → ../../shaA/data
This explicitly records that the MD5 result came from the raw data of shaA.

Key points
Input can be raw or typed

Some transformations (like MD5) don’t care about type → can operate on sha/data.

Output always has a type

MD5 is text → type .txt

Any pipeline result should always materialize as a typed node (.type)

Symlinks remain consistent

Forward symlink: shaA/md5 → shaB/.txt

Backlink: shaB/.txt←shaA/md5 → shaA/data

Multiple materializations supported

If MD5 transformation is parameterized (e.g., uppercase/lowercase, salted), each result gets its own shaB and backlinks.

✅ Conclusion

Even for raw-data transformations, the result should always be a typed node.

Backlinks record whether the source was raw (data) or typed (.type).

Your symlink schema still works without modification, just distinguish raw vs typed in backlinks.

In a hypothetical scenario where the API user generates a 1x1 thumbnail, many images would point to very few images.  Each thumbnail would have possibly many backlinks.  Can be a many-to-few relationship.

Exactly — in that scenario, your backlink system naturally handles many-to-few relationships:

Scenario
API user requests a 1x1 thumbnail for hundreds or thousands of original images.

All of these pipelines may materialize to the same result SHA, e.g., shaTiny/.jpg

Forward symlinks (one per source):

shaA/.jpg>resize-1x1 → ../../shaTiny/.jpg
shaB/.jpg>resize-1x1 → ../../shaTiny/.jpg
shaC/.jpg>resize-1x1 → ../../shaTiny/.jpg
...
Backlinks (many pointing to the same result):

shaTiny/.jpg←shaA.jpg>resize-1x1 → ../../shaA/.jpg
shaTiny/.jpg←shaB.jpg>resize-1x1 → ../../shaB/.jpg
shaTiny/.jpg←shaC.jpg>resize-1x1 → ../../shaC/.jpg
...
Many sources → single materialized thumbnail

Backlinks record all origins, allowing safe cleanup: you know exactly which sources depend on this shared result.

Implications
GC / cleanup

You only remove shaTiny/.jpg when no more backlinks exist.

Ensures shared results are not prematurely deleted.

Efficient storage

Many images can share the same materialized result (deduplication).

No extra storage cost for common outputs like 1x1 thumbnails.

FS / symlink scaling

Backlink symlinks may accumulate, but symlinks are lightweight.

Ext4 handles tens of thousands of symlinks per directory without issue.

Pipeline resolution

Forward lookup: each source SHA knows which materialized result to use.

Backward lookup: each materialized SHA knows all sources depending on it.

This is exactly the kind of many-to-few relationship your reciprocal symlink model was designed to support, and it works without any modification.

Forward: pipeline → materialized result

Backward: materialized result → all pipeline origins

It scales naturally, even when many images collapse into a small set of derived results.


No normal operation requires full scan of entire CDN

Correct — in normal operation, you never need a full scan of the CDN. All lookups and operations are deterministic and local:

Normal operations
Materialization / pipeline resolution

Start from a known SHA (or URL) → compute /abcd/sha/ path → follow forward symlinks if needed.

Only touch the directories relevant to that SHA and its pipeline.

Serving content

Request URL maps to a pipeline → resolve forward symlink → serve materialized .type.

No traversal beyond the SHA directories involved.

GC / cleanup

Only scan directories for the specific SHA whose backlinks are being removed.

Reciprocal symlinks let you quickly identify dependencies.

FSCK / integrity check

Can operate per-SHA or per-prefix (/abcd/)

No need to touch unrelated SHA directories.

Implications
O(1) or O(small N) operations per request, even if total CDN contains millions of files.

FS and symlink structure ensures all dependencies are local to the SHA directories.

Full scans are only needed for rare auditing or repair, not for routine operation.

This keeps normal operation extremely fast and predictable, regardless of total CDN size.


Deadlock potential: loop or inverse resource locking order between different sha difectories.  Possible workaround is backlinks are manipulated under lock of shaA, at least when shaB already exists.  This locking will require careful analysis.  Releasing locks strategically could work.

Yes — that’s a real concern. When manipulating reciprocal symlinks across different SHA directories, you can run into deadlocks if two threads try to acquire locks in conflicting orders.

Scenario
Thread 1: materializes pipeline shaA → shaB

Locks shaA to add forward symlink

Locks shaB to add backlink

Thread 2: simultaneously materializes pipeline shaC → shaA

Locks shaC

Tries to lock shaA → deadlock if Thread 1 hasn’t released shaA

Safe strategies
Lock ordering by SHA

Always acquire locks in lexicographical order of SHA (or some consistent hash order)

Ensures no cycles in lock acquisition

Lock only what is necessary

Forward symlink: lock shaA (source)

Backlink: lock shaB (destination) only if it exists

If shaB doesn’t exist yet, create it without backlink first, then add backlinks after

Use fine-grained, short-lived locks

Acquire, modify, release immediately

Minimize window for contention

Transactional approach (optional)

Build symlink changes in a temporary structure

Atomically swap in once all locks are acquired

Fallback: retry on conflict

If a lock cannot be acquired immediately, back off and retry

Combined with consistent lock ordering, this prevents deadlock while allowing high concurrency

Recommendation
Primary: enforce consistent lock order across SHA directories

Secondary: keep lock scope minimal and short-lived

Tertiary: fallback retries if lock contention occurs

This ensures safe concurrent pipeline materialization, even with many-to-few relationships and reciprocal backlinks, without risking deadlocks.

If you want, I can sketch a concrete lock sequence for forward/backlink creation showing how to avoid deadlocks in all cases.


Idea: separate locks:
1) for data and direct type assertion: lock data file itself.
2) for a pipeline: have pipeline be empty or non-empty file, lock on this file for symlink and backlink access and updates.

Yes — that’s a very clean way to minimize lock scope and avoid cross-directory deadlocks. It effectively separates concerns and keeps operations fine-grained.

Proposed scheme
1. Data / type assertion lock
Lock target: sha/data (or sha/.type if asserting type)

Purpose: ensures safe writes to the original raw data or type assertion

Scope: very short; only while writing or validating the file

Notes: doesn’t involve any other SHA directories → no deadlock risk

2. Pipeline lock
Lock target: a dedicated file in shaA/ for the pipeline, e.g.,

shaA/.jpg>resize-800x600.lock
or reuse the pipeline file itself if non-empty

Purpose: controls creation/update of:

forward symlink (shaA → shaB)

backlink (shaB ← shaA)

Scope: only while reading/modifying symlinks

Benefit: lock is local to the source SHA, not both source and target directories

Workflow example
Materializing pipeline shaA/.jpg → shaB/.jpg

lock(shaA/.jpg>resize-800x600)        # pipeline lock
if shaB exists:
    lock(shaB/.jpg←shaA.jpg>resize)   # optional, very short
create/update forward symlink in shaA
create/update backlink in shaB
release locks
Type assertion / raw data:

lock(shaA/data or shaA/.jpg)          # data/type lock
write data or assert type
release lock
Advantages
Deadlock avoidance

Locks are fine-grained and mostly per-directory

No global multi-directory locks unless necessary

Concurrency-friendly

Different pipelines touching unrelated SHA directories can proceed simultaneously

Simplicity

Two lock types: data/type and pipeline

Easy to reason about for FSCK and GC

Flexible

Can extend for multiple transformations per SHA without changing lock semantics

This design effectively isolates raw data access from pipeline graph management, which reduces both contention and deadlock risk.

If you want, I can draw a small diagram showing the lock layers and symlink updates to make this fully visual.


This would mean that detecting all backlinks, which is a rare clean-up type operation, could scan shaB/ directory without any lock, then lock each back referenced shaA for verification or manipulation.

Exactly — that’s the cleanest approach for rare operations like full backlink inspection or GC:

Backlink scan workflow
Scan shaB/ directory without locks

List all backlinks (shaB/.jpg←shaA.jpg>...)

Read the target of each symlink to identify originating SHA (shaA)

Lock each source SHA (shaA) individually

Acquire pipeline lock on shaA corresponding to this pipeline

Verify or update the forward symlink / backlink

Release lock immediately

Optional materialized node check

If needed, check shaB/.type or shaB/data under short data/type lock

Advantages
No deadlocks

Locks are acquired one at a time, always per source SHA (shaA)

Scan itself is lock-free → no global blocking

Safe verification / cleanup

Ensures you never manipulate a pipeline or backlink concurrently with another operation on the same source SHA

Efficient

Only touch SHA directories that actually have backlinks to shaB

Extremely lightweight even for many-to-few scenarios (e.g., 1x1 thumbnails)

Concurrency-friendly

Other pipelines can materialize or create new backlinks while scan is ongoing, as long as they respect the pipeline lock on their source SHA

Summary
Forward pipeline locks: per-source SHA

Backlink scan: no initial lock, acquire per-source SHA locks only when needed

Data/type locks: per SHA, short-lived, for writes

This keeps rare cleanup operations safe, deterministic, and fully decoupled from normal high-concurrency materialization requests.

If you want, I can sketch a visual flow showing scan → per-source lock → verification/update → release for clarity.


Our cleanup code would have complicated checks where concurrent updates would invalidate its decisions mid process.  For example when detecting a loop if resources that can all be removed.  Need a global lock, too?   Multi read, exclusive write?

Yes — for complex cleanup operations like loop detection or multi-resource GC, relying only on per-SHA locks is not sufficient. You need a higher-level coordination mechanism to prevent concurrent updates from invalidating your decisions mid-process.

Options
1. Global lock for cleanup
Acquire a global “cleanup” lock before starting a multi-resource analysis.

Ensures that no other materialization or pipeline update can modify forward/backlinks while cleanup runs.

Pros: simple, safe, prevents inconsistent state.

Cons: blocks all concurrent writes during cleanup → could be heavy if cleanup is long.

2. Multi-read / exclusive-write lock (RW lock)
Normal operations (materialization, forward/backlink updates): acquire read lock

Cleanup / loop detection / GC: acquire write lock

Behavior:

Multiple readers can proceed concurrently

Writer (cleanup) blocks until all readers finish, and blocks new readers until done

Pros: allows high concurrency during normal operation, only blocks during rare cleanup

Cons: more complex to implement correctly across distributed or multi-threaded context

Recommended approach
Per-SHA locks for normal materialization → fast, fine-grained, avoids deadlock.

Global cleanup lock → only for rare GC/loop detection:

Acquire exclusive lock (write) before scanning for loops or safe deletions.

Release as soon as decisions are made or changes applied.

Can combine with per-SHA pipeline locks if you want extra safety.

Effectively, you separate high-frequency operations (materialization) from low-frequency, complex operations (cleanup).

Implementation notes
Lock file can live at root of CDN storage, e.g., /lock.cleanup

Use standard OS file locking (flock or FileChannel.lock)

Optionally, timeout or retry to prevent deadlocks if some thread crashes during cleanup

This way, your loop detection / GC logic can safely reason about multi-resource states without worrying that concurrent updates invalidate its assumptions.

If you want, I can propose a full locking hierarchy combining per-SHA locks + pipeline locks + global cleanup lock, showing safe orderings to prevent deadlocks entirely.


Thinking this through, there is a bit of complexity once I flesh it out.

Exactly — once you factor in:

Reciprocal symlinks (forward/backlink)

Raw vs typed inputs

Multiple materializations per pipeline

Many-to-few relationships (e.g., shared thumbnails)

Concurrent updates across SHA directories

Complex cleanup operations (loop detection, GC)

…it becomes clear that the system is non-trivial to implement safely.

Key sources of complexity:

Deadlock potential

Multiple threads acquiring locks on different SHA directories

Pipeline + backlink creation can cross directories

Consistency during cleanup

Concurrent updates can invalidate decisions mid-process

Loop detection requires a snapshot or exclusive access

Locking hierarchy

Per-data / type lock

Per-pipeline lock

Global cleanup lock (rare)

Must define consistent acquisition order to prevent cycles

Materialization vs cleanup interplay

Materialization must respect locks taken by cleanup

Cleanup must safely scan and update without blocking normal ops too long

The upside: with well-defined lock scopes and hierarchies, plus lightweight per-SHA locks for most operations, you can:

Keep normal materializations fast and concurrent

Handle rare, complex cleanup safely

Support multi-stage pipelines and many-to-few relationships without corruption

It’s definitely manageable, but requires careful design and disciplined implementation.

Fsck could be done with fine grained locking.

Yes — FSCK (filesystem check / integrity verification) can generally be done with fine-grained, per-SHA or per-pipeline locks, rather than a global lock, because:

Why fine-grained works for FSCK
Locality of checks

Each SHA directory contains its own data (data), typed nodes (.type), and forward/backlinks.

Most FSCK operations are confined to a single SHA or its immediate pipeline links.

Minimal interference

While checking shaB, you only need to lock the pipeline files in source SHA directories (shaA) if you want to verify or fix backlinks.

Other unrelated SHA directories can continue normal operations concurrently.

Deadlock prevention

Acquire locks one at a time, in consistent order (lexical order of SHA or pipeline file)

Avoids cycles and ensures FSCK doesn’t deadlock with ongoing materializations.

Optional snapshotting

FSCK can also tolerate slight staleness in backlinks if only reading for verification

Only acquire locks when planning modifications (e.g., broken symlinks or cleanup)

Typical FSCK workflow with fine-grained locks
Scan shaB/ directory for all forward/backlinks without locks

For each problematic link or pipeline:

Lock the source SHA (shaA) pipeline file

Verify forward/backlink consistency

Apply fixes if needed

Release lock immediately

Move to next SHA / pipeline

Advantages
Highly concurrent: only touch directories involved in the check

Scales to millions of files without blocking normal operations

Safe: locks only when necessary, ensuring integrity while allowing normal reads/writes elsewhere

This approach keeps FSCK lightweight, deterministic, and compatible with your reciprocal symlink and multi-stage pipeline model.

If you want, I can sketch a diagram showing FSCK traversal, locks, and symlink verification — it makes the process very clear.


Initial release would not need clean-up at all. Global lock could be added later.

Exactly — for an initial release, you can safely skip cleanup and global locks:

Normal operation (materialization, serving, forward/backlinks) works entirely with per-SHA and per-pipeline locks.

No need for FSCK, loop detection, or GC on first deployment.

Reciprocal symlinks and pipeline structure still fully support deduplication and multi-stage transformations.

Advantages for initial release:

Simpler implementation — fewer moving parts and no global coordination needed.

Fast rollout — focus on core functionality (materialization, URL resolution, transformations).

Safe concurrency — per-directory locks handle all in-flight operations.

Extensible — global cleanup, FSCK, and loop detection can be added later without changing normal operations.

This is a practical way to get the system working quickly while preserving the full design for future robustness.


Our previous implementation has uploader and non uploader modes, webapp is uploaded, cdn is not.  It affects which cleanups can occur during startup and fsck, such as removing files in upload directory.

Yes — the uploader vs non-uploader distinction is important for cleanup and FSCK logic:

Implications
Uploader mode (webapp)

Can safely remove or rebuild files in the upload directory because it controls input.

Can perform startup cleanups (e.g., temporary files, incomplete uploads).

FSCK can be more aggressive — fixing broken forward/backlinks, removing unreferenced materializations.

Non-uploader mode (CDN)

Must never remove original uploads — it only serves content or materializes pipelines.

Cleanup is limited to deferred or intermediate symlinks within its local cache or materializations.

FSCK can verify integrity, but cannot remove upstream user content.

Practical approach
Introduce a mode flag in startup/config: uploader = true/false.

At startup and during FSCK:

Uploader: safe to clean upload directories, orphaned raw files, incomplete materializations.

Non-uploader: read-only on upstream content; only clean what is fully local and derivable (e.g., symlink caches, temporary pipeline materializations).

Reciprocal symlinks and pipeline logic remain the same; only allowed cleanup scope differs.

This cleanly separates responsibilities: the CDN remains safe and read-only, while the webapp can manage source content and perform aggressive cleanup when necessary.

You could also enforce this in code via a canDeleteSource check whenever FSCK or cleanup is about to remove anything.

Lock on sha/data itself, not a dedicated lock file?

Yes — locking sha/data itself is a practical approach and often simpler than creating a separate lock file.

Advantages
No extra files

Avoids cluttering the directory with dedicated lock files.

Every SHA directory already has data (or .type) — natural lock target.

Atomic and OS-supported

Standard file locking (flock on Unix, FileChannel.lock in Java) works directly on existing files.

Guarantees exclusive access while writing or asserting type.

Fine-grained

Locking sha/data affects only operations on that SHA.

No risk of interfering with unrelated SHA directories.

Consistency with pipeline locks

You can still have separate pipeline locks for forward/backlink symlink operations.

Keeps data write/validation separate from pipeline graph updates.

Implementation notes
Read locks if just reading/verifying data (optional).

Write/exclusive lock while writing or asserting type.

Short-lived — acquire, perform operation, release immediately.

✅ Using sha/data as the lock target is clean, lightweight, and aligns with your existing per-SHA locking design.

It keeps the system simple while maintaining safety for concurrent operations.


Once again, we share characteristics with other things like IPFS.  What about no-SQL options?  Standard sharded distributed storage, Redis?

Yes — conceptually, your design shares several characteristics with IPFS and other content-addressable storage systems:

Content-addressed: SHA-based identifiers

Immutable core objects: original sha/data never changes

Deduplication: multiple pipelines/materializations can share the same result

Graph relationships: forward/backlinks resemble Merkle DAG edges

NoSQL / distributed options
Key-value stores (Redis, RocksDB, DynamoDB, etc.)

Key: SHA or pipeline ID

Value: metadata, type, symlink targets, optional materialized data pointer

Pros: fast lookups, built-in sharding (Redis Cluster, DynamoDB)

Cons: large binary objects may not fit well (need external blob storage for raw data)

Sharded distributed file systems (HDFS, Ceph, MinIO)

Store sha/data and materialized results in sharded directories

Metadata can be stored in a small database or embedded in object keys

Pros: scalable to billions of files, handles replication

Cons: heavier infrastructure, less simple for low-latency serving

Hybrid approach

Raw binary content in distributed object store

Forward/backlink metadata, pipelines, types in NoSQL key-value store

Advantages:

Fast metadata queries without scanning directories

Data still deduplicated and content-addressed

Can scale horizontally without moving entire filesystem

Considerations vs current FS-based design
Aspect	FS + symlinks	NoSQL / distributed
Simplicity	Very simple, lightweight	More moving parts
Concurrency	OS handles locks, per-file	Need distributed locking / transactions
Scale	Millions of files per FS	Potentially billions, sharded
Deduplication	Native via SHA	Must implement in store
Materialization	Immediate symlinks	Need metadata mapping
Recovery / FSCK	Easy to scan locally	Must scan keys in DB or maintain graph
Conclusion:

Your current FS + symlink model is lightweight and fast for a single-server or moderate-scale deployment.

Moving to a NoSQL or distributed store makes sense if you need:

Multi-node horizontal scaling

Automated sharding

Global deduplication across nodes

Otherwise, the FS approach is simple, transparent, and well-aligned with your reciprocal symlink model.

If you want, I can sketch a hybrid architecture showing how the current symlink + SHA design could map to a distributed key-value / object store system. This often clarifies scalability trade-offs.


We maintain Redis for a client.  It's a bit much.

Right — introducing Redis (or another NoSQL store) just for this CDN pipeline would likely be overkill.

You already have a content-addressable FS, with SHA deduplication, per-SHA directories, symlinks for pipelines, and lightweight locks.

Redis would only accelerate metadata lookups, but your current per-SHA directory structure already gives O(1) access for forward/backlinks within a single SHA.

Adding Redis adds extra infrastructure, operational overhead, and failure modes, which may not be justified unless you need cross-node sharding or extremely high metadata concurrency.

Bottom line:

Stick with filesystem + symlinks + fine-grained locks for now.

Keep Redis in mind only if scaling to multiple CDN nodes or billions of files, where FS indexing and locks could become a bottleneck.

This aligns with your goal for a clean, simple, extensible initial release.


If we had a sha256 transformation, it would symlink to the sha of the textual form of the original sha.  Meta

Exactly — a sha256 transformation is just another pipeline that produces a derived resource, and in your model it fits naturally as a meta transformation:

Example
Original resource: shaA/data (binary content)

Compute textual SHA-256 of the original SHA:

shaA/.sha256 → ../../shaB/.txt
shaB is the SHA of the textual SHA ("abcdef1234...")

Type is .txt (since it’s text representation)

Forward/backlinks work the same as normal pipelines:

Forward: shaA/.sha256 → ../../shaB/.txt
Backlink: shaB/.txt ← shaA/.sha256 → ../../shaA/.data
Many pipelines can reference the same meta SHA, just like any other many-to-few relationship.

Notes
This works even if the original SHA is unknown type or intermediate.

It’s fully compatible with reciprocal symlinks, pipeline materialization, and GC rules.

You can apply further transformations on the meta SHA if needed (e.g., compress the textual SHA representation).

Essentially, meta-transformations are first-class citizens in your pipeline model — they don’t require special handling beyond ensuring a proper type for the derived result.

If you like, I can outline a general rule for representing meta vs content transformations in your symlink/pipeline structure, so all future meta operations fit seamlessly.


Pipeline can be arbitrary.  What if a pipeline needs multiple inputs?

Exactly — multi-input pipelines introduce a new dimension, because your current model assumes one source SHA → one derived SHA. You can extend it carefully while preserving symlink and reciprocal logic.

Multi-input pipeline concept
Inputs: shaA, shaB, … shaN

Derived output: shaX

Pipeline key: must encode all inputs and transformations in a deterministic, order-sensitive way.

Example: an overlay transformation:

shaA/.jpg + shaB/.png > overlay-50pct → shaX/.jpg
Forward symlinks from each input SHA:

shaA/.jpg>overlay-50pct → ../../shaX/.jpg
shaB/.png>overlay-50pct → ../../shaX/.jpg
Backlinks on derived SHA:

shaX/.jpg←shaA.jpg>overlay-50pct → ../../shaA/.jpg
shaX/.jpg←shaB.png>overlay-50pct → ../../shaB/.png
Considerations
Deterministic key

Pipeline representation must include all inputs and transformation parameters

Order may matter (e.g., overlay A onto B is not the same as B onto A)

Locking

Multi-input pipelines need consistent lock order to avoid deadlocks:

Acquire locks on input SHAs sorted by SHA

Lock derived SHA only if writing materialized result

GC / cleanup

Backlinks from derived SHA now point to multiple sources

Cleanup can remove shaX only when all backlinks are gone

Meta and text transformations

Works the same: multi-input meta pipelines produce a SHA of combined inputs

Filesystem implications

Each input still has a forward symlink to the derived SHA

Derived SHA contains backlinks to all inputs

Summary
Multi-input pipelines fit naturally in the forward/backlink model.

Forward symlinks: from each input to derived SHA

Backlinks: from derived SHA to all inputs

Lock acquisition: always sorted by SHA to avoid deadlocks

GC: only delete derived SHA when no backlinks remain

If you want, I can draw a small diagram showing 3-input pipeline symlinks — it makes the multi-input relationships very clear.


Multi not required for this client, nor is multi-step pipeline, just image scaling.

Perfect — that keeps things much simpler for your current client:

Single-input, single-step pipelines are sufficient.

Forward/backlinks and symlinks remain one-to-one (or many-to-one for shared derived images like thumbnails).

No need to handle multi-input locking, complex pipeline keys, or order-dependent transformations.

Scaling images alone fits neatly into the existing per-SHA directory + per-pipeline symlink model.

This allows the initial release to be clean, fast, and easy to maintain, while keeping the system extensible for multi-step or multi-input pipelines if needed in the future.


Would need url and fs encoding for multiple pipeline, possiblity arbitrarily nestable: shaA/{.jpg,shaB}>xor>md5

Yes — if you ever support multi-step or multi-input pipelines, you’ll need a clear URL and filesystem encoding scheme to represent arbitrary nesting. Here’s a structured way to handle it:

URL Encoding
Single input, single transformation (current case)

shaA.jpg>resize-800x600
Forward slash can still separate transformations if desired:

shaA.jpg>greyscale>resize-800x600
Multi-input / nested pipelines

Represent multiple inputs in {} or another safe delimiter:

shaA{.jpg,shaB} > xor > md5
Deterministic ordering of inputs is essential for reproducibility: sort by SHA or filename.

Transformation parameters

Keep them in the same -param syntax after >:

shaA.jpg>resize-800x600>greyscale
Type assertions

Still appended as .type at the end if forcing output type:

shaA{.jpg,shaB}>xor>md5.txt
Filesystem Encoding
Pipeline directory structure

Base SHA directory: shaA/

Each transformation stage can be a symlink pointing to derived SHA/.type:

shaA/.jpg>xor>md5 → ../../shaX/.txt
Multi-input forward symlinks: each input points to the derived SHA:

shaA/.jpg>xor → ../../shaX/.txt
shaB/.jpg>xor → ../../shaX/.txt
Nested transformations

Treat each stage as a pipeline segment in the symlink filename:

shaA/.jpg>resize-800x600>greyscale → ../../shaY/.jpg
The filesystem doesn’t need recursive directories per pipeline stage; all stages are encoded in the symlink name.

Backlinks

Derived SHA keeps backlinks to all sources:

shaY/.jpg←shaA.jpg>resize-800x600>greyscale
Multiple inputs just produce multiple backlinks to the same derived SHA.

Key Rules for Multi-Pipeline Encoding
Deterministic ordering of inputs ({shaA,shaB}) to avoid ambiguity.

Transformation names and parameters clearly separated (>).

Final type always explicit if known, otherwise deferred.

Symlink targets always point to sha/.type of the derived SHA.

No loops — avoid self-referential symlinks unless valid noop transformations.

✅ This approach scales to arbitrary depth and multi-input pipelines without creating a nested directory structure per transformation stage.

If you want, I can draw a small example of a 2-input, 2-stage nested pipeline, showing both URL and FS encoding with forward/backlinks — it makes this concrete.


Stopping here on this idea.  It would be a logical extension of the single input pipelines

Exactly — keeping the focus on single-input, single-step pipelines keeps the system simple and fully functional for your client.

All the multi-input or nested pipeline ideas are just logical extensions, and can be added later if needed. For now, the design you’ve settled on — per-SHA directories, per-pipeline symlinks, reciprocal backlinks, and per-SHA locks — is sufficient and clean.


Now, do we track original filenames from uploader(s), or generate filename as sha.type always?

For simplicity and consistency, it’s usually best to always generate filenames as sha.type in your storage layer.

Reasons to prefer sha.type
Uniqueness guaranteed

SHA ensures no collisions, even if multiple users upload files with the same name.

Simpler pipeline and symlink logic

Forward/backlinks, deferred transformations, and type assertions all work naturally with sha.type

Avoids filesystem conflicts

Original filenames may contain characters that are unsafe in FS or URLs

Deterministic materialization

Any derived pipeline always maps cleanly to a SHA-based path: sha/.type → shaX/.type

When to track original filenames
Optional metadata: store in a separate JSON or database alongside the SHA, e.g.:

{
  "sha": "abcdef1234...",
  "original_filename": "cow_photo.jpg",
  "upload_timestamp": "2026-04-02T14:23:00Z",
  "uploader_id": "user123"
}
This keeps all storage deterministic and content-addressed, while still allowing the webapp to present the original name to the user.

✅ Recommendation:

Filesystem / CDN: always use sha.type

Optional metadata layer: store original filenames and other info if needed for display or logging

This keeps the design clean, deterministic, and fully compatible with your symlink + pipeline system.


We don't track in db.

In that case, the cleanest approach is to forego storing original filenames entirely and always use sha.type as the canonical name:

Filesystem / CDN storage: sha.type (e.g., abcdef1234...jpg)

Webapp presentation: can display a generic label or require uploader to provide a display name at upload time if needed; the original filename is not retained.

Metadata for transformations: derived pipelines, type assertions, and reciprocal symlinks rely only on SHA and type, so nothing is lost.

This keeps the system fully deterministic, simple, and self-contained, and avoids introducing any additional state management.


Image scaling pipeline reuse original exact bytes when scaling chooses same resolution and type.  This can happen when asking to scale to size already is.  Benefit is no recreating jpeg and losing quality.

Exactly — that’s a very practical optimization. In your pipeline:

Behavior
Request: scale shaA.jpg to 800×600, output type .jpg

Check: does the requested output exactly match the source bytes?

Same resolution

Same output type

Optional: same quality/compression parameters

If yes:

Reuse original shaA.jpg bytes

Symlink the pipeline to shaA/.jpg without re-encoding

If no:

Perform scaling and/or type conversion

Store as a new SHA

Benefits
No quality loss from repeated JPEG encoding

Saves CPU and I/O for materialization

Symlink logic remains consistent: forward/backlinks point to the SHA holding the bytes

Implementation notes
Pipeline materialization can first compare parameters against source SHA:

Resolution

Output type

Optional quality or other transformation flags

If identical, simply point derived symlink to original SHA’s .type

This is a safe, deterministic optimization and fits perfectly into your current symlink + per-SHA directory model. It also naturally handles “noop” transformations like scaling an image to its current size.


But scale down by 50% then back up by 200% should not use original image data.

Exactly — in that case, the bytes have changed, so the pipeline must materialize a new SHA.

Rule: only reuse the original bytes if the resulting image would be bit-for-bit identical to the source.

Scaling down and then up (even if dimensions return to the original) produces different pixel data and compression artifacts, so reusing the original would be incorrect.

Your pipeline logic can handle this by:

Checking requested output resolution vs source resolution

Checking requested type vs source type

Only shortcutting to the original SHA if both match exactly

All other transformations — even seemingly reversible ones — must generate a new SHA. This ensures content-addressable correctness and avoids subtle corruption or misidentification.


Initial release: single transformation.

Perfect — for the initial release, you only need:

Single-input, single-step pipelines (e.g., sha.jpg>resize-800x600)

Forward/backlink symlinks per derived SHA

Per-SHA locks for materialization

Reusing original bytes only when output exactly matches input

No cleanup, no multi-input pipelines, no nested transformations

Everything else — multi-step, multi-input, FSCK, global locks — can be deferred for future extensions.

This keeps the system clean, deterministic, and easy to implement, while fully supporting the client’s current image scaling needs.

JSON, XML, Java serialization, other?

For this use case, JSON is the best choice.

Options evaluated

}
Parsing logic
Simple and robust:

if (!json.contains("version")) {
    version = 1;
} else {
    version = json.getInt("version");
}
Why this works well
Backward compatible — old files remain valid

No migration required for existing data

Minimal upfront complexity

Future changes can be handled explicitly per version

Important constraint
Once you release:

Never change semantics of version 1

Only add new behavior in higher versions

Keep version 1 parser stable forever

Conclusion
Yes — omit version now

Treat missing version as v1 later

Add "version" only when you actually need it

This keeps the initial system simple while leaving a clean upgrade path.


If using pipeline definition file as lock, how to both lock and do atomic writes of new file content?

Good question — this is one of the subtle parts.

You want all three properties:

Locking (mutual exclusion)

Atomic visibility (no partial writes)

Safe creation/update

Using the same file for both lock + data requires a careful pattern.

Recommended pattern: lock + temp + atomic rename
Step-by-step
Assume pipeline file:

sha/.jpg|pipe-id
1. Open (or create) and lock the target file
FileChannel ch = FileChannel.open(path,
    CREATE, WRITE, READ);
FileLock lock = ch.lock(); // exclusive
This is your mutex

Works even if file is empty or newly created

2. Write to a temporary file
sha/.jpg|pipe-id.tmp
Write full JSON content to .tmp

fsync it if you want durability guarantees

3. Atomic rename
Files.move(tmp, path, ATOMIC_MOVE, REPLACE_EXISTING);
This is the critical step

Guarantees:

No partial file ever visible

Readers see either old or new content

4. Release lock
lock.release();
ch.close();
Why this works
Lock prevents concurrent writers

Temp file avoids partial writes

Atomic rename ensures consistency

Even if process crashes:

.tmp may remain → harmless

main file remains valid

Important nuance (very important)
Locks do NOT follow rename
You lock the original file path

After move(), the file contents are replaced

Lock still applies because:

you hold the file descriptor

other writers also lock via path before writing

This is safe as long as:

All writers follow the same locking protocol

Readers
Readers should:

NOT lock (for performance)

Just read file normally

Because:

They will see either old or new version (never partial)

Edge case: file does not exist yet
Handled naturally:

Open with CREATE

Lock it

Then proceed

Alternative (not recommended)
Writing directly into the file while holding lock:

❌ risk of partial writes if crash

❌ readers could see corrupted JSON

Conclusion
Use:

lock(target) → write temp → atomic rename → unlock

This gives you:

Correct locking

Atomic updates

Crash safety

No extra lock files

It’s the standard, proven pattern for this kind of system.


Reader without lock could see empty file.

Yes — that’s the subtle flaw in the previous approach.

If you create + lock the final file first, a reader can observe:

empty file

partially initialized file (before rename)

Correct pattern: never expose incomplete file
You need to ensure:

The final file path only ever points to a fully written file

Revised approach
Case 1: File already exists (update)
This is safe:

Lock existing file

Write to temp

Atomic rename over existing file

Readers will see:

old file OR new file

never empty

Case 2: File does NOT exist (first creation)
This is where the issue happens.

Fix: lock a separate lock target
You cannot safely use the final file itself as the lock for first creation.

Solution options
Option A (recommended): separate lock file
sha/.jpg|pipe-id.lock
Flow:
Lock .lock file

Check if .jpg|pipe-id exists

If not:

write .tmp

atomic rename → final file

Release lock

Readers:

Only read .jpg|pipe-id

Never see partial/empty

✔ Clean
✔ Safe
✔ No race

Option B: lock parent directory (advanced, less portable)
Lock sha/ directory

Then create file via atomic rename

Problems:

Not portable in Java

Harder to reason about

Over-locks unrelated pipelines

→ Not recommended

Option C: tolerate empty + retry (not ideal)
Reader:

If file is empty → retry

Problems:

Adds complexity everywhere

Still racy

Violates your “clean deterministic FS” goal

→ Avoid

Key insight
You can only safely use a file as its own lock if it already exists

For creation:

You need an external lock point

Best practical design
Metadata file:

sha/.jpg|pipe-id
Lock file:

sha/.jpg|pipe-id.lock
Temp file:

sha/.jpg|pipe-id.tmp
Conclusion
Your concern is correct

Using the same file for lock + creation introduces a race

The clean solution is:

Use a dedicated .lock file for pipeline creation/update

This keeps:

readers simple (no locks, no retries)

writers safe (atomic + exclusive)

system behavior deterministic

This is one of those cases where one extra file is worth the correctness.


Was thinking: if empty treat equal to not existing.

Yes — that can work if you treat an empty file as “not-yet-materialized,” but there are some nuances:

Pattern
Writer checks the file:

if (!file.exists() || file.length() == 0) {
    // Treat as unmaterialized
}
Acquire lock on the file (or a separate lock if first creation)

Write content to temp → atomic rename → overwrite final file

Release lock

Pros
Eliminates need for a separate lock file in some cases

Readers never fail, just see “not yet materialized” instead of crashing on empty JSON

Compatible with lazy materialization

Cons / caveats
Race conditions

Two writers could see empty file simultaneously

Without a lock, both try to populate it

One wins with rename → okay, the other overwrites → still consistent if content is deterministic (e.g., canonical JSON pipeline)

Readers may see empty file

Need readers to tolerate empty → treat as “resource pending”

Locking still recommended

Even with empty-file trick, locking prevents unnecessary duplicate writes and guarantees atomicity

Without lock, you rely on “last write wins”

Recommended variant
Use empty file as “not yet written” marker

Always lock for writes (even if file exists or is empty)

Use temp + atomic rename to populate content

This way:

Readers see either:

valid JSON → ready

empty / absent → not materialized

Writers safely populate atomically

✅ Summary

Empty = unmaterialized is fine as a logical marker

But locking + atomic rename is still needed for correctness and to prevent duplicate work

Readers must tolerate empty file as “pending”

This gives you a lock-free read path, minimal extra files, and atomic writes.


Or pipeline creation is done holding lock on data, while pipeline materialization locks on pipeline file?  Then pipeline file can be written in tmp and atomically moved into place.

Exactly — that is a clean separation and fits well with your previous locking model.

Two-phase locking model
1. Pipeline creation (registering the pipeline)
Lock target: sha/data

Purpose:

Prevent concurrent creation of multiple pipelines pointing to the same source

Ensure that the forward/backlinks from this pipeline to data can be updated safely

Steps:

Lock sha/data

Determine pipeline ID / check if it exists

Prepare pipeline metadata (JSON) in a temporary file

2. Pipeline materialization (processing the pipeline)
Lock target: pipeline file (sha/.jpg|pipe-id)

Purpose:

Prevent multiple nodes from simultaneously materializing the same pipeline

Ensure atomic update of derived symlinks and metadata

Steps:

Lock sha/.jpg|pipe-id

Generate or reuse derived SHA

Write JSON to .tmp

Atomic rename → sha/.jpg|pipe-id

Update symlinks/backlinks

Release lock

Advantages
Separation of concerns

sha/data locks creation / registration

sha/.type|pipe-id locks materialization / updates

Atomic writes

Temp file + rename ensures pipeline metadata is never partially visible

No extra lock files needed

Locks target the existing file (or data file for creation)

Readers remain lock-free

Can read pipeline file at any time

Either empty/not-present → not yet materialized

Or fully written JSON → ready

Extensible to multiple pipelines

Each pipeline has its own lock file automatically (the metadata file itself)

No global lock needed

Implementation note
For first creation, the pipeline file may not exist → temp + atomic rename solves this

If multiple writers race on creation:

Locking sha/data ensures only one writer proceeds to create the pipeline file

Materialization is safe even after creation because each pipeline file is locked independently

✅ Conclusion
This approach is clean and safe:

Lock sha/data for registration/creation

Lock sha/.type|pipe-id for materialization

Write temp + rename for atomicity

Readers never block, just treat missing/empty as “pending”

It fits perfectly into your symlink + per-SHA directory design and avoids races or partial writes.


For pipe that builds on data directly, untyped input:

Yes — your intuition is correct: the filesystem layout can be slightly different from the external URL.

Keeps URLs clean and predictable

Supports arbitrary pipeline IDs

FS                     URL

Data itself (sha/data) is never exposed

Locks and temp writes remain entirely FS-local

This cleanly separates:

Proposal

  jpg|pipe-id

  data|pipe-id

Untyped pipelines: /sha/pipe-id

Benefits

Future-proof: allows nested pipeline IDs if needed, e.g., step1-step2

Backwards-compatible: FS layout unchanged, just mapping layer changes

Implementation
When generating a URL from FS:

url = fsName.replace("|", "/");
When resolving URL to FS:

fsName = urlPath.replace("/", "|");
Pipeline ID validation:

No /, no null bytes

Any other restrictions as needed for URL-safety

Convert to / in URLs for readability and hierarchy

Works for typed (jpg) and untyped (data) pipelines

Keeps FS and locking logic unchanged

This gives a clean separation between FS-safe names and user-friendly URLs.

Exactly — that’s a very clean and safe approach.

Locking summary
1. Lock on data for creation/registration

This ensures safe updates to forward/backlinks at the point of registration

Benefits:

2. Per-pipeline locking
Lock on pipeline metadata file (data|pipe-id or jpg|pipe-id) during materialization

Protects pipeline creation/updating, forward symlinks, backlinks

Avoids race conditions when multiple nodes/processes attempt to materialize the same pipeline simultaneously

Fine-grained concurrency control

This allows lock-free read paths while writers maintain atomicity

Why this combination works well
Single lock for SHA registration (data)

Per-pipeline lock for materialization

Allows multiple pipelines for the same SHA to be materialized concurrently without conflicts

Avoids deadlocks

FSCK or cleanup can scan pipelines without holding global locks

Can lock each pipeline individually during verification or deletion

Future-proof

Adding new types, pipelines, or FSCK operations can follow the same locking conventions

Per-pipeline lock remains necessary for atomic materialization

Combined, this gives:

Primitives:  
1) store (raw data)  
2) add type (verifies, generate meta, creates type file)  
3) add pipeline  
4) materialize pipeline  

All steps could be idempotent.  During add pipeline, if already exists, load pipeline and success if equals pipeline being added.

Anything else required for first release?

For a first release of this content-addressable CDN with pipeline support, your four primitives cover the core functionality. A few additional considerations or helper operations will make it robust and maintainable, but nothing fundamental is missing.

Core primitives
store(raw data)

Saves raw bytes to sha/data

Should be idempotent: if sha/data already exists, verify content matches

add type

Registers a type for the SHA (sha/jpg)

Generates metadata (width/height/etc.)

Idempotent: if .type exists, verify metadata or merge if appropriate

add pipeline

Creates a pipeline metadata file (sha/data|pipe-id or sha/jpg|pipe-id)

Idempotent: if pipeline exists, load and compare; return success if identical

materialize pipeline

Lock pipeline file, generate derived resource, update symlinks/backlinks

Atomic temp → rename write ensures safe visibility

Idempotent: repeated materialization produces same target SHA

Recommended helper / supporting operations for first release
These are not strictly new primitives, but improve reliability and debugging:

lookup / URL resolution

Map /sha|pipe-id or /sha.jpg|pipe-id → FS path

Determine if materialized, and if so, return final SHA and type

read metadata

Load type file (sha/jpg) or pipeline file

Provide fast access to pipeline steps, width/height, etc.

simple consistency check (light fsck)

Validate pipeline files point to valid SHA/type

Optional for first release, but useful for detecting broken symlinks

delete / garbage collection (optional)

Not required for first release

Can be postponed until you have multi-pipeline and cleanup needs

Idempotency rules
store: same raw content → no-op

add type: same type & metadata → no-op

add pipeline: same pipeline JSON → no-op

materialize pipeline: already materialized → no-op

This ensures safe retries, concurrent updates, and simplifies the API.

Conclusion
For first release, you have everything required with these four primitives. The only optional helpers would be:

URL resolution

Metadata reading

Basic consistency check

All others (garbage collection, global FSCK, multi-input pipelines) can be deferred to later releases.

This keeps the system minimal, deterministic, and robust while allowing future extensibility.


Idempotent pipeline materialization: recover from existing partial state?  The two symlinks forwards and backlinks must exist and match.  Create forward or backlink first?  Handle either one missing and auto-recover?

Yes — making pipeline materialization idempotent requires carefully handling partial state, since there are two kinds of symlinks per materialized pipeline:

Forward symlink: pipeline → materialized sha/.type

Backlink symlink: materialized sha/.type → pipeline

Recommended approach
1. Lock the pipeline file
Lock on sha/.type|pipe-id (or data|pipe-id for untyped pipelines)

Ensures only one materialization runs at a time

2. Compute or retrieve the target SHA
Check if the pipeline has already produced a materialized SHA

If yes, use it; if no, generate as usual

3. Reconcile symlinks
For idempotency, treat each symlink independently:

Forward symlink:

Check if sha/.type|pipe-id → target exists and points to correct materialized SHA

If missing or wrong → create/update symlink

Backlink symlink:

Check if materialized-sha/.type←sha|pipe-id exists and points back to pipeline

If missing or wrong → create/update symlink

Order doesn’t matter, as long as both are corrected atomically under the pipeline lock.

4. Recovery from partial state
If either symlink is missing or incorrect, idempotent materialization will recreate it

This automatically fixes cases like:

Crash during symlink creation

Manual deletion of one symlink

Optional: verify that both symlinks point to each other consistently before finishing

5. Write/update pipeline metadata
Atomic write: temp → rename

Ensures metadata always matches actual symlinks

6. Summary flow
lock(pipeline file)
compute target SHA if needed
for each symlink (forward, backlink):
    if missing or incorrect:
        create/update symlink
atomic write pipeline metadata
unlock
Benefits
Safe to retry: repeated materialization will restore partial state

Prevents broken references or inconsistencies

Works even if a crash occurred after creating one symlink but before the other

Simple: no need for separate transaction system

Conclusion
Forward or backlink can be created first — doesn’t matter as long as both exist at the end

Materialization should always verify and repair both symlinks

Combined with pipeline file locking and atomic metadata write, this guarantees idempotent, crash-safe pipeline materialization

This gives you robust recovery from partial state without requiring global locks or extra coordination.

Perhaps sha upload needs to provide first type, no purely untyped data?  If allowing purely untyped data, would need a way to associate pinned with the data file itself, not just pinning by type.

If last type is removed, the data itself would be removed immediately, unless referenced by untyped pipeline.  Basically, pipeline definitions are always considered pinned, too, and types may optionally be pinned such as is upload file.

Exactly — that is a clean invariant for first release:

Pinning and removal rules
Pipeline definitions are always pinned

Any sha/data|pipe-id or sha/.type|pipe-id prevents deletion of the target SHA/data it references

This ensures that active pipelines cannot break

Types may optionally be pinned

Upload files (e.g., first type) can be marked pinned in metadata

If pinned, the corresponding sha/.type (and underlying sha/data) is protected from cleanup

Data removal logic

When a type is deleted:

Check if any pipelines reference it → if yes, do not remove data

If no pipelines reference it and no pinned types remain, remove sha/data immediately

Ensures orphaned data never lingers, but active pipelines are safe

Untyped pipelines

Any untyped pipeline referencing sha/data automatically pins that data

Cleanup cannot remove sha/data while untyped pipelines exist

Summary invariant
- Any existing pipeline definition (typed or untyped) pins its source SHA
- Types may optionally be pinned
- Data is removed only if:
    * No pipeline references it
    * No pinned types exist
This guarantees:

Safety: no active pipelines break

Deterministic cleanup: data disappears when no longer needed

Simplicity: pinning logic is centralized in pipeline definitions and optional type flags

This design aligns perfectly with your first-release goals: minimal complexity, idempotent operations, and safe cleanup without global locks.

Could a pipeline have multiple outputs, such as splitting an image into RGB channels?

Yes — a pipeline could have multiple outputs, but it adds complexity to the filesystem and symlink model. For your current design, it’s useful to think of it as an extension of the single-output model.

Conceptual model
Single-output pipeline (current design)

Pipeline file (sha/.type|pipe-id) points to one materialized SHA/.type

Forward/backlink symlinks are one-to-one

Multi-output pipeline (future extension)

A pipeline produces N derived SHAs

Each output could have its own type (e.g., R, G, B)

Forward/backlink symlinks must be one per output

Filesystem considerations
Current layout assumes one symlink per pipeline → materialized SHA

sha/
  jpg|pipe-id/            ← pipeline metadata directory
    R                     ← derived SHA for R channel
    G                     ← derived SHA for G channel
    B                     ← derived SHA for B channel
    R←sha.jpg|pipe-id     ← backlink symlink
    G←sha.jpg|pipe-id
    B←sha.jpg|pipe-id
Or a naming convention in the pipeline file JSON:

Each output has its SHA and type

Symlinks point to the individual derived SHAs

Locking / materialization
Materialize pipeline: lock pipeline file or directory

For each output:

Compute derived SHA

Create backlink symlink (derived → pipeline)

Recovery from partial state: verify all outputs individually

/sha.jpg|pipe-id/R

/sha.jpg|pipe-id/B

Keeps external API clean and consistent with single-output pipelines

Recommendation for first release
Keep single-output pipelines only

Requires FS changes, symlink handling, and URL mapping

Materialization logic becomes slightly more complex

Idempotent recovery must handle all outputs

✅ Summary

Multi-output pipelines are feasible, but require:

Pipeline directory or explicit output naming

Multiple forward/backlink symlinks

Updated URL mapping for individual outputs

For first release, single-output pipelines keep the design simple and idempotent

Multi-output can be added later as a logical extension of the same model

Named outputs make URLs and API calls intuitive

Forward/backlink symlinks still support cleanup and idempotent recovery

If desired, I can sketch a full FS + URL + symlink scheme for named outputs pipelines, consistent with your existing design and idempotent materialization model. This would cover typed/untype pipelines, multiple outputs, and future cleanup.


But then within single pipeline definition, you get possible pipeline DAG: split RGB, invert brightness of red channel, recombine into single output.

Exactly — once you allow named outputs within a pipeline, the natural next step is a mini DAG of transformations inside a single pipeline definition.

Implications for pipeline design
1. Pipeline DAG
Each node is a transformation

Nodes can consume:

Original SHA (data or typed)

Outputs of previous nodes in the same pipeline (named outputs)

The final outputs of the pipeline are any subset of the nodes’ outputs

Example DAG:

          original.jpg
             |
          splitRGB
       /     |     \
      R      G      B
      |              \
 invertRed          recombine
      \              /
       \___________/
            finalOutput
Here, R is inverted, G and B unchanged, and all three are recombined into a single final output

2. Filesystem representation
Pipeline metadata file becomes a JSON DAG, e.g.:

{
  "nodes": {
    "splitRGB": {
      "type": "splitRGB",
      "inputs": ["data"],
      "outputs": ["R","G","B"]
    },
    "invertRed": {
      "type": "invert",
      "inputs": ["R"],
      "outputs": ["R_inverted"]
    },
    "recombine": {
      "type": "recombineRGB",
      "inputs": ["R_inverted","G","B"],
      "outputs": ["final"]
    }
  },
  "finalOutputs": ["final"]
}
Materialized outputs map to SHAs:

sha/
  jpg|pipe-id/
    R_inverted
    G
    B
    final
Forward/backlink symlinks can still be maintained per materialized output

3. Materialization logic
Lock pipeline file

Traverse DAG in topological order

For each node output:

Check if SHA exists

Generate output if missing

Update forward/backlink symlinks

Unlock

Idempotent: missing outputs or broken links get automatically restored

DAG traversal ensures dependencies are respected

4. Advantages
Arbitrary complex transformations without changing FS or URL schema

Named outputs make DAG nodes addressable and allow partial reuse

Compatible with current single-output model: a single-node pipeline is just a trivial DAG

Easy extension to multi-output pipelines, intermediate caching, and future GC

If you want, I can draw a full example FS + URL + DAG mapping showing split RGB → transform → recombine with forward/backlink symlinks for all outputs. This would make the design concrete and ready for implementation.


But then you could get mixture of sha resources and internal pipeline intermediates.

Exactly — once you allow internal DAG nodes to have outputs, you have a mixture of:

External (materialized) SHA resources

Usually produced as the final outputs of a pipeline

Referenced in URLs

Backlinks and forward links always point to these

Internal (intermediate) pipeline outputs

Only exist as part of a pipeline DAG

May or may not be materialized on disk, depending on pipeline execution

Usually not directly exposed in the URL

Could be referenced by downstream nodes in the same pipeline

Implications
1. Filesystem layout
Two classes of files under sha|pipe-id/:

sha/jpg|pipe-id/
  final                 ← materialized, external
  R_inverted            ← intermediate
  G                     ← intermediate
  B                     ← intermediate
Symlinks for intermediates can still exist for DAG integrity, but URLs may only expose the final outputs

Forward/backlink model still applies: any output (internal or final) points to its source SHA

2. URL exposure
Only the final outputs appear externally:
/sha.jpg|pipe-id/final

Intermediate outputs exist on disk for materialization dependencies and possibly caching, but not exposed in public URLs

3. Materialization logic
Traverse DAG topologically

For each node:

Determine if output SHA exists

Materialize output if missing

Update symlinks (forward/backlinks)

Idempotency: any partially materialized DAG is safe to re-run — intermediates will be restored automatically

4. Garbage collection / cleanup
Intermediates can be treated as pinned while pipeline exists

Final outputs may be optionally pinned by type or user request

Once pipeline is deleted or replaced, intermediate outputs can be safely removed

5. Design takeaway
Mixing SHA resources and intermediates is natural in DAG pipelines

The pipeline metadata file must track which outputs are final vs intermediate

FS + symlink layout can remain the same; only metadata differentiates intermediates from final outputs

Multi input and multi output/intermediate is for future idea. Not needed now.  Just thinking in terms of defining a "universal, deferred transformating data store"

First-release simplification

Multi inputs could also have named inputs.  Pipeline definition file stored with first referenced sha input, additional referenced sha inputs would have back symlinks to track which other pipelines stored in other sha reference them.

Exactly — if you extend to multi-input pipelines, named inputs plus backlink tracking lets you preserve dependency relationships without changing the core FS model.

Conceptual model for multi-input pipelines
1. Named inputs
Each input SHA can be given a logical name in the pipeline definition, e.g.:

{
  "inputs": {
    "main": "shaA",
    "mask": "shaB"
  },
  "transformations": ["applyMask"],
  "outputs": ["final"]
}
This lets the pipeline reference multiple sources in a clear, deterministic way

2. Storing pipeline metadata
The pipeline definition file is stored under the first referenced SHA (e.g., shaA|pipe-id)

Advantages:

Single point of materialization and locking

Forward/backlink symlinks naturally attach to the first input

3. Backlinks for secondary inputs
Any additional SHA inputs (e.g., shaB) get backlinks to the pipeline

This is similar to how forward/backlinks work for single-input pipelines

Ensures that the GC or cleanup logic knows that shaB is still in use by a pipeline

Example layout:

shaA/
  jpg|pipe-id                  ← pipeline metadata file (stored on first input)
  final                         ← materialized output of pipeline
  final←shaA.jpg|pipe-id        ← backlink to pipeline
shaB/
  shaB←shaA.jpg|pipe-id         ← backlink from secondary input
Cleanup can safely delete shaB only if no pipelines reference it

4. Materialization
Lock pipeline file (stored with first SHA input)

Ensure all input SHAs exist

Generate output SHA

Create/update forward/backlink symlinks for all outputs

For secondary inputs: create backlinks pointing to pipeline

Idempotent: re-running the materialization will fix missing symlinks or partially materialized outputs

5. Advantages
Allows multi-input pipelines without changing URL schema

Tracks all dependencies for GC and FSCK

Keeps locking and symlink model consistent with single-input pipelines

Easily extended to multi-output pipelines later

Two SHA inputs

Named inputs

Forward/backlink symlinks for outputs

How GC can safely handle cleanup

This makes the multi-input extension fully concrete while staying consistent with your current deferred-transform design.


JSON for DAG feels unnatural, as does XML

Yes — for a transformation DAG, JSON or XML can feel awkward because you’re trying to encode graph structure in a hierarchical serialization. They’re fine for simple linear pipelines, but DAGs are naturally better expressed as graph-native formats.

Alternatives to JSON/XML for pipeline DAGs
1. YAML
Slightly more human-readable than JSON

Can represent maps, sequences, and references

Still hierarchical, so DAG edges need careful encoding

Example:

nodes:
  splitRGB:
    type: splitRGB
    inputs: [data]
    outputs: [R, G, B]
  invertRed:
    type: invert
    inputs: [R]
    outputs: [R_inverted]
  recombine:
    type: recombineRGB
    inputs: [R_inverted, G, B]
    outputs: [final]
finalOutputs: [final]
Simple for linear pipelines, but edges in more complex DAGs may become cumbersome

2. Graph serialization (DOT, GraphML, GEXF, etc.)
DOT (Graphviz) is minimal and expresses DAGs naturally:

digraph pipeline {
  data -> splitRGB
  splitRGB -> R
  splitRGB -> G
  splitRGB -> B
  R -> invertRed
  invertRed -> R_inverted
  R_inverted -> recombine
  G -> recombine
  B -> recombine
  recombine -> final
}
Explicitly encodes nodes and edges

Human-readable and easily visualizable

Pros: natural for DAGs, can include node metadata as labels

Cons: not a standard for machine serialization in Java

3. Binary DAG formats
Examples: Protocol Buffers, CBOR, MessagePack

Can store node IDs, inputs, outputs, and metadata efficiently

Good for high-performance or on-disk pipelines

Less human-readable

4. Custom key-value file
Could store node list and adjacency map directly in JSON-like syntax but with a DAG-centric structure:

nodes:
  - id: splitRGB
    type: splitRGB
    inputs: [data]
    outputs: [R, G, B]
edges:
  - from: splitRGB
    to: R
  - from: splitRGB
    to: G
  - from: splitRGB
    to: B
  - from: R
    to: invertRed
...
Still JSON, but separates edges from nodes, making DAG structure more explicit

Recommendation for first release
Linear pipelines only: stick with JSON for simplicity

Future DAG support: consider a graph-native format, e.g., DOT-like adjacency list or graph-specific JSON schema:

{
  "nodes": {...},
  "edges": [{"from":"splitRGB","to":"R"}, ...]
}
Allows you to support named outputs, multi-inputs, and DAG traversal without changing the core FS and symlink model

If you want, I can sketch a compact DAG JSON format that feels natural, explicitly separates nodes and edges, and is ready for both single-output pipelines and future DAG pipelines. This can be your universal pipeline serialization format.


If XML, is there a way to use entity/reference

Yes — XML entities and references can be used to represent shared nodes or reusable parts in a DAG, but there are caveats.

Using XML Entities for DAGs
1. Internal Entities
You can define reusable pieces of XML inside a DTD section:

<!DOCTYPE pipeline [
  <!ENTITY splitRGB "<node id='splitRGB'><type>splitRGB</type><inputs><input>data</input></inputs><outputs><output>R</output><output>G</output><output>B</output></outputs></node>">
  <!ENTITY invertRed "<node id='invertRed'><type>invert</type><inputs><input>R</input></inputs><outputs><output>R_inverted</output></outputs></node>">
]>
<pipeline>
  &splitRGB;
  &invertRed;
  <node id="recombine">
    <type>recombineRGB</type>
    <inputs>
      <input>R_inverted</input>
      <input>G</input>
      <input>B</input>
    </inputs>
    <outputs>
      <output>final</output>
    </outputs>
  </node>
</pipeline>
&splitRGB; and &invertRed; are entity references

Avoids repeating identical nodes if used in multiple pipelines or multiple places in the DAG

2. External Entities
You can reference nodes stored in separate files:

<!DOCTYPE pipeline [
  <!ENTITY splitRGB SYSTEM "splitRGB.xml">
]>
<pipeline>
  &splitRGB;
</pipeline>
Useful for reusable transformations across pipelines

Each file can define a node or subgraph

3. Limitations / Considerations
Parsing complexity

Not all XML parsers resolve external entities automatically

Can complicate streaming or on-demand loading

Cyclic references

XML entities can’t directly encode cycles in DAGs

DAG cycles would need to be represented with IDs and references inside nodes, not with XML entities

Debugging

Entities make XML less readable at first glance

For human editing, a flat node list + explicit id/ref attributes is usually clearer

Security

Be careful with external entities (XXE) if input comes from untrusted sources

Recommended usage
Internal entities: for repeated subgraphs within the same pipeline file

External entities: for shared reusable transformations across pipelines

References inside XML nodes: still needed for DAG edges and multi-input pipelines

If you want, I can draft an example XML DAG using internal and external entities showing:

Single-input, single-output pipeline

Named outputs

Reusable transformation nodes

Backlinkable intermediate nodes

This would illustrate a full XML-based DAG serialization that could evolve into multi-input/multi-output pipelines.

Key design points
Linear parsing

Even though it represents a DAG, you can parse stages sequentially

Each stage declares its inputs and outputs clearly

Named inputs/outputs

Materialization & FS mapping

For each stage output, create SHA in FS if persisted or if referenced by another stage

Forward/backlink symlinks still apply, even for intermediates

Idempotency

Re-running pipeline materialization ensures missing outputs and symlinks are restored

Partial materialization is recoverable

Compatibility with first release

Single-input, single-output pipelines are just a single stage

Named outputs and DAG logic can be added incrementally

Advantages
Explicit DAG structure without awkward XML entities

Fully compatible with deferred materialization

Makes multi-input / multi-output pipelines feasible later

Supports intermediate caching for performance

Easy to reason about dependencies and cleanup
