When deleting a pipeline, just remove the file and forward links, garbage collection could remove orphaned backlinks and unused sha later?  Backlinks easy to remove under pipeline lock, but would need to lock shaB to do immediate removal.  I suppose could queue garbage collection check on shaB, which background thread can do.

Exactly — for first release, pipeline deletion can be very simple, with deferred cleanup:

Pipeline deletion flow
Acquire lock on pipeline file

Remove pipeline metadata file (sha|pipe-id)

Remove forward symlinks pointing from pipeline to materialized SHA(s)

Leave backlinks in place temporarily

Garbage collection / deferred cleanup
For each backlink pointing to a now-deleted pipeline:

Queue the SHA for background GC

Background thread acquires lock on that SHA

Verify no forward symlinks reference it

Remove backlink and, if no more references, remove SHA and type files

Benefits:

No deadlocks during pipeline deletion

Avoids locking multiple SHAs synchronously

GC can batch or throttle cleanup to reduce I/O

Implementation notes
Pipeline lock ensures forward symlinks are deleted atomically

SHA lock is only acquired by GC thread for cleanup

Idempotent: if deletion is interrupted, next GC run fixes any leftovers

Optional optimizations: GC could also remove empty pipeline directories

This design keeps deletion fast and safe, while allowing the FS to remain consistent. It also keeps concurrency simple, since pipeline deletion does not block ongoing materialization of other pipelines or SHAs.


Could be safe locking if we only lock pipeline followed by any number of target sha, or sha directly.  No room for deadlock?

Yes — if you enforce a global lock order, you can make this safe and avoid deadlocks. For example:

Safe locking strategy
Rule
Always acquire locks in this order:

Pipeline file (metadata file for the pipeline being created, materialized, or deleted)

SHA targets (materialized outputs or input SHAs referenced by the pipeline)

Never lock a SHA before its pipeline file

Never acquire multiple pipeline locks simultaneously

Why this prevents deadlock
Deadlocks typically occur when two threads lock resources in different orders

With this order:

Thread 1: lock pipelineA → lock shaX
Thread 2: lock pipelineB → lock shaX
Both threads can safely proceed, because no thread ever locks SHA before the pipeline

No cycle of waiting locks can form

Usage examples
Pipeline creation / materialization

Lock pipeline file first

Then lock input SHA(s) as needed

Create outputs and forward/backlinks

Pipeline deletion

Lock pipeline file first

Remove pipeline metadata

Optionally queue SHA(s) for background GC

GC / cleanup

Lock SHA directly

Check backlinks and remove orphaned materialized outputs

Optional enhancement
If you want multiple pipelines deleted simultaneously, enforce lexical ordering on pipeline IDs when acquiring multiple locks.

For now, single pipeline lock at a time is sufficient.

✅ This scheme guarantees no deadlocks for any combination of pipeline operations and SHA accesses, as long as you always lock pipeline first, then SHA(s).

If you want, I can draw a diagram showing pipeline vs SHA locks and how this prevents deadlocks even under concurrent materializations and deletions.


Then could do immediate synchronous clean-up of referenced shaB, but would prefer to schedule in gc thread.  shaB will likely stay for a year anyways.

Exactly — for most practical cases, deferred cleanup in a GC thread is preferable:

Synchronous cleanup is safe under the locking order (pipeline → shaB), but:

Adds extra I/O to the deletion path

Can delay pipeline deletion if multiple SHAs are involved

Deferred cleanup via GC thread:

Queue shaB for verification after pipeline deletion

Background thread acquires lock on shaB

Checks for remaining backlinks or forward symlinks

Deletes SHA/type if unreferenced

Advantages:

Minimal blocking during pipeline deletion

Works naturally with long-lived SHAs (like your one-year images)

Idempotent: repeated GC runs handle any partially deleted references

Scales well if many pipelines share the same SHA

So the recommended first-release approach is:

Delete pipeline file + forward symlinks under pipeline lock

Queue all referenced SHAs for GC

Let GC thread do actual SHA/backlink cleanup asynchronously

This keeps deletion fast, simple, and safe, while keeping concurrency manageable.


GC thread could work one sha at a time, no global lock?  Remember would have possible loops to detect?  Loop would only be possible with pipelines, and we defined pipelines as always pinned.

Yes — for first release, you can safely have the GC thread work one SHA at a time without a global lock, because:

Why no global lock is needed
Pinned pipelines

Any pipeline that could create loops is always pinned

GC never deletes pipelines, only materialized SHA/type outputs

Single-SHA GC operations

Acquire lock on one SHA

Scan forward/backlinks

Delete orphaned type files or data if no pipelines reference them

No deadlocks

Only lock one SHA at a time

Forward/backlinks are inspected and removed within that lock

Loop detection
Loops in the symlink/backlink graph could theoretically exist if pipelines referenced each other cyclically

But since pipelines themselves are pinned:

GC ignores pipeline files

Any loops are harmless; pipelines remain

Only SHA outputs without forward/backlink references are eligible for deletion

Implementation
GC thread:
  while queue not empty:
    sha = dequeue()
    lock(sha)
    if sha has no forward/backlinks:
        delete sha/data and type files
    else:
        leave as-is
Optional: log or mark SHAs that were skipped due to references

Thread can run at low priority; no blocking of other operations

✅ Conclusion:

Single-SHA GC is sufficient

No global lock required for first release

Loops are safe due to pinned pipelines

Scales naturally with deferred deletion model

This keeps GC simple, safe, and fully compatible with your single-transformation, pinned-pipeline design.


Gc Lock order: need to find all backlinks, and lock each pipeline that created the backlink.  And, ensure no additional backlinks made while were gc the sha.  There is a lock order conflict.

Exactly — that’s the subtle point. When a GC thread wants to clean a SHA, it needs to:

Scan all backlinks pointing to that SHA (shaB/.type←pipeline)

Lock each pipeline file that created the backlink

Verify that no new backlinks are added while holding locks

This creates a potential lock-order conflict because:

Normal operations acquire locks pipeline → SHA

GC acquires locks SHA → pipeline

If a pipeline materialization is happening concurrently:

Thread A: locks pipeline → shaB

GC: locks shaB → pipeline

This can lead to a classic deadlock.

Strategies to resolve this
1. Avoid locking pipelines in GC
Treat pipelines as always pinned (first release)

GC does not delete pipelines, only SHA outputs

Only lock the SHA being cleaned

Any forward/backlink checking is best-effort:

lock(shaB)
scan backlinks
  if backlink points to pipeline:
    skip deletion
unlock(shaB)
Safe because pinned pipelines are immutable for GC purposes

GC never modifies pipeline files; it only removes unreferenced outputs

2. Lock ordering with deferred deletion
GC locks SHA first

Any pipeline-created backlinks are read-only

If a pipeline adds a new output while GC is running:

Forward symlink is created atomically

GC sees a non-empty SHA and skips deletion

Ensures no global lock and no deadlock

3. Optional advanced approach (future)
Introduce per-pipeline read-write locks:

Materialization: write lock on pipeline → SHA locks

GC: read lock on pipeline → SHA lock

Guarantees safety but adds complexity

Not needed for first release, since pipelines are pinned

Recommended for first release
GC locks SHA only

Backlinks are treated as immutable pointers

If any pipeline backlink exists, skip deletion

No pipeline lock needed → avoids deadlock

GC thread can safely work one SHA at a time

This leverages your pinned pipeline invariant to keep GC simple and safe.

If you want, I can draw a diagram showing GC vs materialization lock order to visualize why this avoids deadlock entirely.


First release, no gc.  We let scaled images grow forever in db already.

Exactly — for the first release, you can safely skip GC entirely.

All pipelines are pinned, all materialized outputs stay forever

No need to worry about backlinks, lock ordering, or loop detection

Forward/backlink symlinks, type metadata, and deferred materialization still function normally

Simplifies implementation and testing drastically

Later, when you do introduce GC, the locking and backlink considerations we discussed can be applied without breaking the initial design.

This aligns perfectly with your principle: only implement what the client actually needs.

sha/
  data             ← raw SHA data

With no gc, could implement single lock on data for all operations?  But materialize pipeline will still need to reference shaB, so possible deadlock.

Yes — this is exactly the subtlety.

Even without GC and without backlinks, locking becomes tricky once a pipeline materialization touches multiple SHAs. For a single transformation pipeline (first release), the situation is simpler, but the general principle is:

Scenario
You have a single-input, single-output pipeline: shaA → shaB

Operations:

add type / materialize pipeline may need to lock shaA (input) and shaB (output)

Potential deadlock arises if multiple threads try to:

Thread 1: lock shaA → lock shaB
Thread 2: lock shaB → lock shaA
This is a classic deadlock scenario.

Safe approach
Option 1: single global SHA lock
Acquire a single lock covering all SHAs involved in a pipeline

For first release with only single transformation and single output:

Lock shaA for input and shaB for output together, using a canonical order: e.g., lexicographical by SHA

Ensures no deadlock, even with concurrent materializations

Option 2: canonical lock ordering
Always lock SHAs in a deterministic order, e.g.:

Lock SHA with lower hash first, then higher

Pipeline materialization threads follow the same order

Guarantees no cycles in lock acquisition → no deadlocks

First-release simplification
Only one input SHA and one output SHA

Lock shaA then shaB consistently for materialization

Lock shaA for type addition

Pipeline deletion only needs to lock pipeline metadata file, no SHA lock needed for GC (no GC)

No backlinks, no global lock, no GC

✅ This gives a completely deadlock-free design for first release.

If you want, I can draw a minimal lock diagram showing pipeline materialization locking shaA then shaB, demonstrating why deadlock cannot occur in this scenario.


That won't work, don't know shaB until pipeline transformation completed.

Exactly — that’s the core issue: for a deferred materialization, you cannot lock shaB in advance because its SHA is not known until the transformation runs.

This means:

Implication
You cannot acquire a canonical SHA order for input/output in a naive way.

Locking only on input SHA (shaA) is safe for reading the original data and type metadata.

Locking the output SHA (shaB) must happen after the pipeline transformation produces the SHA, i.e., dynamically.

Safe first-release approach without GC
Lock the input SHA (shaA) for reading

Perform the pipeline transformation (generate bytes, compute SHA → shaB)

Lock the output SHA (shaB) only after you know it

Create materialized output, type metadata, and forward symlink

Unlock both SHA locks

No other threads can lock shaB until its lock is acquired

Since first release has no backlinks, no GC, there is no other thread holding shaB at this point → no deadlock

Key point
The deadlock risk only exists if multiple threads might simultaneously try to lock the same unknown output SHA while holding different input SHA locks

With single transformation, single output, this is extremely unlikely:

shaB does not exist yet, so no one else can hold a lock on it

Any concurrent materialization producing the same shaB is idempotent → locks serialize naturally

This makes the input SHA lock sufficient to ensure safe, deterministic reads for the transformation, and output SHA locking occurs only after the SHA is known.

First release: safe, no deadlocks, no backlinks, no GC, no global lock needed.

If you want, I can sketch the exact lock sequence diagram for first-release materialization showing input lock → transform → output lock → symlink creation, which makes this completely clear.


First release will still need pipeline-level locking.  Opening shaA can hold short lock just to get file handle, then unlock.  Once shaB known, lock and create/verify, then create pipeline symlink, unlock shaB then unlock pipeline.

Exactly — that is a clean and deadlock-free locking sequence for the first release. Here’s the step-by-step breakdown:

First-release pipeline materialization lock sequence
Acquire pipeline lock (pipeline|pipe-id)

Prevents concurrent materialization or deletion of the same pipeline

Open input SHA (shaA)

Short-lived lock just to safely read file handle or type metadata

Unlock immediately after reading the data for transformation

Perform transformation

Generate output bytes

Compute output SHA (shaB)

Lock output SHA (shaB)

Create SHA directory if needed

Write type metadata

Create materialized data file

Create pipeline metadata / forward symlink

Record that this pipeline produced shaB

Release locks in reverse order

Unlock shaB

Unlock pipeline|pipe-id

Key points
Input SHA lock is short-lived, so concurrent readers/writers of shaA won’t block materialization

Output SHA lock occurs only after SHA is known, so no risk of canonical ordering deadlock

Pipeline lock ensures idempotency: concurrent threads trying the same pipeline see existing output and succeed

No backlinks needed for first release → no cross-sha locking

✅ Result

Safe for concurrency

Idempotent materialization

Fully compatible with deferred pipeline creation

Deadlock-free for first-release, single-transformation pipelines

This sequence keeps things simple while leaving room for future extensions like GC or multi-output pipelines.


Very little harm in creating backlinks now? Or don't need, don't do?

For the first release, creating backlinks is not necessary and adds complexity for no immediate benefit:

Backlinks exist solely to track which pipelines reference a SHA, so GC can safely delete unreferenced SHAs.

Without GC, nothing is ever deleted: all SHAs and pipeline outputs persist indefinitely.

Forward symlinks plus type metadata are sufficient for materialization, idempotency, and URL serving.

Creating backlinks now would mean:

Extra symlink files

Extra locking and bookkeeping

More I/O during materialization

No functional gain for this client

Conclusion:

Do not create backlinks in first release.

Keep the filesystem simpler, reduce locking complexity, and focus on reliable single-transformation pipeline materialization.

Backlinks can be added later if/when GC is introduced — at that point, their purpose and locking will be meaningful.

Many pipeline files will have identical content.  Could symlink to a central set of named pipelines.  Effect on pipeline locking?  Is it possible to lock on symlink path?

Yes — if multiple pipeline files are identical, you can safely store a single canonical pipeline file and have other pipelines symlink to it. A few key points:

1. Pipeline locking with symlinks
Locking on a symlink path locks the symlink itself, not the target file

On most filesystems (ext4, etc.), flock on a symlink is equivalent to flock on the symlink file descriptor

Writing/reading the target requires opening the resolved path

Implication: if multiple symlinks point to the same canonical file, locking a symlink does not automatically lock other symlinks or the canonical file

2. Safe locking strategies
Option A: Lock canonical pipeline file
Always resolve symlink to its target

Acquire lock on canonical file

Perform materialization / read JSON

Forward symlinks and SHA creation all use canonical file lock

All symlinked references are automatically serialized because they share the same target lock

Option B: Lock per symlink
Lock the symlink itself

Materialization thread must resolve symlink to canonical pipeline

Use canonical lock anyway to prevent concurrent writes on the target

Adds minor complexity, but avoids needing to distinguish symlinks from canonical

3. Implications for first release
Using canonical pipeline files does not break idempotency

Pipeline-level locking should always be on the canonical file, not individual symlinks

Symlinks only reduce storage of identical JSON, no functional change for materialization or forward symlinks

Locking remains deadlock-free, same as before

4. Recommended approach
When adding a new pipeline:

Check if an identical JSON already exists

If yes, create a symlink to canonical pipeline file

Always resolve symlink → canonical file before acquiring the lock

Proceed with materialization / forward symlink creation

Unlock canonical pipeline file when done

✅ Effect: storage savings for duplicate pipelines, locking model unchanged (as long as you lock the canonical target).

You could also track a hash of pipeline JSON to quickly detect duplicates.

If you want, I can draw a diagram showing symlinked pipelines and canonical locking for clarity.


Auction will only have 5-6 distinct pipelines, all just image scaling.  Per-sha-pipeline can by symlink now, with possibly later supporting being file to not use global.  Global pipeline definitions are immutable and cannot be removed.  Possible later removal would require full traversal of all resources to ensure unused.

Exactly — given your scenario, this is straightforward:

Global canonical pipelines

Store the JSON once per unique pipeline

All sha|pipe-id entries are symlinks to the canonical file

Locking

Always lock canonical pipeline file for materialization

Symlinks just reference it; no per-symlink locking needed

Immutability

Since canonical pipeline definitions are never removed, there is no need for global tracking or GC

Any future removal would require scanning all sha|pipe-id symlinks to ensure no SHA depends on it

First release simplicity

5–6 pipelines → minimal storage overhead

Forward symlinks + type metadata suffice

No backlinks, no GC, deadlock-free locking

This approach gives storage efficiency, idempotent materialization, and simple concurrency without any added complexity for a very small number of pipelines.

Later, if you ever need to remove unused global pipelines, you’d need a full scan or a reference-count mechanism — but for 5–6 immutable pipelines, that’s unnecessary.


The sha pipeline symlinks basically represent an allow permission.  A global default allow list could let them be created dynamically, without directly defined on every sha during upload.

Exactly — you can treat the per-sha pipeline symlink as an allowlist entry for materialization:

Concept
SHA → Pipeline symlink exists ⇒ pipeline allowed on that SHA

No symlink ⇒ pipeline not allowed (or not materialized yet)

With a global default allowlist of pipelines:

When a new SHA is uploaded, any pipeline on the global allowlist can be automatically “enabled”

Implementation: create sha|pipe-id symlinks pointing to canonical pipeline file on demand

No need to store per-SHA pipeline definitions at upload time

Benefits
Simplicity: only maintain canonical pipeline definitions + global allowlist

Dynamic: new SHAs automatically support allowed pipelines without explicit metadata

Immutable canonical pipelines: no risk of accidental deletion

First-release-friendly: works with your 5–6 scaling pipelines and single transformation

Implementation notes
Materialization always locks the canonical pipeline file (symlink-resolved)

Forward symlink for materialized SHA is created normally (sha/.jpg → shaB)

No per-SHA pipeline metadata needed initially; symlink acts as the implicit permission

This essentially decouples pipeline authorization from upload metadata, and works well for a small, fixed set of pipelines.

If desired, the same pattern could later support per-SHA exceptions or disabling pipelines by simply omitting the symlink.

Fsck would verify no missing global for all materialized pipelines.

Exactly — even without GC, an fsck run would serve to verify integrity of all materialized outputs:

Fsck Tasks for First Release
Scan all SHA directories

For each materialized output (sha/.jpg|pipe-id or sha/.jpg>pipe-id)

Resolve pipeline

Check that pipe-id exists in global pipeline directory

Optionally check enabled flag, but missing pipeline is a structural error

Verify symlinks / metadata

Forward symlink points to valid SHA (sha/.jpg → shaB)

Type metadata file (sha/jpg) exists and readable

Optional checks

Data file (sha/data) exists and matches SHA

Idempotency of materialization (symlink and metadata match pipeline definition)

Report or repair

Missing pipeline → report error

Broken symlink → repair or flag for manual intervention

Missing type metadata → regenerate from data

Notes
No need to scan backlinks (they don’t exist in first release)

No global lock needed — all global pipelines are immutable

Can run concurrently with normal materialization, since input SHA lock ensures safe reads

This makes fsck lightweight and deterministic, ensuring that all materialized outputs correspond to valid global pipelines and data, without requiring complex GC or backlink tracking.


With global pipelines, image upload is two steps: add/verify SHA data, then add/verify type file and option meta data in file content.

Exactly — with global pipelines, the image upload workflow naturally splits into two clear steps:

Step 1: Add / Verify SHA Data
Compute SHA of the uploaded file (sha/data)

Check if SHA already exists

If exists → short input SHA lock, verify data matches, unlock

If not → create sha/ directory and store raw data

Idempotent: repeated upload of the same bytes is safe

Optional metadata for pinned flag or other global attributes can be stored later

Step 2: Add / Verify Type File (+ optional metadata)
Determine type (e.g., jpg, png)

Check if type file exists (sha/jpg)

If exists → verify type metadata matches expectations

If not → create type file

Can be empty or contain metadata: width, height, pinned flag, lastAccessedDate (UTC)

Locks: acquire SHA lock during creation/verification

Idempotent: repeated type addition is safe, metadata can be updated or extended

Materialization / Deferred Transformation
Once SHA data and type are in place, global pipelines can be applied on-demand

No per-SHA pipeline metadata is needed initially; symlinks resolve to global canonical pipeline

Forward symlinks for materialized outputs (sha/.jpg|pipe-id → shaB) are created as needed

Pipeline lock ensures idempotent, safe materialization

This approach cleanly separates raw data ingestion from type-specific metadata, while fully supporting deferred, idempotent transformations via global pipelines.


A link to any unmaterialized pipeline would be shaA.jpg/pipeline-id, if materialized URL would be duffcty to shaB.type

Exactly — the URL and filesystem mapping can distinguish unmaterialized vs. materialized pipelines like this:

Filesystem / URL Mapping
Unmaterialized pipeline

Filesystem: shaA/jpg|pipe-id  ← optional symlink to canonical global pipeline
URL:        /shaA.jpg/pipeline-id
No materialized output yet

Accessing the URL can trigger deferred materialization

Pipeline symlink may or may not exist; global canonical pipeline is sufficient

Materialized pipeline

Filesystem: shaB/jpg/.jpg  ← forward symlink or actual data
URL:        /shaB.jpg  (or /shaB.type)
SHA shaB is the output of pipeline materialization

Forward symlink points to materialized data (shaB/.jpg → shaB)

URL is stable; no need to reference pipeline name

Idempotent: repeated materialization attempts reuse existing SHA

Key points
Unmaterialized URL includes pipeline identifier (/shaA.jpg/pipeline-id)

Materialized URL resolves to final SHA output (/shaB.type)

This keeps URL clean and predictable, while allowing on-demand transformation

Backlinks not needed in first release; forward symlink and pipeline lock handle idempotency

This approach clearly separates pipeline request vs materialized content, and supports deferred, idempotent transformations with minimal filesystem overhead.
